mined effort, I have pleasure in informing you that they were defeated
by the hardened steel plate which protects the lock from their drills. I

cares to call and see it. Yours faithfully, (Signed) J. STEDMAN,

THE BED PAGE.
AN INTERKOPTED ARTICLE
TO-DAY we are to talk high pnnosopny.
What is philosophy ? Call it the
science of wisdom. What wisdom ?
Human wisdom ? Who is human ?
With the grand refusal of grammar—Me,
and You. Five lines bring us to the middle
battle.
There is Me—what am I going to do about
You ? There is You —what are you going to
do about Me ? Two sides of one problem—a
problem old as humanity—the only pro-
blem. There are no others.
Let’s fight it out, with the irrelevant
Cosmos for arena. I have foothold there,
and You : we are creatures of that Cosmos ;
yet it does not not regard us, goes im-
partially about its business, permits free
play within our limitations, claims no part
in our controversy (so convenient of the
Cosmos !).
I, I assume, am I. You, I presume, are
You. You may be my mental I
may be your illusion of the senses. Tar-
water may be a divine principle, ichor in the
veins of Jove. Not knowing, can’t say.
Where was Moses when the light blew out ?
Where was the light when Berkeley blew
out 1
Whether I am,
Or whether I seem ,
What does it matter
If I ’to my dream ?
Poetry—and observed that it leaves You out:
one can’t get everything into a quatrain ex-
tempore. The point is : should n’t You be
omitted from Me altogether, unless by grace
and favour I use you as a cockshy ?
You being You, I notice You are legion :
there are unnecessary lots of You : You
hustle Me in the market-place. I am legion
too, but “the name of this legion is Me.”
I do my sums in my head with a single
Roman numeral. You repeat i i i i i inde-
finitely, and never get an answer. Have
You an answer ? If You have, can You tell
Me why I should help You to find it ?
I see that You profess to answer by segre-
gating your i i i i as I, I, I, I. This infringes
my prerogative, and is distasteful to Me.
Besides, it is not an answer —for Me. True :
from your point of view, I am You, and You
are I. But from my point of view, I am I,
sole and indivisible, and You continue iiiii
to weariness. Why do You ? Things would
be so much simpler if You were eliminated.
I admit the retort, and hasten to say I
have no intention of eliminating You. In
fact., I cannot—as a Queensland poet ob-
served to a Woman of the Night. Like him,
I find it is no use crying “Get hence ! ” if
You won’t go. Since, then, You are there,
and your odours offend me, am I to spend
time and money with disinfectants, or
quickly to pass with nose averted ? Am I
to reckon with You, or without You ?
O Cosmos ! Still in the middle battle, still
asking questions with treacherous words !
But there are answers, a whole worldful.
Hedonism’s, for example —Ego Sol, with a
select troop of moons of both sexes, accom-
panying and adoring. (Compositor will
please exhibit graphically, indicating moons
of both sexes.)
i 11 i i 11 i 1111 I I I I
Anarchism’s :
/, I, I, /, I, I, I, I, I. I,
Socialism’s :
Anti-Socialism’s ? '(Compositor will kindly
construct “ a necklace of negatives.”)
o o
( 0 G R 0 }
°
0 0
T
0
And so on. But, as a distinguished writer
has remarked, there are some answers that
are no responsesj and I rather think these
answers of yours take my consent for
granted. 1 rather suspect that my answer,
if I have one, will take your consent for
granted. The moment I overstep Me, I am
on the foreign territory of You, who own
other allegiance, speak another lauguage.
Nor is there refuge in a parallel of
identities. Beyond all agreements remains
the difference between Me and You. No
metaphysical arbitrator can justify a common
rule of conduct for You to
at which point Hubert Church came in. He
is on a health-furlough ; and, as he is another
man who thinks that books are the next-best
thing in the world, and can write a line like
j Break as all vows of Love that unabides,
he seemed better worth entertaining than high
philosophy. That will wait until tlieie is oppor-
tunity to resume Ibsen, who died recently after
striving hard—and fruitlessly—to find a moral
clue to the relationship between Me and You.
Moralist: —and such a dramatist ! Hasta ruanana !
Church says that the only two poets in the Mono-
wai library are Burns and Scott. These Scottish
shipowners are the D.
WANTED, A SOLOMON
H. MCO.
HALF-A-DOZEN words borrowed from Sir
Thomas Browne, and half-a-dozen added
plus one, when placed brickwise thus in
two lines and a bittock—
The silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that
made (on 26 April) a pedestal for another in a
universe of “ splendid offers.” Was desired “the
most poetical continuation, in not more than 12
lines ” —and continue :
H. McC. ; Mars ; Alf B. ; F.B. ; E.W.D. ; J.C.A. ; D. 8.;
E.V.K. ; W.H. ; Platman ; Llewellyn.
Whence appears that the offer was obscurely
phrased—for some send 12 lines in all, some 16.
“ ... 12 lines (ample to turn the theme and. close
on the keynote) ” proceeded the dark oracle ; and
certain illuminati-ae understood that the meaning
was four lines to come in, four to linger in, and
four Napoleonically to...decamp in : total 12, in-
cluding fragment given.
However, “what ’e meant isn’t hevidence” ;
and, being bound by the wording of the Act ac-
cording to the rule adopted (when convenient) by
the High Court, we point out that the word “con-
tinuation ” excludes the 13 words of theme, and
is governed by the clause “in not more than 12
lines.” Consequently friends who have sent in
more than 12 original lines have placed themselves
beyond the competitive pale. Fiat lex, ruat jus-
titia !
J*
8ut...0n re-examination, it appears that every
Jim, and every Gladys, is safe inside the paling.
Consider this deludhering piece (marked “Ex-
hibit H. McC.”):
Those look like 16 lines, hein ? But has the com-
petitor added more than 12 ? Observe (his first
plea) that four are a refrain. Now, passing the
point whether a competitor who is eventually dis-
qualified (the question at issue) ever enters into
competition at all, or is really disqualified in
se before he competes (though the disqualification
lie perdu, like a sore toe, until discovered by his
guilty limping): passing that point (like the wise
Judge who decides as little as he can help, to
give appeal the slightest fulcrum to overturn
him : passing that point (but reiterating it
oratorically, for fear this wild sentence will bolt
with us): If a postulant-competitor is asked for a
“continuation in not more than 12 lines,” and
composes not more than 12, and writes four of
them a second time, Does he in fact offer “not
more than 12,” or does he offer “ not more than
12 ” plus four ?
What do the books say ? “ The boobs are
silent, your Honour a piece of information
that his Honour receives always with peculiar
regret ; for it means that he will have to do some
independent thinking in order to create a prece-
dent that will stand the assault administered by
(in the phrase attributed, not fondly, toaN.S.W.
Supreme Court judge) “ Those d—d Welshmen !”
(There is only one Welshman, but our poor
human tendency is to multiply calamity.)
Priina facie, there are four extravagant lines;
but how deceitful are appearances ! What lines
were desiderated ? Original lines, clearly. But
these lines are not original : they are copied by
the postulant from his own writing (vide
stanza 1). Is he then to be disqualified for
fraud ? Which, in that case, is the original
stanza? He may have composed the last first:
wary poets do. Is he then in dilemma—guilty on
either the first count or the last ? But the law
rightly demands precise information. If you steal
my Webster’s-dictibnary-with-Australasian-sup-
plement, and are found with two copies, and I am
positive one is mine, but cannot swear which
one : there is no case for your conviction.
Not even if you were seen walking out of
my office with a copy—for it may have been your
own copy. (On analogous lines a poultrv-pur-
loiner-related-to-literature continues his dastardly
but consoling depredations. At each township he
buys two chickens and gets a receipt for money
paid. When you see him prowling, miss your
chicken, and find—what you know, but cannot
swear, is—lt in his pot: he flourishes the receipt.
Tableau ! Villainy triumphant ! Virtue flabber-
gasted !)
After humble and fervent prayer before a set of
Queensland’s Consolidated Statutes, the Court de-
cides—to hear the next plea. Deft, thereupon
urges that he has added to the theme only 10 lines
and two three-eighths of a line: total 10£ lines ;
whereas he was permitted to add 12.—Barton, J.,
thinks this is a very specious plea.—Deft,
replies that Law is a specious science.—Griffith,
C.J., points out that Deft, has forgotten to count
his second use of the theme, which makes his
apparent total of competitive lines 13jf.—Deft,
answers that those lines, being given originally,
cannot be regarded as part of his continuation. It
is for the continuation that the prize is offered,
and since he certainly would not be credited with
the theme in the award, it is unjust that it should
be cited against him.
After further argument, it appears that
Barton, a judge of known literary leanings, says
he favours Deft. Griffith says Deft, is clearly
disqualified. The new point is, What does O’Con-
nor say ?
10. s. fad', will be paid for O’Connor’s judgment,
with reasons; in not more than 200 words,
strictly counted, delivered before July 4. The
prize will be given for the most convincing utter-
ance, combining wit, weight, “the best traditions
of the English bench,” the discrimination needed
in applying those traditions to a young country,
and as many more things as can be crammed into
200 words.—Award in the previous competition is
deferred till the qualification or disqualification of
postulant-competitor H. McC. is decided by a
majority of judgments delivered before July 4.
The silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that Venus likes
When heralding- her singing swains.
The eager touch of hand on hand
Conveys a dumb sweet serenade
Between two hearts who understand
The reason why the world was made.
Who has not heard the thin white song
Of some fair virgin at her lute,
Aware of one amid the throng
Whose eyes could make her music mute ?
The silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that Venus likes
When heralding her sighing swains.
MEANTIME
MEANTIME we call end some miseries and
blow a peal of Fame. Alf. B., then, is
ordinary ; E.B. wants science with his
temperament; J.C.A. (whose courtesy
is appreciated) is too “tight” and intellectual:
since he’ll never write lyrics, the sonnet's his
mode; Llew. rhymes “strains"’ and “flame ;
E.V.K. is too merely sentimental. The fol-
lowing have been looked at twice: we shall look
at them a third time after July 4.
One thing striking is the paucity of rhymes
offered for strikes. Nobody thought of ‘‘ harmony
that bursts the dykes of grosser clay,” etc. No-
body volunteered a “ harmony that fills the Mikes
and Sandies with unwonted pains.” Nobody is
quite ingenious enough to please us.
♦
Francois Coppee, one of the versatile French-
men, has published another book of poetry. And
he has been forty years in the wilderness of
beauty, for his first of the same appeared in 1806,
In those forty years what wanderings! what
labours ! what modest triumphs, not unholy ! At
his 05th year he writes : “ May I be pardoned my
innocent' pride in cultivating in the evening of
life an art which has been continually my study,
and which has given me the best of all my joys.”
A tale of Skinner’s notorious Sydney book-
shop—where the second-hand (or third-hand, or
possibly fourth-hand, or [it is likely enough]
fortieth-hand) books come from. The genial pro-
prietor (everybody is genial nowadays, so there
isn’t any merit in it) formerly hung out the
antient London sign of his calling, Food fob
Worms. Which one day a petticoated mystery
seeing, and pausing to read again, and pondering,
and hesitating, and finally making up its mind
to enter, quoth it, it quoth (all in one
breathless burst of contidence) : “Oh I see you
have food for worms my little boy has been so bad
with them such a long time and I can’t get any-
thing to do him good and perhaps your food might
would you give me sixpenn’orth please.” But
the proprietor, alert to a situation, prompt to a
customer, most urbanely: “Madam, I’m very
sorry, but I sold the last of it yesterday.” “Oh
dear me what a pity when will you be having
some more.” “ Madam,’ that I cannot say; I
hear last year’s crop was a failure, and no more is
being imported.” “Oh thank you I’m so sorry I
hoped” “Good-night, Madam ’’—sotto voce
to the nearest customer —“now, isn’t that a
woman all over ? ” As indeed it probably was.
mHE silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that thrills the veins,
As hearts attune their loves and likes.
For underneath Life’s surface show
There’s many a secret streamlet veers,
Till merged in Love’s wide ocean flow
Venus Pandemia thence appears.
The pulse beats flutter, wax and wane,
Then fondly fed with mutual fire,
Flame into Hymen’s torch, full fain,
And crowns each neophyte’s wild desire.
L.W.D.
mHE silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that aye dislikes
The bondage of Life’s loveless chains.
When from such chafing bondage free
Those hidden strains take golden wings,
Then over land and over sea
Love’s glorious Oratorio sings.
Then Beauty comes drest as a bride,
All joys are near that seemed remote
And all the world is sanctified—
When Oupid strikes the silent note.
W.H.
mHE silent note which Cupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that in young hearts
Assuages Love’s severest pains.
For youth in haste to gain its goal.
Will torture heart and mind and soul.
It is the keynote of the theme
The stars sang at Creation’s birth ;
It fires the poet’s brightest dream,
It runs through all that’S best on earth.
But sweetest, clearest in the life
Of loved and loving man and wife.
PLAYMAN.
mHE silent note that Oupid strikes
Reverberates in hidden strains
Of harmony that spreads afar
To thrill in Friendship’s blest domains,
Whose lute, in concord to the tune,
Adds rich harmonics to the sound,
Till in that witching lure of love
Refrains that picture peace abound ;
And Cupid feels the subtle thrill
That adds completeness to his song,
And, joining hands in Friendship’s own,
Both singing, wend their way along.
MARS.
LOVE’S CHORDS.
mHE silent note t hat, Oupid strikes
Reverberates i 1 hidden strains
Of harmony that Nature likes
To blend in all the sweet refrains.
We hear it in the long waves’ croon,
Whe. blushing dawn first greets die sea,
And in tne mve of so.ig when Noon
Has set the flowers’ fragrance free :
And when Night’s unseen choir sings
Its vespers to the stars above,
It floats on wandering wild-winds' wings
Till all the World’s attune to Love.
F. BELL[?]AS.
SAID THE ADSMITH
niHE world lias goods to sell, and it pays the
B advertising man to sell them. The old
1 stvle made much money by being ‘a good
fellow,” but to-day the man who gets the
most contracts is the man who gets the advertiser
returns from his contracts. Australia is just
beginning to wake up to the possibilities of adver-
tising. But it’s wasting a terrible lot of money.
Life insurance used to be the refuge of the failure.
Now he sets up an office, and writes “ads.
In Melbourne and Sydney there are many
“ad.” writers, and only two who know their
business. The favourite plan is to cut out
the “ads.” from American magas. and papers and
classify. If your client is a tailor, you sort out all
the tailoring “ads.” ; make a sort of hash; and
your tailor, knowing nothing, thinks you are
smart. That’s why there are so many tailoring
“ads.” that don’t appeal to Australian
wearers. The best way to become an ad.
writer is to study all the American correspondence
courses, advertising books, and rnaga. ads.
Then burn ’em, forget all about them, and begm
afresh. Have a talk with a practical tailor’s
cutter, and if you can’t write a practical, business-
bringing “ad.”, which is original, bright, snappy,
and “fresh,” chuck ad.-writing for starting a new
religion.
The most successful ad.-men in Australia are
those whose “ads.” are most condemned by the
ad.-expert. He will tell you that they are not
constructed on the lines laid down by the great
American authorities on advertising. But, the
real truth is that the most successful “ad. in
Australia is the one that does n’t go over the heads
of the people, but just gets there. To catch your
public, a plain tale makes the best telling.
Tho last two years have seen a big advance in
advertising in Australia. The pictorial ad.
has come to stay. The picture that illustrates,
that tells the tale, sells the goods. One ( does n t
see so many pictures of Blank’s “shop. He 13
beginning to realise he is not selling his s u°P*
The S.M. Herald now publishes illustrated “ ads.,
and the Melbourne dailies will have to follow.
The poster is beginning to have a vogue in
Australia, but the “cheap and nasty ’ is tending
much to disgust the public. For one go<?d
poster there are a hundred that can’t be dis-
tinguished at fifteen yards. The advertiser
forgets that the money he spends on space is
wasted if the poster doesn’t “carry.” Pity that
there is n’t some oensor to wipe out theabortions
that disfigure what should be a people’s picture
gallery !
If only there were some method of educating
the advertiser! [Can’t the “adsmith” educate
him ?] The Aust. manufacturer specially needs
enlightenment. Advertising can and does sell
anything from life insurance to railway transport,
but the average Australian manufacturer will tell
you he doesn’t need to advertise : “We have our
travellers.” In other countries that theory has
exploded long ago. It has long been recognised
by the world’s greatest manufacturers that adver-
tising establishes the “goodwill” of a brand with
the public.
How do I get “ contracts ” ? Do I prefer to do
business with a mug or a clever business man ?
First, I don’t <jet contracts ; they are given to me.
If you show a man how he can enlarge his busi-
ness sphere, and you can interest him, he gives
you a contract. The mug is no good ; because his
advertising will be a failure like his business.
From the clever business man you will learn
much to enable you to do his advertising in the
best possible way. Personally I don’t believe
there is such a thing as an advertising expert,
because a day never dawns when a man in adver-
tising cannot learn something fresh. No man can
become expert in that which we can never
know sufficiently. The most successful advertising
is that which is always unlike the other fellow s,
providing it is aimed at selling goods or making
their character or name distinctive in the public
mind.

“ Hogarth,” says an Athenceum writer, “ repre-
sents the English ideal of coarse frankness resist-
ing in vain that foreign invasion of elegant make-
believe which ultimately, by alliance with British
puritanism, gave birth to modern respectability.”
Richard Garnett died recently : a useful author,
and a commentator of wide learning who servec
his lifetime in the British Museum. He inventec
the maxim that “In the religion of Love the
courtesan is a heretic ; but the nun is an atheist
—which wants a Biblical word to set it shining.
Nora Hopper (Mrs. W. H. Chesson), dead at
five-and-thirty, was the curious authoress whc
never lived in Ireland, and (at least until a year
or two ago) had never been there ; yet wrote prose
ballads and stray verses which seemed as Irish as
the Irish themselves. Her “atmosphere” anc
“colour” were absorbed from books, and those
books translations ; for she knew no Gaelic.
Instead of (like the rest of us) talking about
Australian drama, Fred J. Forrest, of Brisbane,
does the sensible thing—he writes it. His “ I irst
}} 00 ] c ’’—Wanted a Husband— is a one-act farce
(Brisb., R. G. Gillies ; 2s.) The scene is a matri-
monial office in Sydney ; the date is 1840; the
characters are the matrimonial agent, a female
client addressed usually as “Lady,” a pickpocket,
a dustman, a bigamist, the bigamist’s three wives,
and two detectives and the action is naturally
exciting. “Other Dramas by the same author
will soon be issued. The second Drama being an
Australian Historical Comedy in Four Acts (about
48 p.p.) ’’—which we anticipate thoughtfully.

IS THERE AN INCURABLE DISEASE?
From prehistoric ages Leprosy has been considered a disease that would not yield to treatment, and has been recognised as
Incurable. Medical Science has, however, been quietly at work, and to-day we have the welcome news that it has conquer ec le
most obstinate of all diseases. No complaint, therefore, should now be considered incurable, and in this knowledge many pool
sufferers will find relief, and HOPE for a return of the health they had considered lost to them forever. “While there s ie
there’s Hope,” yet how often do we read of persons committing suicide rather than face years of misery as the slave of what t iey
considered an incurable disease. Perhaps the cure was at hand, but they would not put forth the energy to reach it.
FREEMAN AND WALLACE ELECTRO-MEDICAL INSTITUTE HAS CURED MANY SO-CALLED
INCURABLE CASES, proved in the grateful testimonies of the patients themselves. The Institute has kept pace
with the times. It is up-to-date in every detail. It has kept in touch with modern scientific research in Electro-
Therapeutics, and is founded on scientific lines. It has four Medical Specialists on its staff, who are Diplomaed from
some of the greatest and most scientific Colleges and Schools of Science in Great Britain, America, and the Continent,
and who are Experts in the Correct and Speedy Treatment and Cure of Grave Diseases or Disorders of Men. J hey will
give to any man who may be in doubt as to his physical condition, and who may be suffering from any disease which

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ M.C.” gives his idea :
I’m puzzled both by the Sosh and anti-Sosh parties.
Holman, as a representative Sosh, wants to nationalise
the production of wealth and its exchange with borrowed
money, and seems anxious to disown the “ visionary”
German Socialism, which, in its turn, is just as anxious to
disown the borrowing idea. A people owning monopolies
is one thing, an enormous increase of Cohen’s grip on
Australia is another. The level-headed German Socialists
say—tax the rich monopolist, for a tax is the annexation
of a part of national production, and limits monopoly.
Socialistic government based on borrowed money is a
hopeless policy to beat the air about, because any
Socialism with Cohen in it leaves Cohen on top. The Labor
party does not renounce the idea of borrowing, or it would
have a reply to Reid’s imaginary balance-sheet. There is
nothing lucid or definite about either the Sosh or the
anti-Sosh party; both deal only in generalities and
theories. But Australia is urgently in want of more work
and more pay. It has the practical requirements of a
fresh lot of kids to meet every day. It wants to make
more of the things it uses. It wants large estates cut up
to orovide agricultural land. The Labor party believes
in these things, but it ties up its ideas on Sosh based
on Cohen. And Sosh based on Cohen is a house built on
the sands. The interest will blow on it and it will fall.
In 1900, when Queensland was still under
the influence of the old State tariff, which gave
most industries a fair amount of real Protection,
there were 3931 ironworkers along the coast.
After four years of the mongrel tariff which
Queensland helped G. H. Reid to fasten upon
itself—partly by returning Foreign-traders and
more by returning fiscal-sinkers—the number of
ironworkers was 2311. Every third man and a
few over had meantime been sacked. And they
were not sacked because Queensland wasn t buy-
ing any iron goods. On the contrary, between
1899 and 1903 the foreign imports of machinery
alone amounted to £1,152,231. Yet up North they
still “ sink the fiscal issue.” Why not be merci-
ful, and at the same time sink the ironfounder,
etc., whose job is taken from him? He is the
fiscal issue, after all.
Wanted, that progressive land-tax for a
start. The Vic. Government has £BO,OOO sunk in
a beet-sugar factory ; and, after being idle for six
years, simply for want of beets, the waste got on
to Minister Swinburne’s nerves. So, being at
Maffra, he looked round, saw one estate of 4500
acres, capable of supplying all the vegetables the
£BO,OOO mill could handle, and decided that that
was the very thing needed to make the £BO,OOO
State asset hum. Promptly he got a price for the
land for close settlement—£2o an acre! Then
Swinburne’s jaw dropped ; and he knew what it
was that clogged the Maffra beet-sugar mill’s
wheels and made the £BO,OOO State asset a bur-
den. It was the fat landlord.
The N.S.W. legal trades-union is alarmed
at the rapid increase in its ranks, and is petition-
ing for the removal of the regulation which per-
mits a managing clerk, who has served a term of
years with a registered solicitor, to seek admission
by examination. Henceforth, the petitioners pray,
only articled clerks who can pay a heavy premium
for their articles are to be eligible for examination
as prospective solicitors. Apart from argument
on the merits of the question, it is interesting to
remember the holy horror of Pencil and Bar when
the Coal-lumpers' Union objected to all and sundry
demanding the right of admission into its over-
crowded ranks by paying one shilling, or some-
thing like that.
The efforts of N.S.W. Government to put
people “on the land” generally seem to be half-
baked. The Department has just published an
elaborate pamphlet concerning Dorrigo—littered
with gold paint and photo, blocks, and plans, and
the Department’s crest; chock-full of information
about nitrogen and potash, and The best way to
use the land, and how to get there, but not one
blessed word about how to acquire a farm, except
that the tenure is to be “ conditional-purchase
lease.” And, a 3 “conditional-purchase lease”
conveys no fixed ideas, and is a new and unknown
tenure, anyway, the information on this most
vital point is just nil.
The Golden Rule.
It has been the theme of sages
Down thro’ all the misty ages,
I must wearily aver 1
It’s a stocked-to-order sermon
For the wicked folk to squirm on
In Sin’s amphitheatre !
What if Epictetus wrote it ?
Why should every smatt’rer quote it
To long-suffering me and you ? .
What if gay old Omar said it ?
It was little to his credit—
He had nothing else to do !
What if Julius Caesar told it ?
Or if Pliny stole and sold it —
Is it cause for such a fuss ?
What if Keats and Byron sang it,
And Bill Shakspeare, too ? Why, hang it!
Does it matter much to us ?
What if Buddha vaguely guessed it ?
Or Confucius impressed it
On the minds of Chinamen ?
If it came from old Mahomet—
If he shaped a doctrine from it—
Does it matter now or then ?
It is wanted—l don’t doubt it,
And we’d suffer much without it—
It is first amongst our needs ;
Far too plain to misconstrue it,
It’s our duty—let us do it;
Yea, and prove our faith with deeds !
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
Heloise.
I never pass the little road when the quince trees
are in bloom,
With its straight grey paling fences and its foot-
path patched with grass,
But I seem to see the swaying hearse, the funeral
pall and plume,
As, decked in hideous panoply, the great black
horses pass.
By the Tweediana creeper, bright with yellow
blossoms glowing,
There stands a trembling woman, framed in shin-
ing bud and leaf ;
Her slender limbs blaek-draperied, and her little
white hand showing ;
Her little face, behind her veil, an agony of grief.
Heloise, fair Heloise ! You had no right to love
him !
Who was wedded to your rival for half a score
of years.
The nodding plumes that waved there so for-
biddingly above him,
Of surety were a just rebuke to your unlawful
tears.
His wife sobs in the carriage with the tears that
heal a sorrow,
And for comfort has his last born son to hold upon
her knees ;
For her the memory of his kiss shall help the lone
to-morrow,
But there was no kiss of parting for forgotten
Heloise !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Sleeplessness.
Night of unrest, of sorrow, and of pain,
Will ye not go, and let the day again
Come kindly back ?
Art thou so halt, so rusted in thy points,
So blind, so deaf, so crowded on thy joints,
So on the rack ?
Make not of me just such another ghost
As ye, to clutch and hold at every post
The road upon.
Thou art a rag, set up to fill with fear
The very trade which thou didst practise here
In years agone !
The sweet, sweet trade of deep and poppied sleep—
Alas, the fustian stuff to-day you keep
Is worthless all.
Ye cannot cozen me, old addle-pate;
Go by. For I’ll e’en slumber at the gate
Of Phoebus’ wall.
N.S.W.
HUGH M‘CRAE.
Pilgrims.
Regaling on the tavern’s store,
I smiled upon the Pilgrim worn,
Who paused a moment at the door
In garments stained and travel torn ;
But he returned my smile with scorn.
When I besought his company,
“ I have no time to waste,” he said ;
“ My aim’s my food—but give to me,
If so you will, a crust of bread :
The Road to Future Things I tread ! ”
“I take my ease upon the way,”
I answered, “but my path’s the same.
You have an aim ? To that I say,
’Tis a poor life that lacks an aim,
But none is worth while walking lame.
“ At every tavern door I sit,
And ask the best it has in store ;
Then, satisfied, I seek for it—
This land we both are searching for,
With hopes and strength renewed once more.
“ We cannot tell how far it lies.
But are content to take as true
The wonders of its mysteries—
I take the joys of journey, too,
And will get there as soon as you.
He shot at me a glance of scorn,
And passed upon his eager quest.
Beside the roadside, travel-worn,
I passed, with broken hopes oppressed,
The man too wise to be my guest.
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.

acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-

dential.

MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is foncarded without stamps
for return it ivill be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. Tiie Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 7, 1906.
SOME NEW FEDERAL CAPITAL SITES-
Joseph Carruthers, Premier of N.S.W., has
broken out again with his painfully wooden joke
about the Australian capital. The new develop-
ment of Joseph’s peculiar brand of mirth lies in
sending round an official to report on a lot of new
sites selected by himself, whereby he apparently
endeavors to imply two things: (1) that the
capital site has not yet been selected, and is still
an open question, and (2) that it is a matter which
in some way concerns Joseph, the State Insect,
and about which he has a right to buzz as a fly
having authority. As the State Assembly is
entering upon its third (and presumably its last)
session, and the Government has apparently very
little in the way of a definite programme in hand,
the sudden uprising of solicitor Joseph Carru-
thers at this juncture, with three or four new
Federal capital sites in his salary-hook, seems to
imply that the alleged wrongs of the Ma State are
again to be his chief political stand-by. Car-
ruthers’ latest investigation has revealed, it is
alleged, a number of inferior spots, and one
“ ideal site ” at a place described as Mahkoolma.
Mahkoolma is down near the Murrumbidgee, in
the same region as Dalgety, but a little closer to
Sydney. It has no railway, just like Dalgety, but
it is proposed that it should be approached by a
branch line from Yass, or by a sub-branch from
the existing branch line to Cooma. It doesn’t
lie on a permanent river, as Dalgety does ; it is
proposed that a capital at Mahkoolma should
depend for its water supply on such allowance as
the N.S.W. State Government might choose to
make it out of the Barren Jack reservoir,
if the State Government ever builds the
Barren Jack reservoir; and the State official
who has just found the “ideal site” adds
that in a drought like that of 1902-3 the
Barren Jack reservoir might easily become
an empty orifice and a mere dry hollow,
decorated with dead fish. Even if it didn’t
become quite dry it must be remembered that
Barren Jack is intended, first and foremost, as a
State work for the irrigation of State lands, and
that, if supplies ran low, the State which owned
the reservoir would probably consider its own
State population first —it certainly would if CAR-
RUTHERS was in office—and the Federal city
second or not at all. As regards the area of the
ideal capital site there is some obscure allusion to
87,000 acres, equal to about 136 square miles,
which means a patch of soil about 11 miles by 12.
This is 4000 acres less than the area which Sydney
and its immediate suburbs require for mere
standing room, and only a fraction of the area
which they require for water catchment, reser-
voirs, drainage outlets, and the other essentials of
a decent modern city. Of course, there is no
evidence that Joey Carruthers would surrender
the Mahkoolma site even if the Australian Parlia-
ment were willing to accept it. He offered the
Dalgety site and withdrew it with the loud laugh
which shows the vacant mind as soon as it was
accepted, and so long as his chief political stock-
in-trade consists in posing as the wild and hairy
avenger of imaginary wrongs done to the Ma
State he will probably continue to find in every
proposed capital site a bitter wrong and a reason
for secession.
It is to be hoped that the Australian Parlia-
ment, when it meets, will make it clear to Car-
ruthers that it wants no more suggestions about
sites, and that the matter is settled beyond recall.
If it doesn’t, Joseph may go on for years dis-
covering new sites, offering tnem, refusing to
grant them when accepted, weeping about
“Federal delay” and the obvious intention to
deprive N.S.W. of the capital, and wildly defend-
ing the Ma State with his sixpenny axe against
imaginary enemies. Apart from the original
fact that the capital was fixed in N.S.W. by
fraud and lying—that the people of N.S.W.
accepted by a solid majority the original Consti-
tution, which left the capital an open question;
that they accepted it by a majority vastly larger
than the one which, byN.S.W.’s solemn agree-
ment with the other States, was required to
ensure acceptance ; and that the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, by an infamous breach of faith, annulled
this acceptance, and then demanded possession of
the capital before it would get out of the road—
apart from this preliminary infamy, the capital
question has already been more than sufficient of
a curse. The Australian Government spent the
best part of a year in making courteous proposals
to the effect that the N.S.W. State Government
should suggest a site, and in receiving uncouth
and bear-like answers, or in waiting without any
answer at all. Finally, at long last, it in-
duced N.S.W. Government to appoint a
purely State Commissioner to deal with the
matter. He chose what were supposed to be the
best and most suitable positions in N.S.W., and
reported on them. They were formally put under
offer in the sense that the State lands in the
vicinity were reserved from sale, that being the
most definite kind of offer that N.S.W. would
make. The Australian Parliament selected a
location in the district which the N.S.W. State
Commissioner reported on as the very best avail-
able. And when it had thus done everything
that N.S.W. suggested, and conformed strictly to
the letter of the Federal Constitution as well,
Joseph Carruthers howled at it as if he were a
dog, and refused to grant the proposed site on any
terms whatever.
* * •» *
Now that Joseph Carruthers has commenced,
at the eleventh hour, to dig up new sites, with the
apparent idea of starting all over again a matter
which seemed just approaching settlement after
five years’ disturbance, it is time for the Aus-
tralian Parliament to remember that it isn’t
Joseph’s horse nor his ass nor his ox, and that it
owes something to its own dignity and the
dignity of the people it represents. The fact that
Joseph is, politically, an uncivil lout, and an
individual who picks quarrels for no other visible
purpose save that of self-advertisement, gives him
no claim to special consideration. His imaginary
grievance may be a valuable asset to him in State
politics, and he may find it convenient to divert
attention from unpleasant local episodes by abuse
of Federal politicians, and by wild outcry about
imaginary State wrongs, and by posing as the
heroic solicitor who is saving N.S.W. from some
awful invisible injury; but the country’s business
can’t stand over indefinitely to provide Joe
Carruthers with a convenient grievance and
a discordant battle-shriek. The clear duty
of the Australian Parliament is to put
any new suggestions about capital sites at
the back of the stove, to intimate that the matter
is already settled, and to take the promptest
measures it can to get possession of the selected
area at Dalgety. If it re-opened the question,
and finally accepted the new site which Joseph
has just disinterred, it would do no earthly good
to Australia, nor to N.S.W.; and it would
sacrifice its own rights and dignity in order
that Joseph might pose before the electors
as the great man who wantonly kicked the
Parliament of all Australia just to show his im-
portance, and was not kicked as he deserved in
return. Probably the humiliation of the Aus-
tralian Parliament would be accentuated, if it
were foolish enough to climb down and accept
some new offer, by another howl of derisive
laughter from Joseph, and his refusal to surrender
the new site, just as he refused to surrender
the old one; and by his offer to dig up some
more possible Federal capitals, and to commence
the whole discussion afresh for the third time.
After the offer of Dalgety, and its withdrawal as
soon as it was accepted, Joseph Carruthers is
outside the pale of negotiation, or he ought to be.
* * * *
Australia is not Palestine, and Joseph Car-
ruthers is not the Palestine Exploration Society,
with a life-long digging contract. There must be
finality somewhere. Against the selection the
Australian Parliament has already made there is
no possible objection, except the political exist-
ence of Joseph Carruthers and his capacity for
objecting to everything. Carruthers first de-
nounced the proposal to build a capital on Monaro
because it would deprive N.S.W. of a rich,
splendid, and thickly-populated area, yielding
an immense revenue. When his figures and
alleged facts were shown to be blankly untrue, he
next denounced it because the locality was such a
poor, barren, empty, beggarly region that
the necessary railway to the Federal city wouldn’t
pay for axle-grease. Between these two utter-
ances of Joseph’s it may be fairly assumed that
the district is just a good ordinary sample. It is
in N.S.W., as required by the Constitution. It is
not less than 100 miles from Sydnev as provided
by the Constitution. The area asked for is not
less than 100 square miles as set forth in the Con-
stitution. The proposed site is nearer Sydney
than Melbourne, therefore the remnants of the
old Cabbage Tree Mob, to whom Sydney is the
middle of the universe and the only place of im-
portance, have no reason to complain. It is in a
region of which N.S.W. is making less use than it
is of any other permanently habitable part of its
area; therefore the bit of territory asked for is all
the less loss to N.S.W. And because it is an
almost uninhabited area the proposal to put
a city there, instead of putting it at the back
door of Sydney, is all the more commendable,
in view of the fact that everybody—even the
people who are howling most bitterly about
the necessity of putting the city at the back door
of Sydney—profess to believe that what Aus-
tralia wants is to attract population to its empty
spaces (like Monaro), and not to congest it further
in its two big and already over-bloated cities (one
of which is Sydney). Further, the Dalgety scheme
will place the capital in the healthiest part of
N.S.W. and on the banks of an ever-flowing river
—not a precarious unbuilt tank like Barren Jack ;
and the man who would put a city anywhere else
when the bank of a pure, swift, ever-flowing river
is available is a fool.
* * * •
As for the area asked for (900 square miles) it
can only be said that it is a pathetically small
area, and not one-fifth of what should have been
demanded. It is barely sufficient for a city, a
water supply area with necessary reservoirs, and
an outlet for drainage. After the experience the
Australian Government and Parliament have had
of Joey Carruthers, the cantankerous and un-
speakable public worm of Australian politics,
there are certain things the Commonwealth cannot
afford to risk : It cannot afford to risk having the
sources of its city’s w'ater supply anywhere except
in its own territory and under its own control,
lest the Joey Government cuts off the supply. It
cannot risk having a Federal territory which
borders on N.S.W. only, and has no other outlet
either by the sea or overland through another
State, lest the Joey Ministry cuts off its railway
communication. And it cannot take the chance
of having such a small territory that it must de-
pend on the adjacent State of N.S.W. for an
outlet for the sewage of the Federal city, lest
a Joey Government refuses it that outlet,
and makes it a city of typhoid and other
diseases. Long and painful experience has taught
it, or should have taught it, to regard its Joey as
an individual who will do the Australian Govern-
ment any and every kind of injury he can, even
when he gains by it no shred or semblance of
advantage, merely for the same perverted kind of
advertisement which came, in a larger way, to the
man who burned the temple of Ephesus. There-
fore, if Carruthers invites it to stultify itself by
going back on its selection of Monaro as the place
for its capital city, and to follow him humbly
round the country like his dog to look at new
capital sites; and if he tries to beguile it with the
tentative offer of a scrap of land where the
Australian capital will depend on his charity for
its water supply and its communication with the
outer world, it should decline without thanks.
Thanks are wholly uncalled for in such a case.
THE MADNESS OF THE LABOR
EXTREMISTS.
If it were possible for the mad-headed section of
the Labor party (which is just now engaged in
rushing the good work of years down a steep place
into the sea) to think coolly—perhaps it is, but
The Bulletin rather thinks it isn’t—the members
of that section would be at present seeing a danger
signal in the cordial approval they are winning
from Mr. G. H. Reid and from the Tory press.
Said Reid in a Sydney suburb the other night:—
He was glad that the Labor parties were not prepared
to take on Mr. Deakin in his coming battle. They were
prepared to fight their own battle. That was a fair posi-
tion to take up, and would haveja good effect on the fight
And the Sydney Daily Telegraph reprints with
chuckling approval the articles in which Brisbane
Worker urges the Labor party to play the Reid
game, and come into the field for the forthcoming
fight in a hopeless position, armed with hopeless
weapons. “The Lord hath delivered them into
our hands,” said Cromwell at Dunbar; and
foolish fanatics are working all they can to make
it possible for Reid to say that with the same
confidence next November.
* •* * •
George Reid proclaims that such actions as
that of the Victorian Political Labor Conference
in perfidiously breaking the pact made between
the Labor party in Parliament and the Lyne-
Isaacs wing of the Protectionist party re-
present a “fair position,” and “will have a
good effect on the fight.” A good effect !
Now, what precisely does Reid mean by a
food effect ? Does he mean that it will help the
labor party to greater strength, and thus make
the Reid chance of getting to office on an anti-
Australian, anti-democratic policy more hopeless ?
The question is absurd. Mr. Reid means that an
intransigeant attitude on the part of a section of
the Labor party will have a good effect in helping
him to climb into power on a platform of colorless
Conservatism, plus sectarianism, which he calls
anti-Socialism. He hopes that it will take thou-
sands of votes from the Labor party. He hopes
also that it will take even more votes from
the Deakin party, which (differing from the
Labor party only in its fidelity to the root
reform of High Protection in its immediate pro-
gramme of legislation) has elected to stand out at
the next elections as ready to work sympatheti-
cally with the great reasonable body that is the
true Labor party, and will accordingly be
punished by the electors for all the madnesses
with which the name of Labor can be associated.
That is why faith-breaking on the part of the
section of the Labor party outside Parliament—
which wants to get into Parliament and is there-
fore “agin ” the sitting members—and wild extra-
vagance on the part of a section of the Labor
press has a “ good effect ” in Reid’s eyes.
The votes that are frightened from Watson
will also be frightened from Deakin, and, having
no other place to go, will be recorded for Reid—
or else they will not be recorded at all, which will
also help Reid.
• * • •
It is not possible, as the political situation
shows now, for Deakin to score at the ex-
pense of Watson, or Watson at the expense
of Deakin. There is a section of voters which
regards Protection —better and more complete
Protection than the present tariff affords—as of
paramount importance. That section will vote
Deakin. There is another section which, whilst
Protectionist in conviction, is also strongly
Laborite, and will vote Watson on the ground
that Watson is a democrat and a Protec-
tionist. A very small section is Freetrade
and Labor (most of the old Freetrade Laborites
are now in their proper camp, screeching
with renegade Joseph Cook for anti-Socialism),
and will vote Watson because it “ sinks the fiscal
issue.” There is yet another section, Tory and
retrogressive in its ideas of social legislation, nar-
row and bigoted in its religious intolerance, anti-
Australian in its sentiment, which will vote Reid.
But after subtracting all these definitely resolved
elements there remains a great mass of undeter-
mined voters whose ballots will really decide the
issue. They are Protectionists of a sort, or
Freetraders of a sort, or inclined to be
Laborites, but more than all inclined to
be lazy and to dodge the trouble of enter-
taining definite political convictions until a
general election nearly approaches. As a class of
supporters they are not admirable. They are
liable, like a mercenary army, to swing over at
the last moment to what they imagine to be the
winning side. They always come to a decision
which is rather emotional than reasoned. But
such as they are, these fluctuating voters are the
real arbiters of the fight. A few months ago, when
Watson resigned office and wished to appeal to
the country, they would have voted on the
Watson side almost to a man, and the Labor
party, allied with the radical wing of the Protec-
tionists, would have swept the polls. After the
brief and inglorious career of the Reid
Government, the fluctuating vote was left
in very much the same position, except
that it showed some tendency to divide itself be-
tween Deakin and Watson. Since then, that ten-
dency has strengthened, until, three months ago,
the vote in almost every Australian constituency
not guided by religious bigotry or mere Tory
horror of progress would have voted for a Deakin
candidate or a Watson candidate almost indiffer-
ently. The two parties were thought to be en-
gaged in pursuing the same ideal—the advance-
ment of Australia —by. courses divergent a little,
but ultimately converging.
♦ * * ft
Now there is a very great difference. A section
of the Labor party has put its aims and its methods
in the worst possible light before the public. It
has shown itself possessed neither of common-
sense nor of honorable recognition of solemn
obligations. The effect on the voters has been
enormous. Since the decision of the Victorian
Political Labor Council to condemn the Labor
party to be always a sort of mercenary party—and
a faithless mercenary party at that—the Reid
campaign has begun to be taken seriously. Before
that, it was matter only for laughter—the des-
perate efforts of a very fat man to save himself
from drowning with such a poor straw as anti-
Socialism. Now it gets strength, real strength.
If the fluctuating voters would think, they
would recognise that the Labor party in Parlia-
ment does not sympathise with the follies of
the extremists, and has the practical power
to nullify them when it really chooses. If again,
they would only think, the allegiance they are
disavowing would be more reasonably transferred
to Deakin from Watson, or to Watson from
Deakin, than from either to Reid. But this
class of voter does not think as deeply as that.
It sees representative bodies of Labor men adopt-
ing an attitude and a policy which is intolerable
to public opinion; it has been for some months
associating Deakin with Watson as some sort of
allied forces ; and it reckons it must punish both
for the madness or the treachery of the Victorian
extremists and the Brisbane Worker, by swing-
ing over to Reid. The more mad-lieaded of
the Labor organisations insist that the issue is
between them and their folly and Reid and his
folly. The public takes them at their word, and
prepares to stampede to Reid as the fatter and
more comfortable folly of the two.
* * * *
If the position of Australia at the present time
were not so anxious—almost the word desperate
is warranted—there would be no cause for great
uneasiness in the follies of the Labor extremists.
They mean, at the worst, that at the next elections
the Labor party may suffer severe losses; that for
the life of next Parliament the chance of any of
the able administrators who made up the Watson
Cabinet assisting in the government of the country
will be lost; that either Reid will hold office flaunt-
ing his “ necklace of negatives” or (almost equally
bad) Deakin will have office but not power.
Under ordinary circumstances, undesirable as all
this would be, there would be nothing utterly
disastrous in it. The 1908 or 1909 elections would
put things right again, the Labor extremists
having been taught a lesson and the voters a
lesson. But, as things are, Australia cannot
afford to have another chaotic Parliament. It is
almost a matter of now or never. If the present
opportunity is not seized to begin the really essen-
tial work of nation-building—the work to which
Prime Minister Deakin solemnly pledged himself
at his last Sydney meeting —there is no certainty
that that work can ever be undertaken under
conditions of peace and security. We may build
our walls now with both hands. In the near
future it may be necessary to have in one hand a
sword, in the other a trowel.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
N.S.W. Lands Inquiry—the Findings.
The Royal Commission appointed to inquire into
the administration of N.S.W. Lands Department
presented its report last week. Justice Owen has
not quite completed his work. He still leaves the
way open for evidence from W. N. Willis ; and
in regard to McNair and Hoskins, against whom
a charge is pending, he preserves silence as to
matters affecting that charge. But, otherwise, a
great task comes to its conclusion. The gist of
Mr. Justice Owen’s findings are :
The Personalities.
W. P. Crick, ex-Minister for Lands, accepted one-half
the sums received by Peter Close, as land agent. No
officer of the Lands Department received money from
Close.
Premier Carruthers was in no way implicated by the
evidence. He submitted his accounts for examination,
and nothing in any way suspicious was discovered.
Land agents generally did not charge exorbitant fees.
The three exceptions were Close, Willis and Bath.
W. N. Willis’ departure from Australia was not sanc-
tioned by the Commissioner. At the time of that depar-
ture there was, in Justice Owen’s opinion, no legal means
of stopping it. Mr. Owen cannot see that the N.S.W.
Government could well have done more than it did in
applying for the extradition of Willis.
Under-Secretary'for Lands MacFarlane was “ too eager
to carry out the wishes of the Minister ” in regard to
granting improvement leases, “ even when he must have
known that the lease was not within the Act.”
Monet.
The land agents who charged exorbitantly scored as
follows in the matter of fees: —
W. N. Willis .. .. £44,913
P. C. Close 15,649
C. Bath 2,675
Re the examination of accounts, the Commission quotes
this from the accountant’s report
“ His (Mr. Crick’s) and other banking accounts ex-
amined by me really afford very little information having
a direct bearing upon the inquiry now being held ; but
they are nearly all remarkable for the large proportion of
cheques drawn that have been cashed for notes, and
especially for the large amount deposited in notes as com-
pared with cheques.
“ Excluding amounts under £5O, the notes and com
shown as deposited amount, I find, to no less a sum than
£66,619 Is. 5d., as follows :
“ This amount is practically the same as that shown
by the evidence to have been paid t;o land agents, but
this would appear to be a mere coincidence.
“ According to the evidence furnished to me,
would appear to have received in all £57,844 13s. 3d.—
but the Myall Creek and some other payments would


appear to have beeti made by cheque, and, allowing for
these, and for the sums received by agents whose accounts
are not dealt with, the amount shown as paid in notes
and coin would probably be £BOOO or £9OOO more
than the amounts paid to Messrs. Willis, Closb, and
Crick in respect of the matters disclosed.”
Improvement Leases.
Of 50 expired pastoral holdings coming before the
notice of the Commission, only four were subdivided for
settlement, though of the 50, 23 had been previously set
apart as suitable for settlement.
In some cases the land was allowed on improvement
lease, not to the old Crown tenant, but to some new man
who had no right to benefit under the policy of allowing
the tenant of a lapsed leasehold an extension under
improvement lease.
In 39 cases improvement leases were granted in spite of
adverse reports ; one of these occurred during Carru-
thers’ administration (but was subsequently cancelled by
him): three in the administration of Thomas Hassall, 35
in the administration of W. P. Crick.
Improvement lease lands were liable to revocation for
public purposes, but the sinking of an artesian well on
any block made that block safe from revocation. In some
cases improvement leases were granted with the condi-
tion of an artesian well being sunk (which supplied a
safeguard against revocation), though the land affected
was outside the known artesian area. Thus “the lessees,
by sinking bores to the limited depth which, under the
circumstances, the conditions allowed, obtained at a very
small cost the waiver on the part of the Crown of the
right of withdrawal, and thus secured for themselves the
land for the full term of the lease.”
Extensions of improvement leases were granted in
cases when, in the opinion of the Commissioner, the
Minister had not the power to make any such grant.
Two Typical Land Agency Cases.
The Windagee leases, taken up in the names of Hayes
and Rae and Mrs. Willis, were really for the benefit of
W. N. Willis. The names mentioned were used only to
enable W. N. Willis to get the lands for himself.
The Mungery West improvement leases were got by
W. N. Willis for Mr. Strahorn through dummies; the
land —at any rate the greater portion of it —was suitable
for settlement, and should not have been let on improve-
ment lease.
The Policy ok the Past.
The Myall Creek land purchase was justifiable and not
corrupt.
There was nothing illegal shown in regard to land
exchange transactions.
The granting of improvement leases was carried too
far by Lands Minister Crick, in that some land suitable
for settlement was locked up in such leases ; but the im-
provement lease “ policy ” was in itself justifiable.
Tiie Policy op the Future.
The Lands Department should be vested in three com-
missioners—not present officers of the Lands Department.
To encourage closer settlement all unnecessary reserves
should be cancelled ; illegal leases should be revoked ;
light railways and irrigation works should be pushed on ;
re-purchases of private estates should be made.
Land agents should be registered by the Land Appeal
Court, and their charges made liable to taxation.
Jt is not open for The Bulletin to comment
with any freedom on these findings, on
account of various pending proceedings. But
Premier Carruthers is to Vie congratulated
on a verdict which clears him of all sus-
picion. The Government generally has been
acquitted by Justice Owen of any wish to hamper
him in his work. The duty now remains with
Parliament and the courts to carry the unhappy
business to an end.
♦
Protection First.
On Prime Minister De akin’s “ Australia for the
Australians, and Protection before everything”
pronouncement, Labor leader Watson expounded
his party’s policy as follows :
We say that the conditions under which, in the national
interest, industries may be carried on should be first laid
down—how the industries should be run, and whether it
is necessary by tariff enactment to encourage the crea-
tion of industries. Important as I consider Protection to
be, industrial and social legislation is more important
still. . . .
The Labor party’s desire to ensure decent living
and wage conditions is proper ; but, so long as
there are no industries worth mentioning, the fix-
ing of such conditions is to a great extent wasted
effort. The Labor party may regulate wages,
hours, etc., till everything is as beautiful as a rose-
bud ; but all the time it is doing this pretty busi-
ness, the foreigner is dumping his surplus pro-
ducts into this country and rendering it harder
and more hopeless for the Australian manufacturer
to establish an industry, and daily driving the
Australian worker either out of the Common-
wealth or to one of the poorly-paid, drudging
“great natural industries” in which labor and
hours are not regulated. Through Melbourne
streets, the day the visiting brown contingent
marched from Port Melbourne to the Zoo, there
passed another procession—eight two-horse lorries
packed with foreign-made motor cars. The Aus-
tralian mechanic has proved that he can do what
the American can do—has shown him the way at
times—and, given efficient Protection, every
ounce of material and every particle of the labor
on those eight loaded lorries could have been
supplied in the Commonwealth. But the Labor
party says, “ No ; we must first fix fair industrial
conditions; afterwards we may decide that the
building of motor-cars is necessary, or perhaps we
may make it a State monopoly. In the meantime,
instead of training and employing hundreds of
clever, cultivated mechanics, who would be of the
first service in defending their country and would
all the while constitute the finest and best citizens,
we are satisfied for our people to milk cows under
conditions of gross sweating.” In theory the
Labor party’s fiscal policy is as attractive as a
house on fire. In practice it is not much more
useful than the same house after the fire has been
there. »
A Second Ballot.
The report goes that a short Bill will be rushed
through this coming session of the Australian
Parliament to provide for a second ballot at
Federal elections in cases where the top candidate
has not got an absolute majority, and it can only
be hoped that the report is true. In the present
House of Reps, there are 12 members who repre-
sent each a minority vote— Culpin (Q.), Quick
(Vic.), Hume Cook (Vic.), G. Wilson (Vic.),
Crouch (Vic.), Gibb (Vic.), Skene (Vic.),
Ronald (Vic.), Robinson (Vic.), Phillips (Vic.),
Fysh (Tas.), and McWilliams (Tas.). This is a
pretty large proportion out of 75 politicians,
but bad as things are now, they are likely
to become much worse. According to present
appearances, next election is to be run on
purely triangular principles, as far as the bulk
of constituencies is concerned, with small, casual,
irresponsible angles thrown in. They will almost
certainly be worse in Victoria, the State in which
minority representation is already at its worst.
There it seems likely that almost every election
will be a three, or four, or five-cornered fight, the
parties being
(1) The Labor Protectionist.
(2) The other Protectionist.
(3) The Foreign-trade Tory.
(4) Anybody else —perhaps several of him.
As a rule, the Freetrade Tory vote will go pretty
solidly to one man while the Protectionist vote
will be split—in many cases it would appear that
it will be very evenly split. And the probable
results will be something like the following :
Tory Foreign-trader .. .. • • • • 4106
Protectionist No, 1 (Labor or otherwise) .. 4050
„ No. 2( „ ) 3998
Smith
Jones -
If there is a second ballot between the two leading
men, Foreign-trade and Toryism will, of course,
go down with a memorable smite. The Protec-
tionist vote that went at first to the third man
will go to the second when the third man is dis-
qualified, and the Foreign-trader will be beaten—
especially with Smith, Jones and Brown out of
the way —by two to one. Without the second
ballot he will probably get in very largely, and it
seems that the Protectionists will only have a fair
chance of winning where they represent nearly a
two to one majority. The Protectionists of the
Deakin party must needs nominate a candidate
in every constituency, unless such a high and
complete tariff is carried this session that the
fiscal question will lose some of its urgency. The
Labor party also threatens to nominate a candi-
date in every constituency, and as the Labor can-
didate in Victoria will be a Protectionist every
time at the polls (though he may be a fiscal-sinker
in the House), it will be exclusively the Demo-
cratic party that will be split. The Labor Pro-
tectionist won’t capture a vote from the Foreign-
trade Tory gang,- but will rather consolidate it.
The position matters less in N.S.W., for there the
Labor man is a Freetrader or a Protectionist in-
discriminately ; he will capture votes from both
sides, and in the end things may be just about as
broad are long. Certainly at last election
N.S.W. put up the most honorable record of elect-
ing every one of its 20 members by a clear
and definite majority. But in Victoria the
splitting of votes between Labor Protectionists
and plain Protectionists, and the multiplicity of
candidates generally, influenced the result greatly
in 1903, and out of 23 Victorian members no less
than nine represent minorities. The coming
election will see a better-defined split; there will
be three parties everywhere, and the irrepressible
Smith, Jones, and Brown as well; and it is quite
possible that, without provision for a second
ballot, something like 17 out of 22 seats may be
captured for the Reid party by miserable Tory
and Foreign-trade minorities. And if three
tickets are nominated for the Senate the same
sort of thing may happen there. The second
ballot is urgently wanted as regards both Houses,
therefore the hint that a Second Ballot Bill may
come to light this session sounds like an indefinite
promise of good news.

Maoriland’s Freak Railways.
The Maoriland idea of building a railroad is the
British idea of taking the Tugela position—climb-
ing over obstacles regardless of cost or of the
possibility of finding a way round. Maoriland
has been making freak railways for forty years.
The first ever constructed—it was in the old pro-
vincial days—was bored cheerfully through the
Port Hills from Christchurch to Lyttelton, and
freight carried on that line still pays a tunnel
impost. Then a track was laid on a steep
gradient round the sea-washed cliffs at Blueskin
(Otago). It afterwards had to be ripped up
and carried through a tunnel for safety. The
next thing that happened amazes every engineer
and stupefies every tourist who looks at it to this
day. This is the Rimutaka incline on the Wel-
lington-Napier railway. The line is carried oyer
a summit 1000 ft. high for the sheer joy of going
down on a grip rail through the dirtiest tunnels in
the country. There is a drop of over 1000 ft. in
three miles to the Wairarapa plain, and every
train has to be pulled up by three Fell engines
on a special three-rail track that gives the
most unpleasant ride money can buy in Aus-
tralasia. It is only within the last few years that
the Rimutaka has been made a decently safe line at
all, and it takes an hour and a half for the express to
travel eighteen miles. After this, the Palmerston-
Wanganui line was constructed through the
country of fat squatters—Rangitikei. To be
quite sure of properly serving one run-holder, the
line was carried up a hillside rising 440 ft. in five
miles, while there was a flat route with a short
tunnel running alongside. This is the spot where,
a few years ago, an army of caterpillars crawled
across the line and brought the express to a stand-
still. Of course, all these things were done in the
bad old days of the capitalists, but the present
Liberal Government had been in office ten
years, and had fairly lived down all suspicion
of helping the squatter—besides which there were
no squatters thereabouts to help—before the
route of the North Island main trunk railway was
definitely settled. And this is what it did. The
railway is now bang up against the edge of a
plateau at each end, and has somehow or other to
make a rise of 2000 ft. to get on the plateau. This
is achieved by a steady grade, not unworkable,
at the southern end, but at the northern ex-
tremity there is a regular tangle of tunnels and
cuttings, in the throes of which the line crosses
itself, and performs four curves of the smallest
possible radius. And when the line reaches the
top cf the plateau it has to travel for fifty miles
through God-forsaken pumice country, which will
never be worth the breath spent in cursing it.
When engineers look at the Pukerimu spiral they
will laugh and cry at so much folly. The thing is
a sheer and absolute blunder, committed for fear
Auckland would be jealous if the line passed
through the rich country of Taranaki. The bad
old days are not yet over.
