The “Star’s” Stinking Fish Yarn.
Last week the Australian Star (Sydney) broke
out into a huge scare-heading about “Four
Britishers Gaoled” and the “ Indifference of Re-
sponsible Ministers,” and it burst into a smaller
sub-heading about what the four British subjects
in question “think of Sydney.” And it seems
that Sydney is crushed by their disapproval—at
least, this is the only verdict that can be reason-
ably arrived at after a careful reading of
four columns of slush, and an indignant ietter
signed “A Britisher,” in which the Britisher
said that the treatment of the four British subjects
in question was a “rotten sham,” and that his
heart bled over the business. After all this
screaming and riot it might be reasonably sup-
posed that something dreadful had happened to
the four, but the case seems a very ordinary one.
They reached Australia first as stowaways on the
British steamer Runic. They got on board at
Liverpool, their reason being that England was
so cold and brutal to four British subjects that
they wanted to get away at any price. The
British captain, they allege, made them work hard
all the way out without any pay—the Star
says nothing about his callous treatment of four
Britishers—and perpetually threatened these four
British subjects that he would take them back
with him to England (presumably working them
hard without pay all the time), and then get a
callous English magistrate to give them six weeks
in gaol to finish with. This habit of giving British
subjects six weeks’ gaol in cold, unfeeling London
aroused no comments on the part of the Star.
At Albany the British captain is charged with a
further outrage on four free British subjects by
locking them up lest they should escape. At
Adelaide, however, they did escape, and there
they stole a passage on an Australian steamer
bound for Sydney. The Australian captain
naturally wanted to collect fares from his passen-
gers, and when they scornfully offered id. for the
crowd he seems to have become a Fenian or a
pro-Boer or something; at all events he gave them
into the custody of a Sydney policeman. They
were handcuffed —which gave them a bad opinion
of the country—and the magistrate ordered them
to pay a small fine or do seven days for diddling
the Australian captain out of £8 in fares.
And, though this was much easier than the
six weeks they had expected to get in England,
the episode brought their opinion of the country
down to zero. They simply crushed it with their
disapproval. However the fines were paid by an
enthusiastic admirer named Gosche, and they
were let out of gaol, and money was given them,
and a billet was found for one of them, and the
other three became a temporary charge on the
State, and the Star stated in columns of feeble
rage that Australia was a degraded, low-down
place for not carrying cheeky Englishmen free on
its boats, and that the business was a Horror.
Yet it is pretty certain that the Star would
violently prosecute any British subject resident in
Australia who did it out of £2, let alone £8 ; and
if he got three months, instead of seven days, it
would regard the matter as quite natural. The
Star tries hard to be a real Stinking Fish at
times, but a prawn is about its natural size.
An Early-Closing Fiction.
The Melbourne shopkeepers who howled to Bent
for the repeal of the Early Closing Act, rashly
got down to particulars, and gave a list of 16 per-
sons who are being “ ruined ” by Early Closing.
Inquiries were ordered, and this was the re-
sult :
A. —lt was alleged that receipts in three months had
fallen £3O, but it was found that during March there was
an increase of £l2.
B. No books were kept, and the shop is one that re-
mains open till 8 o’clock. The complaint is that there
are less people about owing to other shops closing.
C. —Receipts were about the same as before the Act
came into force, and this month have been very good.
Refused to show the books.
D. Takings decreased £3O in 10 weeks on a fairly large
turnover. The trader strongly favors closing at 6 o clock,
but thinks all others should close at the same tinie.
E. —Keeps no books, and favors closing at 7 o’clock.
P. —Keeps no books. Complains of losing trade to
repair shops.
G. —Not required to close at all, but has closed volun-
tarilv, and trade showed a decrease in three months of
£9.
H. —Said she had lost trade, but refused to show her
books. This shop, as to a large portion of the goods, is
kept open to a late hour.
I. —Books showed a decrease of £lO in three months,
but the takings of three weeks of May were greater than
for the whole of May, 1905.
J. —ls not required to close at all, but considers she is
hurt because other shops are shut.
K. —Says he has lost one-third of his trade. Keeps
open late for the sale of a large portion of his stock.
L. —Has a very small stock, and keeps no books.
M. —Would not allow her books to be seen. “Could”
tell her losses, but would not do so.
N. —No shopkeeper of the name at the address.
O. —Refused to produce books, but said his takings had
fallen off £3 a week.
P. Said to have lost trade in repairs, but no books
were produced.
So the only persons who can prove any decrease
in trade either voluntarily close or want to be
closed. Melbourne Argus says: “The results
did not assist the Cabinet very much.” In the
way of creating an impression that the Tory is an
unblushing liar they should have helped consider-
ably.
The Cannibal in “God’s Country.”
The amazing and almost incredible things which
the United States has lately been finding in its
canned meat and sausages at Chicago have made
the world look on American provender,
moment at least, with a bitterly hostile eye. r lhe
contents are varied, and are alleged to include
ordinary plain meat, horse, rat, rat-bait, soap-
suds, entrails, old rags, old sausage meat that had
been freshened and revived by various artifices,
cancerous and tuberculous carcases, unborn calf,
and man, or at least bits of him. It doesn t seem
to be distinctly alleged, though it is distinctly
hinted, that whole men have ever been tinned
or sausaged at Chicago. But it is sworn to that
workmen have often been mutilated in the
machinery, and that the mechanism wasn’t stopped
merely for the purpose of rescuing a severed leg or
arm or finger out of the mass that was to become
part of the public victuals. Solongasit was only the
arm that went in the sausage while the rest of the
man remained outside, instead of the arm remain-
ing outside while the rest of the man went into
sausage, the meat purveyors of “ God’s country ”
seem to have been satisfied, and perhaps they
wouldn’t have drawn the line even there but for
the fact that a hat and a whole suit of clothes in
the meat would have been hard to account for.
For a time, at least, there should be a big slump
in U.S. meat all over the world, though the
matter cannot directly affect Australia to any
great extent; this country has for years been
tinning better meat than the U.S. produces, and
is, or should be, a stranger to the products of the
curiously mixed industry at Chicago. Still the
knowledge that such an industry exists, and the
probability that there are many dozens equally
horrible, should make Australia realise the wisdom
of the Federal statesmen who drafted the Com-
merce Act, and the wisdom of the Parliament
which passed it. The object of that Act is to
prevent the cannibal variety of tinned meat, and
other alarming things of the kind, being imported
into this country to ruin its stomach, or sent out of
this country to ruin its good name and someone
else’s stomach. So far the loudest section of the
Australian daily press has been very busy trying to
prove that it was Protection that caused the man
and the horse to get into the tinned meat, and
the dog into the lard at Chicago, and they have
quite omitted to notice that the Chicago case
shows the great need for a measure like the Aus-
tralian Commerce Act to prevent unrestricted
Private Enterprise producing similar sensations
here. In a general way the Foreign-trade press
seems to hold that but for Protection the United
States would have been fed on imported tinned
meat grown in Belgium or England, and, being
imported, that meat would necessarily have been
clean and wholesome. As a matter of fact, the
tinned meat with the man in it supplies
Australia with three lessons—on the need for
high Protection, the need for some such measure
as the Commerce Act, and the need to hang on
for all we are worth to the system of keeping our
railways national property. Australia requires
high Protection to build up national industries as
great as those of Chicago, so that it won’t need to
rely too much on unclean foreign products in
the meat line or in any line. It requires
a rigidly - administered Commerce Act to
E revent its industries, when they are built, from
eing run on dirty Chicago principles. And it
wants to carefully preserve its system of national
railways to prevent its industries, when they are
built, degenerating into monopolies, like the one
at Chicago. Every great U.S. monopoly, without
exception, has been built on private railway
ownership and on nothing else. The Beef I rust,
like the rest, grew up by getting private rail-
ways to carry its sausage at rates so much below
those charged on everybody else’s sausage that
there was nothing left for everybody else to do
save close up or become part of the great Trust.
All the Freetrade in the world would make little
difference to a Trust which can prevent the rail-
ways carrying any meat but its own, and which
can bring a dozen other influences to bear to pre-
vent any meat but its own being sold even in
places which can be reached without railway car-
riage. It was when the private enterprise railway
system had put the Beef Trust beyond competi-
tion that it began to get utterly reckless about
what animal went into its tinned meats and its
sausage. It reckoned that it was the only sausage,
and so could use any ingredients it pleased.
The Men of the Melville Island.
What time Frank Bullen was talking to Sydney
audiences of the urgent need for reform in Britain’s
mercantile marine service, telling them how hardly
the crews of sailing vessels are treated and how
little is known of their grievances by the lands-
men who sing so many songs about them, it
happened that nine sailors of the Melville Island,
from Peru, were charged at the Queenscliff (Vic.)
court with the crime of refusing to do any further
duty on the vessel in question. They said that
the food was insufficient and putrid, that the. ship
had not been carrying proper protective side-lights
according to regulations, and that they had
other reasons for being “fuirup” of their job.
The captain said his say in opposition to these
complaints, but the Bench sided with the men,
and they were discharged. The captain then sent
them to Melbourne with a note to the superin-
tendent of the Sailors’ Home, instructing him
that they were to be paid off. Nevertheless, the
captain did not pay them off. He decided to pay
them out, if possible, to which end he ordered
them to return to the ship, threatening to prose-
cute them for desertion if they refused.
Banzai.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am a speck to be ignored, a weak and worthless
thing;
I kiss the dust before a lord, and scrape before a
king.
I have no independence worth a counterfeited
cent —
To all the craven cults on earth I am a monument!
With blood grown weak and cold and thin, and
spirit mean and small,
I bend, and creep, and toady in a Land of Smoodge
and Crawl!
To vanished deeds of bygone days and kings of
other lands
I turn my sham-adoring gaze and clasp my pallid
hands;
I halt, salute, I turn aside, I shamble, and I bow
Before old glories dead and gone that cannot
matter now.
Let others risk a man’s disgrace when Freedom’s
voice shall call—
But I won’t dare to show my face—l’ll merely
cringe and crawl !
My prided crest shall be a worm—a symbol of the
way
That I must creep, and twist, and squirm, and
toady day by day !
For I must bow to High Degrees, and condescend
to Fat—
Must fall upon my humble knees, and meekly
lift my hat.
Across the sheets of history I’ll leave my languid
scrawl—
A fearsome thing for folk to see when men no
longer crawl.
No great and noble dreams have I no hope, nor
strong man’s wish
Save but to shout “Gorsave !—Banzai! ” and
“ Ho, for Stinking Fish ! ”
I see the striving people trudge upon their
thriving way ;
I see them—and I slouch in sludge, I slobber, and
I pray !
Through all the land I glibly rant, and most
abjectly sprawl,
Expounding well the creed of Cant, of Cringe,
and Cadge, and Crawl.
We’ve heroes here who one by one have done the
grandest things
That I have dreamed were only done by God’s
Titanic kings.
We’ve men I never dote upon who put the Fates
to rout,
Whose creed was ever “ On and On ” —and always
“ Further Out!”
The daring men of plain address who swung the
axe and maul.
Who cleared the howling wilderness—and gave us
room to crawl!
I know that I should make sublime this land of
brighter scenes
In place of wasting golden time with idle kings
and queens;
I know that I should lift my voice in praise of
this, my land.
And teach our children to rejoice in patriotism
grand;
But then I have no time to think, but only time to
bawl —
To croak and cavil, sneer and slink—to kneel, and
smoodge, and crawl!
I am a speck to be ignored, a base and servile
thing;
I pay my homage to a lord, and fawn before a
king.
I have no independence worth a fraction of the
name—
/ harp upon my humble birth, and glory in my
shame !
With blood long grown a sickly hue and spirit mean
and small,
I crouch, and creep, and whimper thro ’ a Land of
Smoodge and Crawl!
N.S.W.

Meanwhile Senator Guthrie, of the Federated
(Australian) Seamen’s Union, had instructed a
lawyer (Mr. J. Woolf) to act for the unhappy
seamen in their legal fight for liberty, and
for the next few days Woolf was compelled
to fight tooth and nail. The captain brought the
crew before Magistrate Panton at Melbourne
City Court, on the charge of being absent without
leave, and the men repeated the story which had
been declared a good and true story by the
Queenscliff bench. But Panton, P.M., chose to
treat it as “a trumped-up farce,” and, after lec-
turing the defendants on the beauties of discipline
and the virtues of obedience, asked them if they
would return to the hated vessel, like good, bid-
dable tars. One and all refused. A sum total of
£2OO was owing to them, yet they were prepared
to sacrifice their pay rather than go back to earn
some more under the same conditions. Then the
wrathful Panton, in his astounding eccentricity,
divided the men into two classes of culprits, sen-
tencing three of them to 10 weeks’ imprisonment
each, and six others to a month only in gaol. The
tenth culprit was a stewardess, and because she
was a woman, bound by the same agreement as
the men, the autocrat ordered her to be discharged ;
she had done the same as the men, but Panton
hadn’t the courage and the logic to put her in
gaol. Woolf applied for a stay of proceedings.
The application was refused. Next day the captain
of the Melville Island instructed his solicitors to
apply to the same old Panton for an order direct-
ing the imprisoned seamen to be transferred from
their dungeons to the deck of the vessel—at
Queenscliff. Panton, with a fine disregard for
the fact that he had awarded 10 weeks’ imprison-
ment to some of them, and four weeks to the rest,
promptly ordered the lot of them back to duty.
Lawyer Woolf protested that the men were
now" claiming their absolute right of appeal
against the imprisonment decision oi the pre-
vious day, and were bound to win on the
appeal if it were heard, but it couldn’t be
heard if they were forcibly put back on
the ship, and carried away to sea. To this
objection Panton had practically nothing to say
except that he was going to grant the order for
the men’s removal, as “it would give the porta
bad name if the captain could not get the vessel
away as he desired.” In short, Panton desired
just what the captain desired—to get the men
away to sea ere the appeal could be heard. The
men were hustled off to Queenscliff on the precious
magisterial order, and there was no time to be lost
if they were to be saved from exportation. In the
District Court next morning Mr. Woolf applied
to Mr Dwyer, P.M., for an order to release the
unwilling crew of the Melville Island from cus-
tody, on the ground that notice of appeal had
been lodged, and that under the Justices Act of
1890 a Court of Petty Sessions must release from
custody any appellant who can give sureties.
The Act is explicit on this point, but Dwyer,
P.M., ranged himself on the side of Panton,
P.M., and refused to do the thing he ought
to have done in opposition to the thing
that brother Panton ought not to have
done. Finally the persistent WoOLF rushed
round to the Supreme Court, and instructed
counsel to tackle Justice a’Beckett on the burn-
ing question as to whether nine men could be
sentenced to terms of imprisonment one day,
ordered off to the custody of a ship’s captain
next day, and deported from Australia the fol-
lowing day, notwithstanding that the first sen-
tence was subject to review by a higher court.
Justice a’ Beckett granted an order to review the
Panton proceedings, which means that the
Supreme Court ordered the seamen to be released
from captivity on the boat until the peculiarities
of Panton and the Merchant Shipping Act have
been judicially considered.
A Sydney daily paper recently published an
interview with Labor-leader Watson, and if the
interview is correct (as Watson hasn’t made any
denials, it seems to be), it is a most dispiriting
document. It sets forth that Watson has got the
fiscal peace idea so badly that he doesn’t want
anything serious done to the tariff this session ;
and that he has got some other lingering com-
plaint in such an aggravated form that he doesn’t
want to do anything with the Federal graduated
land-tax this session either. At that rate what
is to be done this session ? Australia’s biggest
want is men —good, prosperous white men, and lots
of them. It wants them to make the country
strong for defence ; it wants them also because it
requires lots more taxpayers, and heaps more
revenue, so that it can build its two transconti-
nental railways, and open up the Northern Terri-
tory for settlement, and pay off the public debt,
and Federalise the railways and make them of
one uniform gauge, and build new railways ; and
it needs them so that it can have the money with
which to establish old age pensions, and create
a serviceable navy, and do heaps of other things.
For all these, the first essential is more people-
more prosperous taxpayers. The only way to get
them is to find work for artisans by means of a
Protective tariff that will create industries, and to
find land for agriculturists by breaking up the big
estates. And J. C. Watson, it seems, wants to
postpone both these things for at least a year —
possibly longer. The other day, little Motor-car
Kelly cackled foolishly on a public platform that
by reason of the Brito-Jap treaty Australia
was safe for the next ten years. Well, even if
that were true, which it probably isn’t, ten years is
not long ; and after the election we shall only be
safe for nine years ; and by the time next Parlia-
ment meets, and has a good look at the tariff and
the proposed land-tax, we shall only be safe for
about eight years, and still everything will be wait-
ing to be done. It is time Australian leaders
left off having these lingering political diseases.

Melbourne Age lately dug out an amazing stench
in the Victorian Lands Department. When the
north-west country was opened up it was under-
stood that there at least the small farmer was to
get a chance. All the rich south-west was in the
hands of the big grabber, but in the dry north-
west the small man was to get his bit of
desert undisturbed. And the Age discovers
that the Murray frontages—2,39l,3l7 acres
of them have been let out to 12 people
or institutions for a total rent of £1655 a
year, or about half-a-farthingper acre per annum.
The 12 holders quoted are not all large men;
there are six enormous grabbers with from 227,000
to 686,000 acres each ; three moderate grabbers
with from 28,800 to 95,180 acres each ; and three
small men. And there is a marvellous difference
in the rents charged to the big men and
the small ones. The smallest man has 1900
acres, and pays the comparatively steep rent of
£47 10s. per annum. The biggest man has about
350 times this area for just about ten times the
rent. For a Government which simply yearns to
settle the people on the land the letting of 686,000
acres to one man for £502 10s. per annum is a
auaint proceeding, and smells like a whole avenue
of dead whales.
Socialism in Victoria is being dragged into a
malodorous association with Wren, the tote-
shopkeeper. Wren poses as the true friend of
the working man all the time that he is spreading
snares for the working man’s wages. He talks of
giving Hope to his tote investors and club punters,
although his business is founded on the fact that
the less hope they have the better for Wren. And,
now that the police are again making some show of
an endeavor to suppress When’s pestilent silver
hells, it appears in evidence that his “City Tat-
tersall’s Club” was on one occasion only devoted
to some other purpose than the laying of
wagers. On December 23 a lecture on Socialism
was delivered at the club bv Senator Dawson.
The cause of Socialism cries to Heaven for
protection against friends so undesirable as
Wren. Admitting that a moral right to gamble
on horse races is claimed by the needy in common
with the punters who can afford to lose—a claim
which is not admitted by many of the most
liberal reasoners —it must also be admitted that
Wren and his ilk are sweaters of the poor and
rich alike. They live by making the mass of their
customers poorer. As parasites, they are the
natural enemies of Socialism. Wren's donations
to the funds of Labor organisations are merely
small refunds of money ho has drawn from
the workers, and a lecture on Socialism, delivered
by an unwary Senator, is as utterly out of place
in Wren’s club as a temperance sermon in a
brewery.

State Premier Carruthers alleges that things
were so good last year in N.S.W. that less than
£500,000 of the interest bill had to be made good
out of taxation, the revenue from public works
covering the rest, and this despite the fact that
the railway freights charged to farmers are not
one quarter so high as in England. In regard to
which The Bulletin only desires to say three
things :
(L) Even £500,000 is a pretty big deficit.
(2) It, is a disgrace that the people in general should be
taxed to the extent of £500,000 a year to keep the fanner
afloat, and that the manufacturer, in addition to paying
his share of the £500,000, should pay most exorbitant
railway charges to prevent the deficit being larger even
than it is, and then that they should have the farmer
thrown up at them as the line, upstanding, independent
“backbone of the country,” the noble “primary pro-
ducer,” and the embodiment of the “ magnificent natural
resources” of Australia.
(3) if things have improved so much as Joe Carrutiiers
says they have—if the deficit on public works has gone
down so much while the general revenue has increased
so largely—then Joe’s continued borrowing is a public
infamy.

Not long ago, when the Labor party formed a
Federal Ministry it took in Higgins, M.H.R. —
—though he was not a pledged Laborist —as its
Attorney-General. Higgins was a good enough
Democrat to rank with Watson, Hughes, and
Batchelor, and he is just as good to-day. Yet
Higgins is to be opposed by a Labor candidate
next Federal election. The Collingwood branch
of the P.L.C., in trying to justify the attempt to
drive this most conscientious Democrat out of
politics, used some curious logic: “Mr. Higgins
is said to be a cultured man, so also are some of
the biggest rascals. ’ Furthermore: “No reliance
or security can be found in educational ability.”
This reads like supreme foolishness. No accusa-
tion can be brought against Higgins in his capa-
city as a representative ; he has been absolutely
true to the Democratic ideal, and does not ask for
trust because of his “educational ability,” but on
the strength of past service and present assur-
ances. Ignorant laborers have been rascals
as well as men of culture, and no re-
liance or security can be found in illiteracy.
Yet the reasons put forth for trying to unseat
Higgins would seem to imply that the only
objection to him is that he isn’t ignorant enough.
He has done nothing to arouse suspicion, but he
“ is said to be a cultured man,” and that in itself
is suspicious. A little of Higgins’ “educational
ability,” or a little more ability of some kind,
might do the P.L.C. no harm. It might at least
teach that body that it is sheer foolishness to
sacrifice seats to its enemies by wantonly fighting
its friends. If Labor can’t associate with Higgins
it should have remembered the fact before taking
him into its Ministry when it was desperately
hard-up for an Attorney-General.

FRANCES ALDA, Australian, now accepted in
the front rank of the world's singers.
Photo. Talma, Sydney.
Messager’s. new comic opera, produced
for the first time this end of the world at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night, has a good
deal of “ Girofle-Girofia ” about the story. As for
the music, with those quaint orchestral effects
which made “ Veronique ” so conspicuous a suc-
cess, we have the former level pretty well sus-
tained though certainly not bettered, and it leaves
one doubting whether Messager has any great
stores to draw upon. The beginning is excellent;
but the value tapers off, especially in the last
of the three acts. The lyrics are bright—better
and richer in quip than they were in the piece
which whetted public appetite for this one; the
il atmosphere ” is almost as varied as could be got
into three acts, and the stage-dressing is entirely
satisfactory. ...
The story of “The Little Michus” is
placed in the days of the Revolution, some 100
years ao-o, when, it appears, fathers were just as
forgetful as they are to-day. Anyhow, having
handed over his motherless daughter to farmer
Michu, where she got mixed with Michu’s
own daughter, Marquis Des Ifs overlooked her for
the next 16 or 18 years, and then (the two girls
believing they were twin daughters of Michu), he
suddenly remembered her and promised her in
marriage to Gaptain Rigaud, his aide-de-camp
and saviour at the taking of Saragossa. Such a
peppery old person is General Des Ifs, the Mar-
auis of the old days, that Michu pere concludes
that, whatever he took Saragossa for, it hadn t
cured him. Meantime Michu moves up to town
and goes into the produce line; and Marie
Blanche, who isn’t the real Des Ifs, but nearly has
to marry the aide-de-camp all the same, is left
in the end happier in serving out pounds (more or
less) of butter, and waiting for Aristide, the shop-
assistant, to marry her and consolidate the business.
The dress circle will note with great outward
manifestations of joy (and, mostly, with anguish
in secret) that blood will tell. How could the
daughter of the old nobility, even if she had got
mixed up with somebody else in a baby s bath-
how could she take a subsequent interest in butter
and bacon ? And how could the real farmer’s
daughter sit squarely in the seat of the jnighty ?
The makers of ‘ ‘ The Little Michus' ’ did.
not know, perhaps, that Australia has its Lauri,
and asks for him early and often ; and so, as they
made no part to fit him, he has to take the nearest,
and make of it something of the sort of thing
that is always understood to be expected of
its first comedian. Des Ifs, therefore, be-
comes an ultra-comic-opera general, attended
by one Bagnolet (Fred. _ Leslie) who is
even more ultra. But much is forgiven for the
excellence of some of Leslie’s eccentric by-play,
and his singing (he has more than usual to do) is
much more tolerable than it is sometimes. Add
W. S. Percy, as the assistant who is dying of love
for one Michu, though he can’t tell which, and
there remains only one among the men, Reginald
Roberts, who had on Saturday a most enthusi-
astic welcome back after two years spent in the
land where sausages have become unpopular. His
acting hasn’t lost its stiffness; and, though his
songs are among the successes of the performance,
he doesn’t improve the best of them by an in-
artistic high show note, which it is pretty certain
Messager never wrote. Miss Florence Young and
Miss Margaret Thomas are the little Michus who
got mixed in the morning tub; and well, you
can hardly believe it now. A striking contrast
these two; and yet, duetting, their voices blend
as they have never blended before. Miss Young
is in rare spirit; nothing she has done since her
return to Australia has been so good.

Though “The Man from Mexico ” looks as
if it could put in an indefinite time in Sydney, the
run must soon cease, as the co. has arranged to
move on to Melbourne. A fresh song and dance,
“Tammanee,” in which Grace Palotta, Ward and
Willoughby take part, is just now giving a reason-
able excuse for many people to attend again, and,
take lessons in artistic domestic lying. “ Ihe Man
from Mexico ” is as daring aground and lofty liar
as farce has ever seen, and Mrs. Man from Mexico
has a credulity that makes the mouth water.

Sydney Palace Theatre still cherishes the
Harris- Yeaiuaiis-Titus laugh-makers. Madame,
who seems to have a repertory of some 6000 songs
and imitations of songs as others sing them,
changed her tunes and gowns during the week,
and Mr. Harris’ sketch “All in the Day’s Work
shows what may happen to man born of woman.
The latter is born to trouble as the sparks fly up-
wards (and on to the roof of our neighbors fowl-
house), but it takes Harris to let the man know
how amusing are his worries and Ins work-
amusing, that is, to the other fellow. Jhe
H-Y-T combine will ripple along with fresh pro-
grammes every night until further orders.

“For England,” revived by Bland Holt
at Sydney Royal, on Saturday night, as a drama
falls something below the usually fiigti
standard of Holt productions. It deals
with the first Boer war, and its inci-
dents and characters are mostly ob-
viously unreal, and wholly wooden
the Boers, for instance, being repre-
sented as carrying on warfare on lines
which would make even Lord Kit-
chener blush. But it has some com-
pensating features, of which the chief
is Bland Holt as Manasses Marks, a
Jew bill-discounter, who is as soft-
hearted as he is hard-headed. Iliis
Hebrew, allowing for the broad touches
needed for melodrama, is a very human
character, and Bland Holt gives him a
really humorous aspect. His appear-
ance in the first place on a low-comedy
frame-work of a horse which is unjustly
keeping the Botany Bay ducks or an
American sausage factory out of meat,
gives Manasses a good start, and from
that on he is the strongest feature of
the play. The rest of its score, “For
England’’ must make out of an ex-
citing story and well-staged situations.
Miss Ross and Walter Baker rather
overdo the boy-and-girl love business
in the first act, and Albert Norman
fails to give any individuality to his
villain. It’s a failure that excites sym-
pathy. In the course of a year Norman
has to be about 20 villains;and in most
cases the villains have been machine-
made by the dramatist, and one couldn’t
be recognised from the other unless by
the name label. It is not easy to gi ve;a
fresh smile to one’s seduction and a new
frown to one’s threat under such condi-
tions. Several minor members of the
company give good pictures of a group
of Boer generals, including Kruger
and Joubert.
Miss Zaida Ben-Yusuf gave an eye-
rivetting entertainment, “Japan Through My
Camera,” at St. James’ Hall, Sydney, on
Thursday night. The color views thrown
on the screen pleased even those of the
audience who had travelled through the
country. And every fourth person one meets has
some personal (and nasal) acquaintance with the
land of card-board houses and its desperately poor
inhabitants. As foi?- the quality of Miss Ben-
Yusuf’s accompaniment of comment on the pic-
tures, her manager, Mr. Southwell, disarmed
criticism by saying (after the entertainment), It
wasn’t a lecture ; it was a conversational yarn.

W. J. MacDermott has a number of
amusing subjects for a new Bio. show now run-
ning at the Centenary Hall, Sydney. Most
of them are of a comic element, and others are of
a melo-dramatic tone of mind The Biograph
flickers less than most, and with W. E. Scott, who
is a clever ventriloquist and entertainer, the show
does something to dispel the air of gloom and Cold
Tea at the Centenary Hall.
* * *
Queen’s Hall, Sydney, now shelters a
company which styles itself vaudeville, and the
principal feature of which is Miss Cora Vane,
who sees weird things, and has second sight.
Second wind is a good thing in vaudeville, if you
have a dance turn, but second sight doesn t seem
very entertaining. Two lean girls, with very long
legs and very short socks, who sing and dance,
and a deaf and dumb man with curly hair who
does lightning sketches, are the other features of
the show, which attracted a splendid audience on
Saturday.

A streak of the variety that began to be
rather conspicuous by its absence has been painted
on the Tivoli programme by the return of the
Miles-Stavordale quintette, which gives a chance
in a dull season to revive all over again the heated
arguments as to whether they really and truly get
all their choral effects out of their unassisted
banjos.
THE LITTLE MICHUS.
The tale of a tub in the early morn,
With the sun aglint on the ripening- corn,
In the summering fields of the land of France—
The tale of a tub and a sad mischance :
She had served her lord had Madame Micliu
As ladies of France still sometimes do ;
And Des Ifs’ queen, having proved her worth,
Surprised, had died at her daughter’s birth.
Des Ifs, hard pressed by the cares of State,
Carried the child to the Michus’ gate.
Madame Michu she lay in bed,
Sleeping the sleep of the Fat and Fed ;
And the well-trained Michu, to he of use,
Gave the babies their morning sluice.
Hut alas land likewise alack-a-day !)
When Michu had taken their nighties away,
And they squirmed in the tub with never a stitch,
There wasn’t a soul knew t’other from which.
So Marie Blanche and Blanche Marie
Blossomed as twins on one family tree.
Now, it’s all very well when Spring begins
For blossoms to bloom like Siamese twins ;
Hut the petals will heed and, answering, fall
When they hear the warm noteof youngSummer’scatl.
Then hastens the season of fruitage apace,
And a lover awaits for one Blossom’s embrace.
Des Ifs, his medals all girded on,
To the war with the castles in Spain had gone ;
And we shouldn’t be writing this fugitive song
If he hadn’t been saved by his bold aidy-kong.
For he puzzled his head for a fit reward
For a tenor knight with a flashing sword ;
And being a pa with a frugal mind,
lie gave him the daughter he’d left behind,
lie, of course, didn’t know as you and we do
That they couldn’t tell one from the other Michu.
So they sent Marie Blanche,and there they chose wrong;
And to make matters worse for the bold aidy-kong,
Already in secret he loved Blanche Marie —
And she was the child of the old Mar-kee.
It looked for awhile as a matter of course
That later there’d be a small case in Divorce ;
But the playwright who gives good lovers their dues
Straightened all things for the little Michus.


Small boy,—little pool,
Oh joy,—no school,
Felt wet, —bad cold,
Home get,—mother scold.
Boy sick,—nearly dead,
Cure quick, doctor said,
Don’t wait, but secure
Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

“ The Dictator,” in its second week at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), is being played rather less
rapidly and with afore regard for local conditions
than on the first night. Allowances have been
made for the vastness of the theatre and the un-
familiar character of the subject matter. The
points that were brought out by William Collier
and co. in the quietest possible way, as though
the audience were sitting in wait to seize them as
they came along, are now being emphasised some-
what for the enlightenment of people to whom the
idea of a San Marana Republic in Central America
is new and extraordinary. The slower the tempo of
the playing the better it will be appreciated in a
general way, provided that Brooke Travers’ revo-
lution is duly purchased by ten minutes to eleven.
There are immense possibilities of probability in
B.T.’s revolution. He landed at Porto Banos
with 25,000 dollars (the result of backing a double)
and a false name. The military forces of the
country are willing to upset any local Govern-
ment for half the money, and the young man
in charge of the wireless telegraph operations
is clearly the right sort of casual insurrectionist
to lead the disaffected soldiery to the nearest
drinking saloon and bid them charge their glasses
with the deadly cocktail in honor of the Dictator,
from New York. But the young man in question
(Barrymore) is too casual in his utterances. The
original fact of his being a wireless telegraphist is
the key-note to the climacteric episode, and he
doesn’t strike it with sufficient force. He needs to
prepare the audience for the joke which is looming
in the distance, so that everybody may see it the
moment it arrives. Brooke Travers’s bogus
message to Admiral Dewey is the quaintest
farcical comedy conceit this country has laughed
at, but the laughter thusfar has not been loud
enough for the occasion. All the same, _ “ The
Dictator ” is beginning to be quoted at its full
value on the public market, and the work of
Collier in particular is greatly admired.

doctor for four weeks, and the two or three
remaining weeks of the Brough-Fleinming Co. at

was in no way due to Little Mary, but it happens

TWO OF THEM.-“ The Wreck, Sir!"
Somewhere under the floor of Westminster
Abbey the body of a famous deceased actor must
be turning in its grave at the thought of how
“ the students of the Irving Dramatic College”
are playing up at Melbourne Bijou. The con-
spirators aforesaid, supported by Mr. Howard
Edie (in large type), began their terrible cam-
paign with the “Two Orphans,” and were to
follow the inaugural atrocity with two nights of
“ The Bells,” in which Edie undertook to display
himself “to the best advantage.” To-night
( L’hursdav) he can be witnessed as a suffering hero
in “ Proof,” after which he will lapse into comedy
and produce “School” for two final nights.
Edie and the Irving Dramatic Collegians
are dealing with unsuitable pieces in the
worst possible way, but at any other place
their mania for making objects of themselves
might be charitably ignored. Seeing that they
are doing it at the Bijou this commentator would
like to know whether the dilapidated theatre has
been provided with new exits, or in any way
altered since the Melbourne Board of Health
declared that certain changes were essential to
public safety. And if not, why not ? A panic
could easily happen at the Bijou whilst the pre-
sent co. is careering there. The performance
last Saturday night nearly incited the present
writer to shriek “Fire” in the tone of a captain
commanding his minions to shoot.
«# * *
Yasco, the Mad Musician, whose insanity
is expressed in the blowing of 20 different wind
instruments, blew into the Opera House last
Saturday. Vasco works hard. The quality of
his music is pretty good in some cases, but the
“turn” is long, and the toiler’s ideas of humor
are very crude, and if he didn’t roll on the floor
at the finish he would not get a hearty recall.
A short, stout person can always command liberal
applause for rolling on the floor. Mdlle. Alexandra
and Master Bertie are also fresh arrivals from
Europe. Their turn is aerial, gymnastic, and grace-
ful. In the course of the performance they shed a
number of superfluous garments, being ultimately
revealed as a beautiful figure of an Alexandra and
a fine lump of a boy. Master Bertie in fact
is an 18-year-old child with sufficient muscular
development to furnish a large family. Larola,
the Three Lees, and other people assist to keep
the Rickards ball rolling from 7.40 until 11 p.m.
The policy of starting the show earlier than
usual when it is going to last longer than usual is
a wise concession to the crowd that pays an extra
fee for an early seat.

Miss Titfcell Brune has d—d the indenture
and thrown the dinner out of the window for the
last time at Adelaide Royal, somewhat to the dis-
appointment of the public, who loved the improb-
able Haddon Hall play and rushed its every
performance. Matinee audiences particularly
revelled in it, and thrilled with apprehension
when the mistress and maid got hurriedly out of
all their upper garments in full view of the public.
Kingston was not too well suited to the part of the
hero, but Susie Vaughan, as the queenly spinster
of a coming-on disposition, was at her best.
After a brief excursion into “ Romeo and
Juliet,” the co. is now presenting “Leah
Kleschna,” beginning with crime and ending with
cabbages. It is a stirring if uncomfortable play,
and is finely acted. The real ice rink has
started football, which is far more dangerous
on skates than hockey, and is therefore much
more popular with spectators. But it is hard to
■believe that frozen football will ever become a
national game.
AT POVERTY POINT.
It looks as though the Leila Doubleday fund will reach
the £lOOO required for giving that interesting damsel five
years’ tuition in Europe. Prime Minister Dealiin, who
has musical daughters of his own, took the chair at a
Melb. Town Hall meeting, last week, when it was
announced that the recent concert and subsequent sub-
scriptions had put £4OO into the fund. Persons at the
meeting promised another £l3O in annual instalments,
and asub-committee undertook the job of shaking up local
public interest on the child’s behalf. Then comes the
question of taking Leila Doubleday to Sydney and Ade-
laide, for a concert in each city, where, of course, she
would need to be boomed ; and a combination of circum-
stances, quite apart from the merit of the entertainment,
is necessary to a boom.
A suburban manageress recently produced a drama
billed as “ The Fatal Wedding, or the Flower of the
Forest.” A crowd of bellowdrama-lovers turned up,
expecting to see a replica of the, Me.vnell and Gunn pro-
duction, but the only resemblance was in the title. The.
same lady announced once that Miss Eileen Castles
would sing at one of her entertainments. Strange
to say, when a good audience had been attracted by the
name, Miss Eileen’s indisposition had to be apologised
for. She had been struck with a severe pain in the front
waistband or something.
Melbourne Argus remarks that stage realism has never
been studied with greater effect than in the mystery
plays, which combined the religion and the amusement
of the early Christian. The chief actor in the Crucifixion
scene wore a leather wine-bag under his robe. The
Roman soldier’s lance was run into it, and “blood”
spurted out with thrilling results. The audience was not
satisfied with being told of marvels that had taken
place off the stage. Balaam’s ass had to speak its
lines within full view and hearing, of the gallery'.
St, Paul was decapitated on the stage, and bis head then
took three leaps, with a stream of blood at each. Adam
and Eve were brought upon the stage “ strictly in their
state of innocende ”in one scene, and left it to return
with the legendary fig-leaves in the following one. Also;
“ whenever the seventh commandment was to be broken
the actors disappeared behind a curtain which was hung
across a part of the stage.”
“Mr. Joseph Blascheck, Humorist” is an occasional
advt. in London Referee. This must be Joe Blascheck,
the lame young man, who left Melbourne many years ago
to try his luck as a reciter and comic songster.
He was a very fine old ac-tor —one of those splendidly-
matured actors of the vintage of 1870 —with a rich, rolling
voice and a majestic gait. Even after crossing a bar after
the longest 3d. beer known in the trade, his.carriage was
that of a Shakspearean king. Everybody round the
vicinity of the “ Point ” knew him as an ac-tor (with the
accent on the “tor”), but few had seen him act; and
now it was made known that he had been offered, and
had been graciously pleased to accept, an engagement in
a metropolitan theatre. There was to be a performance
of “ Hamlet,” and the good old ac-tor was placed as
second gravedigger. He was a bit swelled up about it in
the bar, and the billiard-marker said: “Yes; but why
do you noo play the part of Hamlet?” The veteran
regarded him gravely for a moment, and then in rich,
fruity tones he replied : “ That point was considered, my
man. That point received profound consideration. But,
sir, if I were to play Hamlet who would play the second
gravedigger—that is the problem ; who would play the
second gravedigger ? ”

with the football team, wrote a long yarn
to the dramatic critic of one of the local

scornful.
“ Num ” : Somebody writing to Tiie Bulletin (26/4/’O6)
denounces the Vic. competition-mongers for their lack of
patriotism in sending to Hingland for judges. There are
two sides to the question. It is permissible to send away
for anything that cannot be obtained in Australia, and
there is a widespread opinion among competition people
that judges cannot. 1 know a little about the competi-
tions and their morals, though I have never recited
“ Curfew shall not ” before a mixed audience, or started
for the baritone solo. The truth —the great unpublished
truth—is that the morals of “ literary and musical
competitions” are about ten shades lower than
those of the pony course and the cycle track.
One of these days I’m going to send you along a column
on the inner workings of competitions, that will make
your hair rise. How many of the outside public ever
dream of accompanists running “ dead” for certain com-
petitors and cruelling the songs of others? Who ever
heard of hocussing a piano in order to knock a dangerous
rival out of the piano solo? These are things which have
been done, though they occur on rare occasions. What
is always there, is the suspicion of unfair judging. Mind,
I think that, in the majority of instances, the
judges are as square as could be wished, and
would give fair play to the pupils of all teachers
irrespectively. But there are one or two against
whom suspicion appears well-founded, and this leaven
leads to every unsuccessful competitor swearing that the
judges run crooked and that Tremolo gives Andante’s
pupils the prizes at Wung Wung conditionally on Andante
giving Tremolo’s pupils the prizes at Slimy Flat. The
imported judge knows nobody and the competitor,
assured of his fairness, has to fall back on attacking his
competence, which is a small thing seeing that every-
body is more or less incompetent except the competitor.
[The imported judge from England probably “knows
nobody ” when he arrives, which would be the same with
a judge imported from N.S.W., Maoriland or S.A. But
how long will it be before he knows somebody? And how
long will it be before he knows everybody?—Ed. B.]

“Melb.”: Musing over Coppin, I strolled last night up
to the Queen’s Theatre, opened 1845. Faithfully built,
There it stands intact. Fires have swept others, but did
not touch this. The front remains the same, and the
rear part has no support from any other edifice. Here,
in front, are the same old doors—a big one in the centre
for the pit, a smaller one on each side of it respectively
for drtss circle and gallery. There were only those places,
and no stalls. How players nowadays would relish it if
thev bad a pit right up to the footlights. The sight of
the Queen’s makes vou think of all vou have read about
Coppin, Brooke, Jacky Tommy Smith, Nesbitt, Morton
King, the Heirs, Charley Young, Rogers, and so on. And
then you go round to the old stage door.
It was during the Maoriland tour of a popular co. that
the glorified leading man, in a burst of righteous indigna-
tion, laid the stage manager low at his feet with a straight
stoush from the shoulder. The blow was administered in
the interests of Art. On several previous occasions the
actor had merely protested against the manager’s neglect
of duty as regards stage fixings, but on this occasion the
setting of the first scene was so incomplete that the actor,
having inspected the state of affairs before the curtain
rose, sent for the supercilious delinquent and knocked
him dowm. The fallen one took his punishment like a
sheep, and the co., with one exception, rejoiced ex-
ceediindy
At Melbourne Rojal, the other Saturday night, an
excited gallery boy marked Ms respect for-the Ander-
soriian melodrama by shouting notes of warning to the
hero when Bert Bailey was sneaking his purse. This sort
of thing, which rarely happens in an Australian theatre,
is common enough at the Cockney houses where “ blood
and thunder” drama is associated with bread and cheese
and beer between the acts. Some.of the intensely robus-
tious actors, playing year after year on the same stage,
become endeared to a large number of persistent visitors,
No manager would venture to let one of these old-estab-
lished heroes be murdered by his mortal foe, the tradi-
tional villain. The gallery wouldn’t stand that. A
heroiiie, who never gets so strong a hold on the affections
of the gods, may be killed occasionally, but the lead-
ing “glory” man must always make a happy
recovery from his worst wounds. When he fights for his
life, he is bombarded with hints and exhortations. The
boys know him only by his professional name of Belvidere,
or what not, and they address him accordingly. “ Take
care, Belvy, there’s a bloke hidin’ behind the tree!”
“ Look out for the cove in the brown’at, Belvy.” “Mind
his legs, Belvy, or he’ll put you on your back,” and so
forth. And when Belvy comes before the curtain to re-
ceive the audience’s congratulations on bis narrow escape
he puts his hand to his heart and smiles gratefully. When
his annual benefit arrives, he makes a speech, thanking-
his friends for their invariable sympathy, as though he had
needed all the advice they could give him in his desperate
for the tabernacular effect of the interior. It is as though
the people who go to skate were intended to remain to
pray. The shape of the doorways, the stained glass, the
square vestibule leading to the inner doors—these things
inspire a feeling of holy calm, which will probably dis-
appear when the rink is iced, and the skating begins,
On entering the Glaciarium one is struck by the
churchy' scheme of seating accommodation. The gallery
to the left and benches on the raised floor to the right
suggest that the place could be easily converted
to Salvation purposes. In the meantime it brings ice-
skating into vogue in Melbourne. The rink will accom-
modateabigerow-d, and unless the casual novices are
“frozen off’* by the action of select cliques who want to
run the show and give performances on their own, the
Glaciarium will be a fairly democratic exercise ground,
like the frozen waters at the “ Welsh Harp,” a cockney
resort where a charge is made for admission.
According to American mummer Lawrence Barrett,
“ the actor is a sculptor who carves his image in snow.’
But so many carve with a jagged meat-axe that, on the
whole, it is fortunate the material is not more lasting. In
some cases snow is too durable. Why not try soup?

Grant Hervey writes :
The Victorian P.L.C. will make a blunder if it nomi-
nates Stephen Barker for the Senate. Barker is an im-
possible old woman, who, if elected, would be on a par
with the ridiculous Simian Phraser. It is a tragedy and
a joke to see S. B. gyrating around himself at meetings in
the Trades Hall. If information is wanted about any
official matter, Stephen falls over fifteen large volumes in
pursuit of the necessary data. The delegates sit back
with a tired expression, whilst Barker remarks that he
thought it was in the BXI book, but that it isn’t there.
He then grabs the Q2K opus, and makes a frenzied
journey through its pages. Irreverent young dele-
gates 'make rude suggestions about fetching their
blanket, but Steve continues to bound over the office
literature with great velocity. Having traversed the
DSP, Zsi, and SG47 volumes without success, Mr. Barker
mops his face and makes a rush at another pile of
ledgers. About this point the chairman announces that
the meeting w ill struggle through without the desired
figures. This sort of thing occurs continually ; and writer
opines that the chief reason for the selection of Stephen
Barker, J.P., arises out of an intense and general desire to
get him out of the road. Which would he all very well
for the Trades Hall, but a mighty poor sort of bargain
for the Commonwealth.
The other candidates selected, Solly (if he stands) and
Tunnecliffe, are men of larger calibre. But Solly, who
does excellent work in Richmond Council, has done
nothing in the Victorian Assembly to justify lifting him
into the Senate. There is an immense difference between
municipal and legislative work ; and Solly, earnest and
indefatigable as he is, is better suited for the former.
The Vic. Assembly itself is only a glorified Shire Council,
and if Solly is wise he will sit tight. Tunnecliffe is the
best of the bunch. His pamphlet, “ The Problem of
Poverty,” published by the Tocsin, is the W'ork of a man
of brains. Unlike the other two, he has that capacity
for getting to the root of things which distinguishes the
serious reformer from the mere tub-thumper. In the
appendix of his booklet Tunnecliffe demonstrates how a
loan of £4,600,000 entailed, in interest, &c., a final repay-
ment of £11,180,537, or nearly 2j times the amount re-
ceived. Men with such scientific minds are very valu-
able ; and —on the question of finance alone—Tunnecliffe
deserves the vote of every Victorian Radical. The whole
question anent the Trades Hall selections boils down to
this : Are the three men chosen the very best Democrats
who can be found in Victoria? Tunnecliffe passes, but
the others don’t. If the P.L.C. has any diplomacy it will
play its cards so as to get the Age support behind it.
Senator Styles has done good work for Democracy ; and,
if the Labor executive supports him and picks another
solid, brainy man besides Tunnecliffe, the Syme influence
ought to be strong enough to return the three. Splitting
of parties will, as usual, result in victory for the invoice-
faking, butter-rebating push.
But deliver us from the futility of Stephen Barker, O
Lord, no matter what happens.
,Willie-motor Kelly, M.H.R., recently
perorated in the Stinking Fish interest
Plow could we keep Australia for the Australians if we
could not keep the empire for Australia, and how could
we keep the empire for Australia if we deliberately set
ourselves to shut out our kindred from oversea from all
participation in our progress ? (Applause.)
The next time Willie pirouettes on the platform,
will he kindly explain what in thunder “ we keep
the empire for Australia” means ? And he might
explain who have “ deliberately set themselves to
shut out our kindred from oversea.” Considering
that Australia has deliberately set itself to spend
money on bringing in its kindred from oversea,
Willie seems in this case even a worse Fish than
usual. The Bulletin often wonders if it ever
occurs to Willie that it is a low-down thing to
utter gratuitous, baseless and wanton slanders on
a country which has at least supplied him with the
means of living without toil, as an affluent and
unproductive person in a motor-car.

Yarious correspondents of Melbourne Age
are running a sort of unintentional competition as
to which locality can boast the slowest train under
Tait’s management. Wangaratta to Beechworth
offers 2G miles in 2hrs. 14min.; Korumburra to
Alberton gives G3£ miles in shrs, ; Fish Creek to
Foster, 7|f miles in 55 minutes. These are all
schedule times, but the travelling record seems to
be held by the Melbourne to Lilydale goods, mail
and paper train, which leaves town at 4.20 a.m.,
and seldom arrives before 8.45—24 miles in 4hrs.
25 minutes ! By the way, Tait’s amazing time-
table gives the distance between Gelliondale and
Alberton as 2f miles on the “down” line, and
3£ miles on the “up.” Heaven and Tait only
know how the figures are arrived at.
An old Bulletin contributor looks in upon
Zion—located for the moment in the Hibernian
Hall, Melbourne:—
On the platform was Elder McCullagh, a good-looking
young man with a square jaw and a thin, lipless mouth.
His present business was to denounce the erstwhile Profit
and heave his whiskers out of Zion. The purple Prophet
was denounced, derided and demolished. But not in the
eyes of all. One stood faithful to the Beard—a mild little
man, Deacon Smith, who reckoned it would be a fair
thing to learn what defence the Beard of Dowie had to
offer. McCullagh turned on him fiercely. But such of
the public as had wandered in insisted on Smith getting
fair play, so McCullagh postponed the deacon until the
following night.
There was a crowded house on the Monday. McCullagh
had studied in a good school, and put in a long hour cas-
tigating Dowie some more, and getting his followers
worked up. Then he asked Deacon Smith up on to the
platform. Deacon Smith went. The elder pleasantly
asked if anybody wanted to hear Smith. One
rash individual stood up, but had hardly got straight
before McCullagh fell on him. Zion was warm and willing,
and after upsetting a dozen chairs, tearing a few collars,
and assaulting some of Zion’s countenances, Smith’s par-
tisan was robustly hustled down the steps into Swanston-
street. Then mild little Deacon Smith commenced to
defend his character from a charge of advocating poly-
gamy, whereupon M’Cullagh ruled that that was a mere
secondary matter. While these two argued at one
another, Zion and the cynical spectators were either
praying or jerking texts and offering irrelevant re-
marks. One politically-inclined youth who wished to
know, “What about a'larid-tax?” was promptly rushed
out in a dismantled state. Just here Smith was heard
observing through the din that “ Voliva was a true
Christian.” A man at the back of the hall ventured on a
pious “ Hear, hear ; God bless Voliva ” ; whereupon
M’Cullagh pointed an accusing finger at him, saying,
“That man is living with another man’s wife.” That
touched the powder. The whole hall rose to its feet,
while through the moving mass, past shrieking women and
tossing chairs, the interjector strove to force his way to
where McCullagh was vainly mouthing objurgations, and
little Deacon Smith was assuring the atmosphere that Moses
was a bigamist but not a polygamist. Zion at length
prevailed, and another dismantled but intensely angry
Christian was bundled down the steps to join the previous
two. After that episode M’Cullagh was boss, and little
Smith was deposed and heaved out. Then Zion sang a
hymn, and went home.
Next night the public turned up in force, but McCullagh
followed his deposed boss’s old tactics, and admitted
nobody except by ticket, and inside all was peace. Even
little Deacon Smith stayed away. Nobody assaulted any-
body, and the only suggestive remark was an intimation
by McCullagh that “ there was a general desire ” that he
should take charge of “ the work ” in the Commonwealth,
and that he proposed sending a power of attorney to
America to recover at law the £l2,ooosent from Australia
to Zion City. This writer remembers Voliva, and his
remarks when he gets that power of attorney should be
interesting.
• • • •
The most humorous thing in the general
denunciation of Dowie, the Flying Elijah with
the purple wings vvho lately struck the earth very
hard, is that he is charged, not only with the
smash of the ten Commandments, but with mak-
ing “ blasphemous claims to Divine authority,”
and is found guilty on all counts. Now the whole
fabric of the Cursing Church was built oh Dowie’s
divine authority—the authority which God was
supposed to have handed down to the Boss Curser.
If there is no such authority, then Zion hasn’t a
leg to stand on, and might as well dissolve. But
Zion, after not only rejecting, but denouncing,
the only reason for its existence, proposes to go
on just as before. It doesn’t seem to know that
it has found itself lying dead on the clerical high-
way, and that the only thing for it now is burial.
It is a roof which has repudiated the walls and
the foundation, and still reckons it can go on being
a roof in the old position. The dullness of Zion
in failing to notice the point is something beyond
the average.

“Stony” : It was nine o’clock at night,
and Melbourne was quiet. In one place there
was a small crowd which overflowed the sidewalk
opposite a cloth-
ing store and
spread out over
the road-blocks.
It was a pecu-
liar crowd. The
people stood
stock still as
though stricken
stiff in their
tracks. They all
stood and stared.
Others came up
and stopped,
stricken, and
stared also. And
still more. Then
I went to find
out what the
matter was, and
under the arc-
lights of the
store I saw two
men gazing into a
window. The win-
I dow contained a Jap-
anese window - curtain
and a feather-duster, also
a placard in Japanese. The
two men were Japs—one with
a little tin sword sticking out at his hip, and the
other with no little tin sword at all. They pondered
silently at the placard and at the window-curtain
and the feather-duster ; and the crowd pondered
at the two men and at the placard and the
window-curtain and the feather-duster I passed
on when I had had a good, hungry look, but as I
turned the corner I glanced back, and the two Japs
were still standing there staring, and the crowd,
with its jaw hanging loose, still stood, staring at
the Japs and the other things. I went by the
place next morning, but the crowd had melted
away in the night, and all that remained was the
placard and the duster and widow-curtain.

A remittance man in Maoriland put in a
busy week recently. In the morning lie got
drunk. In the afternoon he got drunker. In the
evening he took a large quantity of poison. He
was pumped out during the night and spent the
next three days in hospital. Case was set down
as one of accident. On leaving hospital he had
some drinks and went to dine with the doctor.
Produced two razors and said lie was going to cut
his throat, and asked for a lordly dish to spill the
blood into. Was hustled off to police station.
Hustled thence to lunatic asylum. Discharged
after two days. Home again and having more
drinks. Time six days, four hours.
CLEANING UP.
REMITTANOR MAN : “ Ah, if you would only marry me, my past would all be wiped out."
AUSTRALIAN GORL: “ Very likely; only I object to being made a mop of."
An Echuca (Vic.) butter-factory employee
was charged, the other day, with stealing 21b. of
cow-fat. The prosecutor admitted that, though
accused had been 15 years in his employment and
toiled from 15 to 17 hours a day for six days a
week and often on Sundays, he only paid him 245.
a week. He explained that he didn’t pay him
more because “ he felt that if he gave the man a
higher wage he would waste it.” The bench of
dishonoraries said “the evidence clearly showed
that defendant was not getting a living wage ”
—and fined him £l, on the principle, no doubt,
that, not having enough to live on, lie will be able
to thrive on £1 less. The butter-factory wasn’t
fined anything. On the contrary, it will no doubt
be made president of a Deform and Anti-Socialist
League. Perhaps it may even be made a J.P.
* * * »
Sydney dentist profession has been rent
asunder by a recent trial before the Dental Board
of a toothsmith who, having just graduated with
the local degree of B.D.S. (Bachelor of Dental
Surgery), burst on the world with a big brass
plate labelled Dr. Dragger, or whatever the name
was. Dragger found himself charged with “in-
famous professional conduct,” but was held not
guilty. Still, the Board warned Dragger to take
down the sign with “Dr.” on it. The defence
was that Bachelors of Medicine are styled “Dr.,”
and a Bachelor of Teeth has as good a claim, but
the Board of Molars refused to listen. A more
serious argument is that the recently-formed
School of Dentistry at Sydney University makes
no provision for the granting of the degree
D.D.S., while the American Dental Schools, with
a similar curriculum and identical methods of
examination, turn out graduates with a doctor’s
degree. So the Yankee dentist comes here and
calls himself “Dr. Hauler,” while the equally
qualified Australian is just “ Mr. Dragger.” This
is to the detriment of the locally-trained man,
and if the Tooth Board insists on the profession
dropping the “ Dr.” until it graduates as D.D.S.,
the sooner the University gives it a chance to
become D.D.S. the better.
* * * *
N.S.W. Full Court is being taught to take
a clearer view of the law by the High Court, and
the other day (14/5/’0(i) squashed with indignation
a police court point that a conviction was illegal
because there was no evidence before the Glen
Innes Court that it was the nearest court to a
Glen Innes pub. Now an unkind critic digs out
from the musty tomes of the past these decisions
of N.S.W. Full Court:—
1888. (Chief Justice Darley and Justice Stephen.)
We cannot take judicial cognisance of the limits of the
Petty Sessions districts. There is nothing in the affidavit
to show that George-street, Sydney, is not within Cooma
district.
1899. (Justice Stephen, Justice Owen, and Justice
Cohen.)
The Court will not take judicial cognisance of the fact
that Bourke is more than 150 miles from Sydney.
Justice Stephen : The Court does not know that
Bourke is 150 miles from Sydney. I will not take judicial
notice of the fact that Bourke is more than 150 miles
from Sydney. I know, of course, it is ; everybody knows
it.
1904. (Justice Stephen, Justice Simpson, and Justice
Pring.)
It is necessary to prove that the tenements in question
are within the" Petty Sessions district of the court in
which the case is heard. Jurisdiction is not to be in-
ferred or implied with respect to inferior courts.
It is well that wisdom comes to Judges, even if it
comes late in life and is only forced on them by
the Federal High Court.

There is a “ Royal Society of St. George ”
at Launceston (Tas.), but most of the members
are Australian natives. At the recent annual
meeting the society had a credit balance of 2s. 5d.,
and a bank manager moved that a certain mining
potentate be treasurer. The president (born in
Launceston) said he didn’t know how the dickens
the Scotch and Irish worked up enthusiasm for
their societies, and the secretary (an Anglo-
Yankee) said he had worn the boots off his feet
searching for half-crown subscriptions and not
finding them.

TTfEALTHY White will Adopt and Educate Colored
’’ Child. Send parties., , Herald Office.
There are plenty of white children, both here and
in England, who want badly enough to be
adopted and educated, but the person who
gets the nigger craving must have his nigger.
There is a sort of satisfaction, however, in
knowing that the craving is one that goes
back on its degenerate owner in after years.
The white adopted son or daughter can be
a consolation in its adopted parents’ old age.
The coon child may be a qauint curiosity when
young, but when it grows up to be a hulking
black apparition like a coal-heaver or a sweep it
isn’t a graceful prop for anybody’s later years.

Bishop Clarke, of Melbourne, who lately
added an arch to his title, is now bestirring him-
self in the matter of amicably settling points of
difference between “High” and “Low” church
in the Anglican denomination. His gaitered Nibs
seems to have the support of all cynical critics,
Church of England “ religion, ” such as it is, has
been aptly styled the religion of a gentleman, in its
distinction from the vulgah “ isms. ” Andagentle-
man wishes to preserve his soul from worry and
excitement. Points of difference are doosid bad
form, and if the Bishop can make things not too
high and not too low, you know, and just broad
enough, he will be doing a personal favor to some
of the Very Best People.
THE RULE OF THREE.
[For The Bulletin.]
It is rumored that, as a result of the inquiry into the
inharmonious relations of the N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners, one will retire shortly, another later on, a new
junior will be appointed from the service, and a new Chief
Commissioner will be imported.— Daily paper allegation.
The country’s mad ! It’s fairly mad !
Or so it seems to me 1
For everything we’ve got, begad,
Is run by Rule o’ Three !
It’s plainly clear it’s overdone ;
It vexes lots and pleases none ;
IVhat formerly was done by one
Must now be done by three 1
Though one, we know,
Could run the show,
And maybe two’d agree,
We think it bliss
To wink at this
Ridiculous Rule o’ Three !
In every sort of trade and sport
This kind of thing we see !
And in the Arbitration Court,
We have the Rule o’ Three 1
It seems to me that this is fudge—
A Labor man, a boss, a Judge 1
The good intent we can’t begrudge,
But how can they agree?
We must admire
The j ust desire
For peace and liarmonee ;
But all we get
Is small as yet—
And likely so to be !
The Harbor Trust is strict and just,
From rows it’s fairly free.
But harbor steamers seem to “ bust ’’
As oft as formerlee 1
And then the Public Service Board
(A luxury we can’t afford)
Is causing men who fear the Lord
To murmur “ B ! ” and “ D ! ”
They do ! My word !
As you have heard,
And must agree with me 1
It’s hot 1 It’s bad !
It’s rot 1 It’s sad !
This random Rule o’ Three 1
Though Doctor Wattsthis may protest
That little birds agree,
You put three cock birds in a nest —
The row will shake the tree !
It’s just the same in household life,
The same for husband and for wife,
With more than two there’s always strife—
And war there’s bound to be 1
A man and wife
May plan a life
Of calm felicitee ;
But Ma-ln-law
With Pa will jaw—
So bless the Rule of Three 1

129 KINO ST., SYDNEY
A Melbourne carpenter who got drunk and
died from apoplexy a few weeks ago, created more
sensation after his death than when he was alive.
The trouble arose through the arrival at the
morgue of two irate ladies armed with brollies,
each laying claim to the empty honor of being the
carpenter’s widow. There was quite a rush on
the carpenter. One claimant explained that she
married him in the dim, misty past, but had not
spoken to him for 13 years. The other seemed
more recent. Coroner Candler thought he had
got at the root of the matter when he asked each
lady to produce her marriage certificate, but
neither of them could show one. The more recent
relict didn’t seem to know what a certificate
meant, and conducted the following remarkable
dialogue with the coroner, who was in danger of
apoplexy himself most of the time: —
The Goroner asked, “Do you produce your marriage
certificate?”
The Claimant. —No.
Why not ?—You can see from a copy of Sands and
M'Dougall’s Directory that I am his wife.
I don’t want to do anything of the sort.—He used to
sign our names every seven years.
If you cannot produce your certificate and the other
lady can, that is enough for me.—Well, it is not enough
for me. My husband signed in Sands and M’Dougall’s
Yes ; but Sands and M'Dougall are not registrars. They
are printers.
Mrs. Jane Black. —I am not going to be stopped. I
intend to prove what I say.
Well, why not do so?—I went to see Captain Black
last night .
That is enough. Can you get a copy of your certificate I
—I don’t know what my husband did with it. It might
be amongst his papers.
Well, have you looked?—No.
Then why not ?—I am not in a fit state to look. It will
not bring my husband back to life or pay me for the
money I spent on his burial.
But you are in a fit state to come here ? —No, I am not.
I should he in bed. About two months ago to-day my
husband tried to hang himself on a bed-post.
The Coroner. —Oh !
The Claimant.—Yes. It was at 9 o’clock at night. My
husband was calling out, “Jane ! Dear Jane 1
The Coroner.—What husband do you mean? The
deceased ? . >
The Claimant.—Yes. He called out, “Jane, dear
good-bye.”
It is a sad evidence of the brutality of man that
even the fact of the deceased having called out
“Jane” while hanging from the bedpost wasn’t
regarded as sufficient evidence, and the lady was
sent back to hunt for her certificate.
• * * •
Queensland was a torn and divided State
over the Empire Day foolery. The Government
fixed the date, according to the Bank Holiday Act,
for the Monday following the 24th. A section
of the people hung on to the actual 24th just for
sheer cussedness. Their programme consisted of
banners and gay rags in the streets, and dull
speeches at night, all about the flag flag flag.
Then the 28th happened along, when the shops
shut up, and more flags were hung out and talked
about. The only remark of interest was made by
Crown Prosecutor Kingsbury, who said, amid the
flag oratory, that it was not well to look too closely
into the means by which some territory had been
secured.

IN PASSING.
FIEST OLD PERSON : “ Yes, Travers, there goes
the Chief Justice. I sold rag summer resi-
dence, at Darling Point, to him in ’76.”
The State Attorney-General, one K.C. and
eight dimmer legal lights (some of them mere
glimmers) all recently had a chew at a very small
legal bone in N.S.W. Equity Court. A testatrix
had bequeathed £2OO to trustees for the purpose
of keeping her grave green, and the trustees
appealed to the court for an instruction as to what
the corpse meant. By a convenient legal rule, all
legatees under the will were considered parties to
the suit, and each had to produce a separate lawyer
to listen to his Honor. His Honor decided that
the law re perpetuities made the bequest illegal,
no one being able to order that certain things be
done for ever and ever. So the green grave must
take its chance of being brown, and the legal
array retreated in good order to collect its fees.
• * * *
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade has
spoken with just severity to a needless and
bumptious character in Maoriland. Goloiiel
Home, M.L. Inspector of Prisons, who was the
superfluous and bumptious person, lately went
out of his way to say that, in proportion to popu-
lation, N.S.W. had about twice as many prisoners
as Maoriland. Thereupon Wade smote the un-
necessary and bumptious person pretty hard, by
replying that he had got at his figures by the
simple process of multiplying the N.S.W. gaol
population by ten.
* * * *
Sydney’s fish supply again vexes the
public mind. There is plenty of good fish in the
sea. There are hundreds of thousands of mouths
eager to eat fish. But, except to the rich,
0 no supply of fish is available. It is the
f same muddle—only to a greater degree—
as affects the supply and sale of fiuit
Sydney, of all civilised cities on earth,
has the smallest idea of community
organisation. Its 41 municipal institu-
tions are a weird joke. Its “public
spirit” is practically non-existent.
Nothing is done that depends for the
doing on the citizens acting in com-
mon-sensible unison to secure a mutual
benefit. The fault lies with the poli-
ticians who have wrangled for half a
century over Free trade and the “ Con-
stitooshun,” the “rights of the mother
State,” and one another’s characters,
to the neglect of real public business.
Sydney should be the home of a stal-
wart tribe of fishermen and the city of
cheap, abundant fish-food, just as it should be
the ever-hungry market of 10,000 flourishing
orchardists and the mother of children happy
with unlimited jam and fresh fruit. But it
isn’t. Nature is kind. But authority, or the
want of authority, or too many authorities (41
sets of them in a single city) muddle every
blessing.
The queerest theological occurrence this
year is the act of the Bishop of Southwark
(England) in inhibiting the Rev. Dr. Thackeray
M A., of Trinity College, Cambridge, for becoming
the landlord of an hotel “ The Fish and Eels,” at
Hoddesdon, on Thames. The Itev. publican
obtained a transfer of the license in February this
year, and lives on the premises. Rev. V\ . Garble,
head of the Church Army, warmly approves
Thackeray’s action in running the “Fish and
Eels ” pub.

A few weeks ago The Bulletin printed
this from a Perth (W.A.) correspondent
Westralian Civil Service Commissioner recently toured
the eastern States in search of a Government Printer, the
screw for which person is £5OO a year. Out of 40 appli-
cants he could not find one up to the mark—his mark.
Whoever drew up the conditions must have known as
much about the printing business as a myall knows about
the Ten Commandments. Among’ other things, the sue-
cessful candidate is required to know all about bookbind-
ing and stereotyping, the chemical properties of printers
inks, the mechanical details of printing presses of ail sorts
and sizes, and the various branches of the stationery and
paper trades. Also, he must possess a practical knowledge
of type-setting in every form. In addition to these and
other qualifications, the successful candidate must hate
successfully conducted a printing business of his own. All
tins for £5OO a year ! The Public Service Commissioner of
the Sandy State occupied six weeks over his trip, which
was fruitless, but apparently the conditions will be modi-
fied in the interests of some good old Groper, whose know-
ledge of tiie printing trade lias been gamed in some Perth
jobbing office.
And the billet has just been given to a job printer
from the office of Perth Morning Herald. That
Berth correspondent knew the ways of Groper-
dom.
A Maorilander who was at ’Frisco when
things happened, relates that a man got hold of a
cask of water, and was retailing it at 25 cents a
drink. Large numbers were only too glad to do
business even at that price, and Private Enter-
prise was booming. A poor woman came along
with a child and pleaded for a drink, as she and
the infant were dying of thirst. As she hadn’t
the shilling, she was told to go and shift for her-
self. The discussion attracted an officer and a
squad of soldiers, and after the case had been
heard, Private Enterprise was ordered to part
with one drink gratis. He laughed at the idea,
and in the middle of the smile the soldiers fired,
and the discussion was ended. It is one of the
few good things The Bulletin has heard about
martial law.
