The other day a Melbourne policeman,
who was the principal witness to procure a con-
viction against a publican charged with using his
premises for betting purposes, was cross-examined
thusly :
Mr. Duffy : You look smart in your uniform. How
long have you been a constable ? .
Witness: Since March last. I have done about six
weeks’ beat duty in the city. .
You were appointed as a reward for being a spy i— No,
I’m a constable pure and simple.
Are you living with your wife? —No, not for about two
years.
Are you living with another woman ? —I have a house-
keeper. . ... , t
Have you assaulted this woman in a public street l— l
didn’t strike her. I gave her a push.
Were you and this woman drunk together in the
street?—l was drunk. I wouldn’t be capable of telling
what state she was in.
Did your mother have to make good moneys which you
embezzled ?—lt was not embezzlement. There were a
few discrepancies.
Surely Melbourne must be a comic-opera city if
this is a hair sample of the agent employed there
to exterminate tote-shops, two-up schools, Sun-
day drinking, and the Social Evil, and generally to
purify the moral atmosphere.
* * * *
In Maoriland the Englishman has at last
rebelled. As a rule, Bull doesn’t take much stock
in saints or their days, but in Maoriland the
racket kicked up when St. Patrick’s day comes in
the morning, and the row when St. Andrew gets
drunk in the evening, and Scotchmen dressed in
kilts and whisky gibber up and down the streets,
has roused him into raising some dust on St.
George’s Day. So far he has only broken out in
Blenheim, where he holds sports and makes
quotations from the works of Billy Shakspeare,
whose natal day also falls (allegedly) on April 23rd.
But a number of papers are urging the rest of the
volcanic isles to start St. George’s societies, and
make more pandemonium. The end of all this
will be that the country will have to get a saint of
its own and suppress the imported article. The
Bulletin hasn’t thought out a Maoriland saint
yet, but for Australia St. Boomerang should fill
the bill—or else St. Wallaby,

LOCOMOTOR ATAXIA.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“I, Charles E. Williams, Mining Operator m
the state of South Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that Dr. Williams 1 ink Dills for
Pale People, cured me permanently and pos.tively
of Locomotor Ataxia. I am one of the oldest
citizens in South Australia. I was born in Corn-
wall on July 6, 1830, and came to the colonies in
1848. The next year I moved to Kapunda, and I
have lived here in Beck St., in this very house
ever since. . ,
“ In the Kapunda Copper mines I had to work
very hard, and was often exposed to cold and wet.
One day, when off color, 1 caught a chill and
went down with Influenza. Another chill brought
on a relapse and started my disease, llay by day,
I got steadily worse. My ankles grew stiff and
useless. My back was lame and every bone in my
body ached. Rheumatic pains stiffened up all my
muscles and pretty well crippled me. In my
joints a constant, gnawing ache ate right into my
marrow. And then Locomotor Ataxia began to
come on. At first the attacks were gradual, but
they quickly grew more frequent and agonising.
Lightning pains shot through my legs. I hey
lasted only a few seconds, but were like sharp
stabs of a knife ripping through my nerves and
muscles. Soon these pains spread all over me,
and I felt as if my body were squeezed in a great
vice. For two years I did not do a tap of work
and drew sick pay from my lodge. I thought it
was Rheumatism —but soon my sight became
affected, and that is the surest sign of Locomotor
Ataxia. „ _ , , , „
“When I could get about at all I had to walk
with two sticks. It took me an hour to hobble
from the Post Office to my house, only 300 yards
away. I knew if I took one false step and fell 1
could never get up again. I lost all power to
direct my legs. I had to resort to all sorts oi
devices to dress myself. I could not stoop to put
on my boots to save my life. If I closed my eyes
and tried to put my heels together, I would have
fallen headlong. These little things showed me
clearly that I was becoming gradually paralysed
with Locomotor Ataxia.
“At last I had to give up my bed and take a
couch by the fire. I spent nearly all my time
there—and was just able to hobble across to an
easy chair. When I wanted to move I had to use
a rope to lift myself. All this time I was
suffering the most acute torture from the sharp
lightning stabs of pain that pierced every nerve
muscle, and joint in my body. My whole health
failed as the Locomotor Ataxia advanced. My
stomach went back on me. The lightest meal
left a heavy lump on my chest as if my food had
turned to lead. My spirits sank, and I gave my-
self up to die a miserable death. Certainly I had
not much hope, for doctors say that Locomotor
Ataxia is incurable. , . .
“I had the best medical attendance—but
nothing did me the least good. I was as helpless
as if I had been stricken with Paralysis. And,
worse than that, I was torn every hour with tne
cruel pains. Some people said I had acute Rheu-
matism, but I honestly think that I preferred the
Locomotor Ataxia because it promised an early
Death and release from my pain. I was m this
helpless, hopeless state when I saw a book telling
how Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale 1 eople
cured all blood and nerve diseases. On page 1J I
read, in big type, the words Locomotor Ataxia /
It explained that it was a kind of Paralysis caused
by disease of the spine. The symptoms were just
my own —and, best of all, on the same page, Mr.
Thomas Jarvis, of Sydney, told how Dr. Williams
Pink Pills had cured him after he had suffered for
months from the same disease. Every word
showed that his case was the same as mine. 1
bought some Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, and they cured my Locomotor Ataxia,
absolutely and completely.
“ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills did not cure me in a
week or a month, but I can honestly say that I
was a bit better after the very first box. lhey
loosened the muscles of my back and gave me
control over my nerves, so that I could stoop to
lace my boots—a thing that had been beyond me
for months. My appetite picked up, and a hearty
meal caused no pain. After that every dose put
new life into me, and I started to put on weight.
The muscles of my legs and arms grew stout and
strong again. My eyesight became good, and the
lightning pains became fewer and duller, 1 kept
on taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills till I used
sixteen boxes. At last every atom of pam and
weakness vanished. I threw away my sticks and
stood a cured man. Instead of lying at home a
helpless invalid, I joined my mates in the mines.
As long as I live i’ll praise Dr. William Pink
Pills, for curing me of Locomotor Ataxia.
“This is the plain, honest statement—and
every leading citizen of Kapunda can testify to its
truth. I consider that Dr. Williams Pink Puls
for Pale People worked a positive miracle when
thev cured my Locomotor Ataxia.
“My cure is permanent, for I have enjoyed the
very best of health for the last six years. To-day
I ain as energetic as any man in Kapunda—and all
the credit belongs to Dr. Williams Pink I ills for
Pale People. .
“And I make this solemn declaration conscien-
tiously believing the same to be the truth, the
whole truth, and nothing but the truth.’
(Signed) C. E. WILLIAxMS.
Declared at Kapunda, this twenty-first day of
Sept., A.D., 1903, beforejne^
DAVID JAMES. J.P.
Mayor of Kapunda.

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
With only one month more to go in this financial
year, it ought now to be possible to tell how every State
stands in the matter of making or faking a surplus ; but
it isn’t possible to any great extent. As far as the pub-
lished figures run, here is the situation :

From the foregoing and some other data, the
position may be summed up as follows :
N.S. Walk*.—According to the Treasury returns, the
1 1 months’revenue was £11,151,397 anil the expenditure
£10,640,215, so there was a surplus of £511,182. Of the
so-called revenue, however, an at present unknown
amount, but, judging by past experience, about £BOO,OOO,
is; not revenue at all; it is simply that assets have been
sold and the proceeds improperly seized and used as
revenue. These assets consist of public lands ; and as
public lands can’t be sold twice (unless the State buys
back the land from the first purchaser and sells it over
again), the figures that are swollen by including this item
are no good for measuring future prospects by. And,
while something like £BOO,OOO must come off the revenue,
thus turning the alleged surplus of £511,182 into a
deficit of £288,818, there has to be added £1,379,265 to
the expenditure, this amount having been provided out
of loans. Instead of having a surplus of £511,182, N.S.
Wales has therefore not paid its way during
the year so far by £1,668,083. Put in another way,
it has mopped up all of its revenue, sold and spent over
£BOO,OOO of its assets, and then gone into debt £1,379,205.
It must leave off borrowing some time, and it won’t have
any more land to sell soon ; and when that happens, it
will either have to raise £1,668,083 more revenue in 11
months, or spend £1,668,083 less, in order to come out
square in its books. Even then it will not do what every
well-managed private concern does—it will be providing-
nothing either for a sinking fund or for the renewal of
works which have been paid for out of capital. Inside a
month J. Carruthers will get on the highest branch avail-
able and screech about a surplus and the magnificent
results of Reform. And all the real surplus there is and
all the Reform there has been are pictured in the fore-
going.
Victoria.—How Victoria is going to wind up the year
there is as yet no way of telling, it not only doesn’t pub-
lish current loan figures, it doesn’t even publish current
figures as to expenditure on revenue aocount. These
are the only facts to go on with : For 11 months, revenue
this year is £225,725 more than it was for the 11 months
last year ; and for the first six months the expenditure
was £75,439 less than it was in 1905. And for all 1905 the
surplus was £526,984. By rule of three the surplus this
year should therefore be £923,000. In this, of course, is
included the usual steal of land-sales money, amounting
in Victoria to an average of £250,000 a yeai- about one-
fourth the size of the N.S. Wales steal. In fact, for all
Australia the land-sales steal amounts to about £1,700,000
a year, and of that the boss manipulator, N. S. Wales is
responsible for £1,000,000.
Queensland.—This time last year the Northern State
had £503,296 available towards the half-year’s interest ;
now if has £712,398. Last year the surplus was £13,996
and the present position is £204,102 ahead of last year’s.
Further, though Queensland’s land-sales money amounts
to between £250,000 and £300,000 a year,' Premier
Kids ton is pledged to use it only to provide public works
which, under the old gang, would have been paid for with
loans ; and the old gang would have sold the land just
the same. Queensland’s interest and charges amount to
about £1,530,000 a year. Even in this extra-good vear
that means 41 per cent, of the entire Queensland revenue.
Westralia.—This State’s current position :

Loan expenditure figures are not published ; and, with-
out them, the others are not honestly worth much. The
position can't look better with those figures : the chances
are it will look a great deal worse.
S. Australia and Tasmania.—They are tired States, and
are mostly content to take things for granted till the end
of each financial year. And then they see that the figures
are old, and, therefore, don’t worry about them. So
S. Australia has, per head of population, the Greatest
Debt on Earth, and Tasmania has, per head, the Greatest
Unproductive Debt on Earth.
* * * *
There are 1,505,000 people in N.S.W., and
1,504,997 J of them or thereabouts have been busv thank-
ing Heaven during the last few days that they are not as
other men are, and have never played tricks in connec-
tion with N.S.W. lands. But, as a matter of fact, the
whole 1,505,000 have been playing a most disgraceful
trick—and Justice Owen didn’t say a word about it. For
N.S.W. for years past has been selling public lands—the
assets of the State, on the security of which Cohen has
lent £83,662,432—and, instead of putting other assets in
the place of those it has collared, it has coollv used
the proceeds as revenue and applied them in
payment of the policeman and the interest
bill. For years past the average amount of the State’s
assets which have been sold and the proceeds thus mis-
applied has been £1,000,000 a year ; and alongside that
swindle the swindles of the men whose names have been
lately much talked of are small potatoes. Yet hardly a
politician can raise a blush over it, and there isn’t any
demand for a Commission to report that, in this matter
the State has been a reckless financier who, if he were a
private person and had his affairs before a Judge in Bank-
ruptcy, would certainly have his certificate refused and
most likely would get about 12 months’ gaol as well.

In spite of the reduction in rates for deposits
Australian bank earnings have shrunk during the past
twelve months. Advances have been fairly well sus-
tained; but many old ones at high rates‘have been
reduced and new ones at lower rates have taken their
place. Fortunately there has been a keen demand for
money at short call in London, so that the Australian
banks’ large accumulation of deposits has had a fairly
profitable outlet not generally open to it—or only open
to it at such low rates as not to make it worth while con-
sidering'. The position, so far as it can be presented in a
table, is shown hereunder. The figures are for the last
twelve months for which accounts have been published,
in a few cases ending with March, 1900, in others endin<«-
with December, 1905

It might be a better plan in most cases to turn the thing
upside down and show how much was laid by as reserves,
only some banks write down certain assets out of profits,
and that is as good as putting the amounts to reserve. It
can’t quite be said either that the foregoing list
invariably shows the banks which have most regard
for their depositors and other creditors, because the pre-
sent extent of reserves has a good deal to do with that
matter; a bank like the E., S. and A., which has still a
good deal of cleaning up to do, is not in the same state as
the Bank of Australasia, for instance, which isn’t under
the same painful necessity. Still the list certainly shows
some of the banks that are paving away far too much,
even if it doesn’t show' all of them.

The Bank of New Zealand has been in a state of tran-
sition during the year, and it is impossible to submit
similar figures of any value concerning its earnings. The
Q.N.’s figures, also, are of little value ; for, besides capi-
tal, it has £3,116,621 of old private deposits (now con-
verted into interminable 3J p.c. stock), as well as other
liabilities of a similar character. The Australian Deposit
and Mortgage Bank has, during the year, written down
its capital from £909,979 to £198,935, which accounts for
the remarkable increase in its earnings per cent, of share-
holders’ funds.

In addition to the sinking fund (reckoned here among re-
serves)there is a reservefund properand afloa ting balance.
Not only the sinking fund, but the reserve fund also,
is credited with the interest which it earns—which shows
that the aldermen of Launceston either have a truer idea
than the Government of N.S. Wales has as to what a
sinking fund really is, or are more honest in administer-
ing it. The year’s profit (before paying interest) was
£461 less than that for 1904 ; the revenue was larger
but repairs, maintenance and renewals, all charged
to revenue, amounted to £2024 as against £1533
an increase of £491. The reserve fund, which is supposed
to provide for renewals when they become too heavy to be
paid for outof current revenue, amounts to £39BB(included
among reserves in the second table above); of this
amount, £2087 was added last year, equal to ljper cent.-
on the total capital outlay. Summed up, this is the
position : After paying interest and providing the works
with repairs and renewals, the undertaking shows
£ll9s. lOd. per cent, profit on the average amount of
capital invested in it during the year. Out of this
provision has to be made for sinking fund and for re-
newals when the renewals become too heavy to be paid
for out of current revenue. A margin of 2 per cent, is
certainly not too much for the purpose, especially at this
early stage in the works’ history (the margin will get less
as the renewals cost more); yet, compared with what
shiftless State Treasurers do—they don’t even show in-
terest on their borrowed money—it is magnificent.

The earnings are erratic, which in connection with a
trustee co. always arouses a suspicion that it takes full
credit for the commission on the corpus of an estate when
the estate comes in, thus leaving the annual commission
to pay expenses of the subsequent management of the
estate. Interest earnings are also very erratic. Never-
theless business expands, and the profits are still glitter-
ing. If a manufacturer made £l5 16s. per cent, on his
capital and undivided profits in a year, every Freetrader
in the country would be shocked, and every importer
would call upon the gods to witness the horror of it.

The Coastal Farmers’ Co-operative Society,

1905.. 4958 6 „ „ 368 4188 402 1252

So the co. goes to its small reserves, grabs £352, and pays
a bonus to consignors, in order to make to them a cash
concession of 25 percent, on the amount of their com-
mission-payments. The one thing that can be said in
extenuation is that the money is not applied as dividend
to shareholders, though whether there is much differ-
ence between the two things The Bulletin doesn’t know.
Possibly consignors expect this sort of thing ; if they do,
they are very short-sighted. Their true policy is to help
the co. to get together decent resources, not only in order
to guarantee themselves payment when some unexpected
disaster happens, but so that it may be in a position to
battle better for its clients. For instance, the co. on
March 31st owed consignors £19,443, and on other similar
accounts another £7555. On the other side, “outstanding
accounts ” stood at £28,529 ; and there are large possi-
bilities about outstanding accounts of £28,529 should a
disaster happen along. If the co. could not collect, the
co., whose own capital is only £8445, could certainly not
pay. The reserve fund ought to be built up to meet
circumstances of that kind. At present, having all its
own resources liquid, the co. no doubt stands in a strong
financial position—so long as things go reasonably well.
Considering the vast services which these co-operative
concerns are rendering the farmers, the latter might be
a little more generous ; for it is hard to believe that
directors would go to their reserves in order to pay cus-
tomers a bonus if the customers didn’t drive them to it.
* * * *
When the liquidator steps in, honesty and fair
dealing sometimes stay on in the premises ; but, if they
do, they have a hard time of it as a rule. There was a
good deal of swindling during the Melbourne land boom ;
but, for unadulterated villainy, it could not hold a candle
to what happened during subsequent liquidation pro-
ceedings. This is a branch of the public’s business that
the Public Trustee, when he arrives, might very well,
undertake ; at present the job is often handed over to
somebody whose own incapacity or personal greed has
helped to bring the concern to grief.
And it is already becoming plainer that the
Public Trustee should take over the control of every
insurance co. that decides to give up taking new business
in Australia—especially every foreign co. When a co.
decides that it won’t take any new Australian business,
its troubles about what Australia thinks of it or its
methods ! Its concern is to make things good for the
people among whom it is canvassing for policies ; and it
can make thing's better for them by making them worse
for the other people—the people it doesn’t care, two
straws about. And if it wants to do that, there is no sort
of co. which can do it more easily than an insurance co. ;
there is no sort of contract that ought more to be read in
the light of equity, and there is none so liable to
have a flaw in it if viewed in a narrow legal
way. The insurance co. which is asking for new
business knows that; and as long as it is satisfied
that it has not been the victim of deliberate fraud, it
pays up. But the other sort of co., when it sees a
possible hole, is liable to make a bee-line for it. It mav
have the brick of public scorn thrown at it; but the
brick of public scorn only counts when it might hit a
canvasser who is just about to rope in a new “ life.”
The Equitable of the U.S. is big and honest
enough to be above the worst of the tricks that might be
adopted by a foreign office which has withdrawn from
new business ; yet even the Equitable was fighting a claim
on a policy the other day. The society objected to pay
up “ on the grounds that Blake, when passed as a good
life, suppressed the fact that he had suffered from
appendicitis and gallstones.” Even apart from
the fact that the medical evidence about the
gallstones and the appendicitis was conflicting, Blake
didn’t die from them, anyway; in fact, he got into
financial difficulties and ended up in quite another way.
It is hardly possible to believe that any insurance
co. still looking for business in the country would have
mentioned either the g'allstones or the appendix. The
view the Supreme Court of Westralia took of the matter
was that the co. must pay up, with interest. In other
words, it came to the conclusion that the co. had tried to
get out of a payment it should not legally escape—and if
the other sort of co. errs at all, it prefers to err on the
other side. If they did any less than that, insurance
wouldn’t be as popular as it is.
» * * #
Broken Hill Water Supply, the mostly Mel-
bourne co. which owns the only reservoir the Barrier can
get a drink from, is about to return a first £50,000 to the
shareholders. The co. has to hand over to the State its
works in about 12 years, without payment. For years it
has been accumulating funds, and has about £BO,OOO of
assets which it won’t have to part with, among
them over £50,000 of public securities, &c. Hitherto
investors who dig deep into such matters have
assumed that the fund would remain there at
compound interest for 12 years more, by which
time £50,000 would be worth, say £70,000 at least,
and probably £80,000; and of course the situation is con-
siderably altered if the other £150,000 (present capital is
£200,000) is expected back in 12 years without the help
of the compound interest. The contributions out of
profit and loss will have to be much larger. No doubt
that can be managed easily enough ; for, though the
capital will be reduced from £200,000 to £150,000, the
earnings from the works will not be any less. Of course
the change won’t affect present holders ; instead of the
co. investing their accumulated ss. per share, they will
be able to invest it themselves. It is the man who buys
the share who will have to remember that the position
with regard to the fund for the return of capital has been
altered.

The latest estimates of the ore reserves of the
chief W.A. mines are as follows .-—Brown Hill Extended,
52,000 tons, worth 445. 6d. per ton ; Cumberland, 11,300
tons at 855.; Golden Horseshoe, 931,300 tons at 645.; Sons
of Gwalia, 358,500 tons at 40s. 6d.; Associated, 263,700
tons at 48s. 5d.; South Kalgurli, 123,000 tons at 395.;
Ivanhoe, 769,000 tons at 595. 4d.; Boulder, 453,600 tons at
855.; Red Hill, 13,400 tons at 31s. 6d.; Lake View Consols,
103,000 tons at 31s. 6d.; Ingliston Extended, 15,000 tons at
85s. ; Great Fingall, 753,000 tons at 555. 6d. ; Tower
Hill, 60,000 tons at 305.; Great Ophir, 65,000 tons at 425.;
Golden Pole, 134,000 tons at 1275.; Boulder Perseverance,
336.300 tons at 435.; Kalgurli, 548,000 tons at 615.; Merton’s,
202.300 tons at 30s. ; Hainault, 205,000 tons at 365. ; Asso-
ciated Northern, 89,400 tons at 945. ; Oroya, 192,200 tons
at 158 s. Most of these estimates as regards values seem
to be pure guess work, but Associated Northern, Hainault,
Kalgoorlie and Gwalia practically give the extractable
value. Numbers of the mines either extract only 50 per
cent, of the gold, or the managers over-estimate the gold
contents by 100 per cent. The directors of all such mines
are neglecting their duties in not holding an inquiry into
the discrepancy when subsequent work discloses it.
* • # *
Associated, Kalgoorlie, is opening up splen-
didly. The ore reserves on January 1 were estimated at
263,700 tons, worth 48s. sd. a ton ; but from 1000 ft. to
ilOOft. of development per month has since been done,
and the ore in sight has been largely increased. The
fourteenth level at 1700 ft. has been started, and the lode
there also should be cut before long. The Australia East
lode is opening up well at the four bottom levels, and is
adding greatly to reserves. Then the prospects of the
much-talked-of Oroya shoot are much more hopeful. A
great lot of work has been done on this body. Vertical
winzes from the long incline winze (now 600 ft. deep on
the incline) have been sunk at intervals of 125 ft., and
have proved the pipe to be as large as it was in the Oroya.
Proper connection has been made between the 1000 ft. and
1200 ft.; so in future the ore, instead of having to be hauled
up 600 ft. of an incline and then trucked another 1200 ft., will
be passed down to the 1200 ft. level and trucked 200 ft.
only. Two new furnaces have been installed, and the 16
old ones are being gradually rebuilt. Once this is com-
pleted, the output will be increased and the costs re-
duced.
* * * *
Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) has taken over the
Sandstone leases at Black Range, after an eight months’
option, during which a great deal of work was done.
The leases comprise 148 acres, containing 3000 ft. along
the strike of the reef. Numerous shafts have been sunk,
showing the reef to be 2ft. to Bft. wide, averaging about
4ft. There are already 3000 tons of pay ore on the sur-
face, and a 20-bead mill is to be erected immediately. All
this stone has been taken out in the course of develop-
ment, and the result will give a fair indication of the
value of the property. The purchase price is said to be
£lO,OOO cash, and half the net value of the gold from stone
at grass.
* + * •
Lake View Consols, Kalgoorlie, has now proved
the Perseverance lode at 1900 ft. to be 10ft. wide and
worth 555. per ton. Until some driving has been done it
is premature to express any opinion on this discovery,
but, if the pay shoot continues for any length, many
opinions will have to be altered. As much as £133,000 of
Lake View money has recently been diverted to other
countries, as the management, after sinking fruitlessly-
to 1900 ft., decided that the bottom was out of the mine.
Unfortunately- there is no George Lansell in W.A., and
the first blank, or impoverishment, that occurs in the
lodes knocks the stuffing out of directors, and they
are ready lo give the job best. A good many manage!s
are as bad. All the Menzies mines have been abandoned
at a vertical depth of 300 ft. Certainly geologists Jack
and Goeczel were consulted before the abandonment ;
but geologists can see no further than other people.
• * * *
Hannan’s Reward, Kalgoorlie, is working on a
big scale since erecting the new 10-head mill, of 12501 b.
stamps, which can crush four to five tons per stamp daily.
During April, 1431 tons were treated for 3410 z., worth
£1449, or practically £1 a ton. This class of ore would
not have been looked at a while ago. There is an open-
cut on the mine, and ore can be got out for 2s. per ton.
Anything over 2dwt. pays, and 3dw't. leaves a fair profit.
Work is also being done on the main lode at 300 ft., where
the rock is a hard, dense sulphide. This ore was too hard
for the old light stampers to treat profitably, but it is
hoped that the new mill will get through it.
* * * *
Engineer Ridgeway’s patented invention for
treating slimes, after four months’ run on the Great
Boulder (W.A.), is found to save idwt. (or 2s. per ton)
more gold than filter presses, at a cost of 3s. less. The con-
trivance consists of a circular tank about 10ft. in diameter
and 15in. in depth, divided into three compartments.
Round this revolve 14 filter plates connected with valves
used either for suction or expulsion. These filter plates,
which are corrugated, have the filter-press cloth attached
below, and are carried round the tank on wheels. The
rim of the tank is like a switchback, and each plate in
turn dives into the first compartment, which contains the
slimes solution. The suction valve then opens and auto-
matically sucks up about five-eighths of an inch of slimes
against the cloth. The switchback then carries the plate
up out of the solution, and it dives into clean water, and
is dragged through it, and again rises over a shoot.
While there the vaive re-opens, and air is blown through
and expels the slimes off the plate into the shoot, and
they fall into a truck below-. The Great Boulder has
ordered ten of these machines, and they- will shortly bo
installed.

The North End, Kalgoorlie, according to its
new- manager (the fifth in its short career, or at the rate
of one a month) is looking well. But, as reporters
are sooled off the premises, folk become suspicious.
There is a pipe of ore dipping south, which has
been worked right up to the surface on the
north, where it cuts clean off. How far the shoot
travels south has not been proved, as the co.
started with too little capital, most of which was spent
on equipment and plant and surface decorations, and ore
has been simply gouged out to try to keep things
jigging. Co. is in 200,000 shares, w hich have been quoted
about 25., but they cannot be recommended except as a
pure gamble.
* * * *
Brown Hill Extended, Kalgoorlie, is now open-
ing out at 800 ft. from the new main shaft. There are
two payable lodes on the property- besides the Brown Hill
lode, w-hich crosses the north-west corner, and manager
John’s estimates ore reserves roughly- at 52,000 tons, worth
445. 6d. per ton, or over £lOO,OOO. The ore is not blocked
out, but the manager is a cautious man whose estimates
can be relied on to be pretty conservative. There is still
a good deal of ore standing in the Brown Hill lode, as
only the richest ore was broken out. The other two
lodes are practically intact, as no stoping has been done.
Co. is in 100,000 shares of £1 each, latest quote for which
is 10s. After paying last div., on April 15, 1904, co. had
a cash balance of £51,000 ; and, even allowing for equip-
ping and sinking the new shaft. it must still be in a
strong financial position.
* * # *
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), now accounts
for its poor returns by stating that the gold is being
systematically stolen, and the chief detective at Kalgoorlie
has been called in to investigate. No doubt gold robbery
has been carried on extensively in Westralia, and con-
siderable fortunes have been made by- some of the
“receivers”; but for the management to blame it for
such a collapse as that of Golden Pole is too thin. It is
something like what happened at Broken Hill; when the
Fat crowd had scooped out the rich oxidised ores and
sent shares up sky-high on the strength of the big
dividends, they had to find some excuse for what was to
come next, and they said the men robbed the co.’s by-
sleeping underground when they- should have been at
work. The shareholders were thus worked up
to such a pitch of indignation against the miners
that they- forgot to be mad with the Fat men
w-ho had really- done all the serious fleecing.
In the case of Golden Pole, consider a few figures.
On February 1 the ore reserves were given as 13,400 tons,
worth 1275. per ton ; yet the three returns since have
panned out only 665. 3d., 575. 6d., and 635. 9d., or barely
half, although 800 tons of old tailings have been include!:!

Burbanks Birthday Gift, Coolgardie (W.A.),
shows how tributors can score where managers can’t.
Mine is controlled by 8., M. and Co., who, on taking
charge, shut down the mill for months and pushed
developments ahead. When the mill was re-started returns
only averaged from sdwt. to 6dwt., so after running some
months at a stead}' loss, the mine was closed down, and
tributes were offered three or four months ago. Since
then the mine has produced over IOOOoz. monthly, the
last return being 1041 oz. from 1167 tons. Some of the
tribute parties have made as much as £l2 a week per
man.
* . * * *
Merton’s Reward (W. A.) has been closed down,
allegedly because of shortage of water, by Charlie
Kaufmann. In January, co. treated 2590 tons of 275. 9d.
per ton ore at a profit of £100; in February 3017 tons
of ‘2ls. lOd. ore were treated at a loss of £7BO ; and during
March 2033 tons of 265. sd. ore realised a loss of £6O. No
figures have been published for April, probably because
they were too bad. On June 30 last ore reserves were
estimated at 202,300 tons, worth 305., or enough to keep
the mill going for six years. Unfortunately the results
have not come up to expectation, and Kaufmann, the
biggest shareholder, doesn’t see the fun of running the
mine at a loss. Mine is in charge of Judd, who for years
bossed the Lake View South, but neither he nor anyone
else can make a success of a gold mine if the gold
is not there. The Government has arranged a water
supply, but water is not gold. The water scheme was to
save Coolgardie, but it did not put metal into buck reefs.

The Vivien, Lawlers (W.A.), is now equipped
with a 20-head instead of a 10-head mill, and it is expected
that 3000 tons monthly will be treated in future. The
last estimate of reserves gave the value at 435. 6d. per
ton, but actual results have not come within coo-ee of it,
the April extraction being 255. 7d. only. Mine has two to
three years’ supply of ore in sight, but the reef is very
patchy, judging by the erratic nature of the monthly
returns. Co. is in 90,00# shares of £1 each, chiefly held
by the London and Western Australian Exploration Co.,
and is controlled by Bewick, Moreing and Co.
* * » *
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) will soon make a
fresh start on its new property, the Goodenough. The
surface equipment should be ready early this month.
Manager Ferguson is confident of the result; and, as he
has had a long experience of the erratic reefs of the
district, his opinion is worth something.

At last the Cobar (N.S.W.) deal has been
completed—or the money has been put up to complete
it. “ The Syndicate ” gets £BOO,OOO ; another £400,000
has been subscribed for various purposes ; and another
£300,000 is reserved—making up the total nominal capital
of £1,500,000. According to a statement made by the
British Australasian (London) the capital was to be dis-
posed of this way
Purchase money £BOO,OOO
For additional purchases .. 100,000
Reserve debentures .. .. 200,000
Working capital 200,000
Underwriting shares .. .. 75,000
Underwriting debentures .. 37,500
Stamp fees 40,000
Other expenses 47,500
Total £1,500,000
No doubt the “ additional purchases ” contemplated for
£lOO,OOO consisted of the Cobar Gold Mines’ property.
As, however, that mine has not been included, this
£lOO,OOO is now represented by reserve shares ; so there
will be 650,000 shares issued out of 750,000, and 550,000
debentures out of 750,000. The underwriting was a pretty
costly business with copper away up past £BO, seeing
that all the estimates were based on a £6O market.
Previous expert Kendall figured on a total pos-
sible annual profit of £456,750 from Great Cobar ; the
syndicate which put off the present co. talked of £400,000
a year, with ore reserves sufficient for three years at that
rate. The syndicate figured it this way: 1,500,000 tons ;
average, 3| per cent. ; workable extraction, 83 per cent. ;
copper to be produced, 41,500 tons; profit, with gold
contents, (say) £3O per ton = £1,245,000. Expert Kendall
reckoned on a profit of £32 12s. Od. with copper at £52.
Apparently the present valuation is based on a £6O
market, in which case the present valuation of the mine
is more conservative than Kendall’s was. Meantime,
certainly much good ore has been extracted. Ordinarily
£6O would be a high copper price to figure on, even
though it is £25 higher than that now ; and £25 per ton
on the extractable copper in the reserves amounts to
£1,037,500. Still, it is not as though the new co. had to
look at the market ahead for years. Given energy, in
four and a-half years from now, with copper at £6O, the
co. should either have got back its capital or laid it aside
for further developments. And £6O looks a perfectly
safe average for as little as four and a half years.
Of Great Cobar’s reserved debentures £lOO,OOO
worth are to be drawn in two years, and the other
£lOO,OOO in three years, at £lO5.
Cobar district has hung out its joyful rags over
the sale of its boss property to J. Bull; for it means
doubling the number of hands employed, and that means
more beer to be sold, and other local blessings. For the
rest, there isn’t much to be glad about. For £BOO,OOO
to the vendors, and about £200,000 to others, Bull has
got the greatest copper mines in Australia, and will not
be satisfied unless he draws at least £2,000,000 in dividends
out of them. Probably he will spend another £300,000,
and that and anymore he spends will be just so much
foreign debt on which Australia will be expected to pay
interest; and in this case it gives its best copper mines as
security that the interest will be paid. There is mighty
little, therefore, to hang out joyful rags for. There
wasn’t even the excuse that “ foreign capital was neces-
sary to develop the great natural resources of the
country.” For some years past the Read-Longworth
Australian syndicate has made an average profit of
£lOO,OOO per annum ; its recent profits have been at the
rate of nearly £150,000 a year. There was, therefore,
plenty of capital coming out of the mine to develop its
own resources. Certainly they have not been developed
as fast as they might have been recently ; but that was
simply because of this option. People who reckon that
“The Syndicate” has been wasting its time must be
gluttons. In 1893 the mine was practically abandoned ;
it was only in January, 1894—twelve years ago—that the
new crowd, having got a tribute, began to work. During
the first year it got out 13,460 tons of ore, valued at
£26,600 ; and considering that for years it was only a
tribute syndicate, and started with very little capital and
not too much faith (outside the Longworths), the people
who, looking back on that small beginning, reckon that
it hasn’t done enough meantime are pretty hard to
satisfv. Perhaps they are better satisfied with what J.
Bull’s Cobar Gold Mines, Ltd., has done ! That concern
was going to rush things, and put up a vast plant (which
is now just about due to be scrapped) ; and, for the past
18 months, it has been sitting down paying directors’ fees
and wondering why the blessed sulphide problem has not
got up and solved itself.
6 r * * * *
Bulletin readers will not have been surprised
by the creep in the Sulphide Corporation’s Central mine,
Broken Hill. The north end has now settled with a run,
carrying down the mill with it. The vigor of the move-
ment can be judged from the fact that it ran right
through the neighboring Block 10 mine, and affected the
deep workings in the Broken Hill Prop, on the other side.
The Central mill has settled so much that if it is ever to
be used again it will only be by pulling it down and re-
erecting it; that, anyhow, would be the easiest and
quickest way to patch "it. For a couple of years past it
has been known that the mill would go sooner
or later, and a new plant was started ; but it
is a long way from being ready. The zinc plant
is at work, but there is very little money in that.
Meantime 1000 men have been thrown out of work, and
the hope of dividends for the Sulphide Corporation’s
ordinary shares fades further into the distance. Whether
the vigor of the movement means that there has now
been a complete settlement no one can say until an ex-
amination is made far more thorough than has yet been
possible.
B.H. Block 10 is a mine with a remarkable
backing. The morning after the creep shares tumbled to
84s. By the afternoon there was such buying both in
London and in Australia that the market had got back
to 90s.—just about where it stood before the shock. As
a matter of fact, though the settlement could only do
Block 10 harm, it could not very seriously
damage it, because its main workings are below the
level (800 ft.) at which the Central movement must
stop. Block 10 is now going even deeper; the
announcement was made, just when it would do most
good, that from 1100 ft. the co. would push down to
1600 ft. A new windiilg plant will have to be got in ; and
the electric-power plant must be strengthened. The
B.H. Prop, has the lode at 1100 ft. in Block 11, next door ;
and the B.H. ore-bodies pitch away north and south from
an apex somewhere in Block 13 of the B.H. Prop. mine.
Block 10, being south of this apex, might expect the
same ore, then, at a lower level than Block ll’s 1100 ft.
The experience so far has been that, with depth, the
Barrier ores get richer in metals, but harder and more
refractory.
Managements of the southern group of Broken
Hill mines (Block 10, Central and South) are not a happy
family. Each always blames some other one for every
creep. Present trouble in the Central is alleged by the
Central to be Block 10’s fault; and Block 10 is sure it
isn’t. There were such ructions over a previous creep
that a Commission was appointed to find out all about it.
The Commission made recommendations which got lost
next day, and have never since been seriously thought of.
The Central’s troubles really began, like those of every
other Broken Hill mine, with Australia’s importing
disease. It dumped forests of imported softwood into
the mines ; it imported mine managers who imported
foreign methods (and more forests of foreign timber);
and when there was two or three millions’ worth of
timber in the mines, Broken Hill co.’s found out that
Australian mine engineers made fewer mistakes, that it
was madness to rely upon the softwoods to resist the
lateral pressure, and that the pretty imported methods of
timbering were entirely unsuited to lodes of the width of
the Broken Hill body. But it was too late then ; the
timbers had begun to give, the ground had begun to
move, and from that day to this it has never wholly
stopped, in spite of the fact that nowadays the co.’s fill in
with debris the stopes from which the ore has been taken.
* * * *
From a Broken Hill man ‘'The hard facts
about the latest creep are that the Central mill has sub-
sided a clear 3J*t., and will never work again. Block 10
has lost Campbell shaft down to the 700 ft., and is
shut off from what little ore there was left in
the upper levels there. The Central (Sulphide Cor-
poration) intends to put on a spurt in erecting its
new mill, of which the timber framework is up ; but
at least four months must elapse before the job stands
completed. The smash has extended from the 700 ft. to
the surface, and is wedge-shaped, the thin end being
about 250 ft. long and resting on the Block 10 boundary
at the 700 ft. level. Spreading upwards and outwards, it
just about reaches the footwall near the surface. The
footwall runs along a few feet to the east of the mill, and
is marked by the clean break where the ground beneaih
the mill has sunk.”
* * * *
Flooding the northern group of Broken Hill
mines has had the expected effect of starting the old
movement in the north end of the Junction. M‘lntyre
shaft, near the boundaries of Junction North and Block
16 (British), has caved in at 550 ft., and is smashed from
that up to the 400 ft. The old creep just catches the
Junction North shaft between 570 ft. and 640 ft., and that
section is badly dragged. No damage was done in Browne
section of the Junction ; and the North mine is pretty
secure. The crosscut made at 550 ft. in the Junction to
work the new find of high-grade ore in the British has
been smashed. That ore body must remain unworked
till the Marsh shaft is deepened.
* * * *
If the amalgamation of the B.H. North and
Junction North comes off, the North mill will probably be
doubled (present capacity, 2000 tons per week), and all
the ore treated there. But amalgamation is not regarded
favorably in Broken Hill. Broken Hill lives by wages,
not dividends.

South New Moon, Bendigo, puts up a six
months’ tally of 90500 z. from 12,430 tons; average,
14|dwts. Dividends panned out at 16s. per share, aggre-
gating £25,600. This is below the average for the

* * » *
South Caledonia, Warrandyte (Vic.) struck the
Yarra river last week. Now, the gamble is whether the
co. will ever get that pellucid stream out. Warrandyte
folk have been waiting for the flooding. The co. has a
shaft on a dot of an island in the Yarra, and another on
the bank, down about 120 ft. Stopes run from one shaft
to the other, and men were driving at 110 ft. under the
worked-out ground. The general idea is that the blast-
ing opened up the rock under the Yarra ; the mine ex-
planation is that a concrete dome put in the island shaft
to keep out the water did not fulfil its duty. Look-
ing at the matter cold-bloodedly, the men in the
drives at 110 ft. can thank their stars that no' bad
inrush took place. If it had they would have been caught
like rats in a trap. The mining inspector’s obvious duty
is to insist, if the mine is unwatered, that only first-class
work is put in to stop any inflow. The risk to men’s
lives is too great to permit anything else. The Board
was slow in letting out that the mining inspector had
come down thud ; and its reticence was punished by
the slashing attack made on the scrip when the secret
came out.
* * * *
New Dempsey’s, Gaffney’s (Vic.), has its
mascotte again in the original Nolan. Every mahn of ’em
swore that the old skipper was needed to bring luck back
to the mine, and so he has ! Directly he got to Dempsey’s
the reef began to widen in the old shaft as, indade,
was only dacent of it. Moreover, gold came
into the stone to smile him welcome, the
creek ran high, but was also dacent enough to kape out
of the shaft, and so ivery mother’s son of the shareholders
is wearing a green ribbon and a smile as broad as that of
Gaffney’s itself. Nolan deserves to have luck. He has
stuck to Dempsey’s since Wood’s Point was in its prime.
There was nothing jerry-built about him. He went there
to stay, and stay he did till he hit it rich when Dempsey’s
jumped to the front a couple of years back. Men like
Nolan are the salt of mining, and here’s to him and to his
mine !
* * * *
A world of moralising might be indulged in
over the last sentence in the six-monthly report made by
W. A. Mason, mine manager of the Band and Loch, Bal-
larat: —“In conclusion, I may say that the mine is
looking fairly well, and if we are successful in locating
payable stone in the 2300 ft. level, as appearances at pre-
sent indicate, your mine should shortly give a good
account of itself.” With this statement may be bracketed
a characteristically Ballarat paragraph in the directors’
report: “ The large area of ground .. . north of the pre-
sent workings warrants the management in feeling con-
fident that when the shoot of gold (2300 ft.) is met with,
the full length of same will be found within the com-
pany’s boundary.” From these statements, the in-
genuous might deduce' that the co. had at some
time cheered the soul of shareholders with dividends
from some shoot of gold that was making down to 2300 ft.
But while the mine has produced gold worth £116,000,
and has taken £44,000 cash from calls, there isn’t any
item on the other side of the balance-sheet in respect to
dividends paid. Even the last half-year’s work makes
up poorly, in spite of the * fact that the
manager talks of “ highly-payable stone” in the
crosscut off the air winch shaft, at 1970 ft. level; of “ good
payable stone ” south of the south rise, at the 2080 ft.
level ; of “payable” stone in the western ground, between
the 2000 ft. and 2080 ft, levels. Crushing's went 1884 oz.
from 3026 tons, and cost £6lll to win ; so there was a
profit of £l6OO. But prospecting had to be carried on
which cost £3047. Figures like these prove that the mine
is as disappointing as ever, and that there is nothing more
tangible at the 2300 ft. level than a six-months-old un-
realised expectation of picking up a payable shoot of
ground north.
* * * *
The luckiest hit near Ballarat of recent years is
the Birthday, Berringa. On under£2ooo called-up capital
it paid nearly £140,000 in divs. As 1000 ft. was reached
the stone took on the poverty of the district at that depth,
and the outlook became as blue as G. H. Reid’s chances
ought to be. Co.’s horizon was smudged still more by
the failure to pick up stone in the east crosscut at 400 ft.,
put out to cut the Fancy lode. As a forlorn hope,
driving in this crosscut was continued, and at 1034 ft. a
big formation was met. Whether this is the Fancy or
the Surprise lode farther east no one ventures to guess,
as the Williams’ Fancy is a mile to the north, and south
nothing has been done. Some nice specimens coming to
hand, the market got an old-time “buck” going.
Obviously the advance was a pure bit of gambling that
has not deceived anyone. The stone has to be opened
on to prove its worth, for it has always to be borne in
mind that at Berringa a few gold-bearing specimens do
not make a mine. It’s the bulk that tells.

“Yack”: Bulletin (17/5/’O6) under-estimates,
I think, the chances of Fletcher’s dredge, the first bucket-
dredge to start in Yackandandah Creek. Present position
is a large sludge dam, and I believe it will prove the
poorest part of the lease. For a mile up, the creek is all
soft granite bottom ; and, in spite of all talk to the con-
trary, the dredge is paying. Labor required is very small
—two men and a boy per shift.
* * * *
Read it as you like, the report of F. W. Payne,
the Maoriland dredging engineer, does not help the Tas-
manian tin-dredging shows at all. Stripping off the
excuses, Payne declares that the Gladstone dredge is a
failure, badly constructed and incapable of doing good
work. The Ringarooma is passed over with a light hand,
possibly because work has been too small to disclose its
defects. It is no use making the excuse about the indus-
try being a new one, and that unlooked-for things
occurred. Local doubt as to the ability of the dredges to
work the ground was openly expressed, and almost every
tin-miner in and out of the State was dubious about the
saving of the tin even if the dredge could raise the dirt.
So there was a fair field of criticism to help and
warn promoters. The public was with the Gladstone
promoters at the start, so that there was no trouble about
cash. But the dredge bought was too light, the result
being almost a collapse. Payne may talk about tin-
dredging having come to stay in Tasmania, but if three
months do not see a radical change for the better, there
will be some very willing scalp-hunting in the neighbor-
hood of Collins-street.
* * * *
Wyniford (Tas.) is feeling the strain of a second
advent. Co. once owned a tin claim on the Wyniford
River, over which many egg-shell castles were built.
When it came to yields, the castles crumpled. Co. there-
fore moved on to the Frome River, but in doing so had to
repair its castle with the proceeds of 9000 forfeited shares
sold at 2d. each. New manager was once with the
Krushas, and his sampling of the lease told of as rich
tin as was ever intended to be got by the Gladstone
dredge. He thus set the standard to be lived up to, and
also provided a market value, based on anticipation.
Already the co. has had a Black Thursday, when such
mysterious selling took place as to raise grave doubts as
to whether the new property is much better than
the old. Friday brought no relief to the strain,
despite official assurances that everything was right.
Saturday disclosed that a marble bar had come in in
the face. Speckers issued so sad a wail that it stirred
even the Melb. Stock Exchange committee to do some-
thing. What the committee learnt or did can, as usual
in such cases, be very well described as x. There really
was nothing to investigate. In shallow ground some high
rock had been uncovered, and the yield was expected to
suffer. If criticism had to be applied, it was to men who
boosted shares that ought never to have got above 2d.
Then if additional censure has to be given, it is to those
who bought before yields could speak.

Australian tin mining co.’s have a way of
giving assays of tin ore in units of black tin or tin oxide,
and this requires a deduction of 25 per cent, or more for
dross, according to the purity or cleanness of the oxide ;
and that is just where the estimated return gets all
astray. And so does the punter. “ Don’t report man-
ager’s estimate, as yield of tin is unstreamed and greatly
disappointing ” was wired from the Weldbrook Tin Co.
(Tas.). It was a repetition of the Briseis Extended
bungled return last year. Now there is the case of Vul-
can Hope (N.Q.), whose “black tin” gave only 24 per
cent, of white or metallic tin ; so that “4 per cent.” in
that case would have really meant something under 1 per
cent, of the stuff that has been selling for £2OO a ton or
so lately. Another familiar instance of the way
tin assays are reported comes by wire from Smith’s
Creek (Q.): “Ore from bottom of prospecting shaft
assays 2‘3 per cent.; ” but wire does not say 2'3 oxide of
tin, or sulphide, or metal. Possibly it is a mixture of
oxide with sulphide of tin ; for the poor recoveries made
by the mill last year were explained in that way. Not
that the explanation is adequate; for if 2 - 3 per cent, of pure
oxide of tin is in the ore, an excess of sulphide would not
matter. There is only one correct way of giving assays
of tin ore —that is, to give the return in units of metallic
tin, with the proportion of tin sulphide that may happen
to be contained in the ore “ 2'3 per cent.” contains no
information.
* * • •
According to the admission of one of their num-
ber, some of the trustees of St. Hilda Cemetery (Vic.) have
been carrying on a profitable little game for some
years past. The trustees have been accused by Dr.
Norris, chairman of the Board of Health, of abusing
their positions by trafficking in graves for their per-
sonal profit, and one of those affected has given an
explanation, which mainly goes to confirm the charges.
He says that Norris was correct in his facts, but did not
take the circumstances into consideration. He explains
that the trustees, as a trust, were in the habit of allowing
members of the trust, as individuals, and also employees
of the cemetery, to select graves at a price lower than
was charged to the general public. Some of them took
advantage of this privilege, and subsequently sold
the graves at a higher figure. The interviewee
appeared to regard this action as above all suspicion,
seeing that the person who obtained the grave from the
trustee only paid the amount he would have paid had he
approached the officials in the first instance. That the
trustee obtained the advantage of the reduced price, and
was able to pocket the difference, which should properly
have gone into the funds of the cemetery, didn’t seem to
strike him as at all improper, and he finished up with an
assertion that there was “ nothing underhand about the
transaction. Everything was fair and above board.” The
affairs of the St. Hilda cemetery have lately come under
public notice, owing to the hurried departure of the
secretary for parts unknown, and numerous allegations
are flying round. It is asserted, among other things, that
the cemetery gardeners spent much of their time in the
private gardens of the trustees, which is an interesting
item if true.
A popular yarn with the hang-the-expense poli-
tician in Westralia is that, owing- to the increase of popu-
lation, the debt is shrinking; which, of course, means
that there really isn’t any reason why they shouldn’t tap
somebody for a "trifle more. It is a fact that the Westralian
debt per head is a bit less than it was, chiefly because
there are more babies’ heads than there used to be before
the t’othersider took “the missus and the kids” over.
The revenue per head therefore puts the whole show
away. In 1902 it was £lB 15s. lOd. ; and from that level
it has gene on sliding away till last year it was only
£l6 9s. Bd., and this year it can’t well be more than
£l5 15s.—in fact, for the first two-thirds of the year it
was only at the rate of £l4 19s. per head per annum.
Therefore the debt per head keeps growing larger in
proportion to the revenue per head, and in a country
where money, not heads, are currency, that is a serious
matter.

George Musgrove’s Grand Opera Co. shares are
about ready to be pushed off—2ooo of them at £2 each,
nobody to have more than 10. The scheme works this
way: If there is a profit, Mr. Musgrove is to take
half of it (Mr. Musgrove is to be manager, and,
presumably, will therefore practically decide what the
profits shall be); if there is a loss, the co. is to bear it.
By way of bonus, shareholders are to be entitled to apply
for tickets for the operas ahead of the crowd. As a plan
for securing grand opera in Australia, the scheme is all
right; judged as an investment, it is, of course, absurd.

Aerial tramways or conveyors undoubtedly
afford the cheapest means of transport, especially when
they are worked under a system which is entirely
automatic. The largest manufacturers in the world in
this particular line are Adolf Bleichert and Co., of London,
represented in Australia by W. and J. Lempriere, of Mel-
bourne. Delivering ore automatically from the mine to
the mill over almost any distance at a cost of about one
penny per ton, is far ahead of all other means of transport.
Automatic systems are in vogue for the cheap discharge
of tailings. Automatic weighing and counting of loaded
cars is also assured under Bleichert’s system, thereby
nothing is left to assumption or imagination.*

The New Havillah Gold Mining Company has
just installed a high lift pump to make all necessary pro-
vision against any increase in the volume of water now
that they are opening up large areas of wash dirt. This
installation is in addition to the large three throw pump
which has given such high efficiency and such constant
service since its inception. The new pump is of the high
lift turbine type, electrically driven, and is capable of
lifting 2,016,000 gallons of water in 24 hours against a
total head of 350 feet. The mechanical efficiency of the
pump is guaranteed at 71‘5 per cent, and all repairs and
replacements apart from undue wear are likewise
guaranteed for twelve months. The pump has four
runners and four vanes made from best bronze and the
shaft is attached by means of a flexible coupling to a
Ganz three phase motor 440 volts pressure, 60 cycles
capable of giving 207 h.p. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have executed this con-
tract. This Company has had the manufacture of most
of the big volume deep alluvial lead pumping schemes in
Victoria and has met in each with unqualified success.*

Electric haulage is one of the best paying in-
vestments for every mine in which the wash-dirt has to be
hauled from a greater distance than 2000 feet. The actual
saving effected at the Berry Consols Extended Mine,
Allendale, amounts to £3O per week at a distance of 3000
feet; at the Great Southern Consols Mine, Rutherglen, it
runs into £45 per week, at a distance of 5000 feet; in both
instances the necessary plant which was installed to
replace the manual labor has cost but £l2OO. Experience
has proved that at a distance of 4000 feet electric haulage
cost a quarter of that of horse traction and a tenth of hand
trucking. The Charlotte Plains and New Havillah Mines
at Moolort are the latest to adopt electric haulage, and
have again proved it to be highly satisfactory. The favor-
able results obtained with these installations are un-
doubtedly due in a great measure to the reliable and
smooth operation of the electric apparatus, especially the
locomotives adopted, which were all supplied and erected
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, Limited, corner
of Hanna and Kavanagh-streets, South Melbourne, agents
for Ganz and Company, Budapest, Hungary. This firm has
devoted special attention to mine haulage problems, and
during their 14 years’ experience have brought up their
mine locomotives and other haulage apparatus to the
highest degree of perfection.* [2.]
George D. Meudell, Money and Mining Broker,
A.M.P. Building, Bendigo (formerly of Stock Exchange of
Melbourne).*

Aboriginalities.
“Janor”: Herewith a plea of not guilty
of the “ bloomer ” charged against me by “Kul-
fara ” (B. 17/5/’O6) re the sky scraper of the
white ant; likewise the “perforated mounds” that
adorn the landscape in my district are similar to
those in other parts of the continent. Further-
more, “Kulfara’s” billy-boiling yarn is quite
true. The explanation is that many white ant
nests are built around and over the stumps of
long dead trees, and have thus a wooden core. The
termites excavate galleries in this core, but do
not entirely destroy it, and in course of time it
becomes so dry that it w’ill burn like matchwood.
It is necessary to make the holes in the mound
as described by “Kulfara”—otherwise no com-
bustion ensues. When the core is burnt out the
fire expires, and leaves the earthy portion of the
nest (the portion which is the work of the ter-
mite) intact. Any bushman who uses his eyes
may see scores of anthills lying about the coun-
try, that have been thus gutted by bush fires,
simply because they had been tunneled by the
ant-eater. It is impossible to burn an
ant hill that has a sound skin; and
the insects are quite aware of that fact.
Punch a, hole in one and they put on three shifts
until it is built over. Knew a party of prospec-
tors on a Gippsland creek who, having a quantity
of explosives not in use, dug a hole in an ant hill
close to their hut and placed the stuff inside. A
few days later the record bush fire of the district
swooped down on their camp. They made heroic
attempts to save the hut, but found they were
playing a losing game. One of them rushed to
the anthill to seize the “dinny” and throw it
into the creek, but found the opening closed.
“ Never mind the hut, chaps,” he yelled, “this
ant-bed will blow up directly.” And they tarried
not. Returning a few days later, they found the
bulk of the scenery ashes, but the improvised
magazine still raised its topknot proudly over the
blackened ruins, and, after they had built a new
hut, they dug out the explosives and found them
not even singed.
* * * *
Young Australia and its mother had an
exciting scramble with Death in Moyhu (Vic.)
list month. Seven-year-old Lily Lewis fell into
an 80ft. well with 30ft. of water in it. Lily’s
mother put 15-year-old Harry Share into a bucket
(apparently she just borrowed Harry for the occa-
sion), and had lowered him less than halfway
down when the windlass got away from her, and
Harry and the bucket went down with a rush.
But Harry wasn't drowned, and Lily’s skull wasn’t
split by the fall of Harry and the bucket, and
Mrs. Lewis and her sister succeeded in hauling
the youngsters up —Harry clinging to the chain
of the bucket, and Lily coiled round Harry’s legs.
And the only damage done was the bruising of
Mrs. Lewis by the runaway windlass-handle.

“ Paul Cupid ” : The practice of calling
the same snake by different names in different
places often leads to argument, sin, and even
strife, not to speak of incivility and procrastina-
tion. Once heard a Tasmanian explaining how,
only for much brandy, ligatures, scarifying,
suction and a doctor, he would have been called
to his reward after being bitten by a diamond
snake. A New South Welshman casually re-
marked that the bite of this snake was as
harmless as a dead land-agent, and
hinted that the Tasmanian wouldn’t
know’ the truth even if the
Angel Gabriel blew it in
his ear with a trumpet.
War was declared and
a splendid battle
ensued. When the
last of the fur-
V. i 4- r.
niture was
broken I
started t o
explain that
they were
both right—
that the so-
called dia-
mond snake
of Tasmania i
is eminent- J
ly venom- j
ous, and I
that the J
harmless '!■
diamond
snake of
N.S.W. is a
totally differ-
ent serpent.
The combatants
called me
several kinds of
a fool for not
making the
explanation
sooner. I
meekly admit-
ted they were
both right
again, but that
I had vainly
tried to make
my voice heard
above the
shouts, curses
and breakages.
I apologised for
being a fool,
and so warded
off annihilation.
This anomaly in
names is evi-
dent again in
the carpet and
the black
snakes. In Tas-
mania they have a reptile that in some parts is
called the black snake, and in others the carpet.
This fellow is identical with our tiger snake. The
duplication of names is responsible for as much
wasted energy in argument, “language,” and
physical violence as would, if properly applied,
build an Australian battleship.

“T.D.,” from London :
They have just installed a young emu out at ’Appy
’Ampstead, near the Old Bull and Bush, and I went over
the other day to give him my salutations and feed him
up on a handful of iron nails and other delicacies. He
seemed tickled to death with the comments made upon
him by intelligent Cockneydom. Some savant was
gravely explaining to an admiring family, and to all and
sundry, precisely which of the bird’s feathers were used
for the hats beloved of ’Arriet. He said they folded up
just under the wings, and the smooth portions that could
be seen were just the ends, and they had to be adroitly
curled by the feather merchants before they were fit for
market. Then somebody informed him that tlie fowl wasn’t
an ostrich at all, because he had seen a picture of one
in a paper, which called it an “ Hemu.” But the learned
Cockney was not to be made an object of doubt and
possible scorn in the eyes of his family. “ Whv,” he pro-
tested, “I thought any schoolboy in these days’’ud know
that a hostrich was a hemu and a hemu was a hostrich.”
As the other fellow wasn’t quite sure of his ground lie
accepted the information.

“ Gip " : Be snakes and their fondness for
milk. A little while back, I came into possession
of two very tiny orphan kit-
tens. Placed them in a flat
box on the hut verandah,
under the bedroom window.
Regularly I filled a saucer
with fresh milk, and regu-
larly the milk disajipeared.
Yet my kittens did not
thrive. On the fourth day I
found one dead, on the sixth
the other died. I’m certain
I stuffed enough milk down
their little throats with a
spoon to keep them alive.
Also the saucer was emptv.
Having no cat or dog about
the place, I grew suspicious,
and a Bulletin par suggested
snakes. So filling the saucer
again, and arming myself
with a stick, I waited
developments. I was just
giving it up when I heard a
slight rustle in the sweet
peas. A snake —an ugly
brute, 6ft.-long or thereabout
—glode noiselessly on to the
verandah. I waited until he
had almost reached the box,
and then, with the stick
grasped firmly, and my
teeth cutting into my lower
lip, I leant out of the win-
dow. The brute saw me.
He seemed to resent the
stick, and flattened his head
, sickenmgly. They say here he
must have been a tiger. With a gasp and a yell
1 dropped the stick and ran till 1 fell down up-
setting all the furniture in my flight. And now I
see snakes everywhere.

“ Krex ” writes from Far Out :
“C.X.G.”, re Cooper’s Creek (B. 29/3/’O6), earns dis-
respect tor an unreliable correspondent. What sort of an
exclamation would he have made had he known that
Cooper’s Creek, in ordinary floods, spreads nearly 100
miles wide, instead of 50, as he says? In 1898 a flood
went over the bar below Innamihcka, and ran the
Strzlecki Creek for over 100 miles.
Innamincka Station is not built on a high sand-ridge—
it can scarcely be called a ridge on account of its low-
ness. Still the water runs on both sides when a flood of
any consequence happens along, before sweeping over
the place the station is built upon, so I suppose there is
some semblance of a ridge. But even in the record flood
of 1906 the water left an island less than 200yds. from the
station Oft. above water-level, and that island is the real
ridge. Finally, Cooper’s Creek at Innamincka Station
was only about a mile wide, even at the height of the 1906
flood.
I have just ridden from Innamincka to Boulia (Queens-
land), a distance of 450 miles, and the country is practi-
cally a carpet of grass, in most places so thickly matted
that the soil is invisible. The old hands, not to be out-
done, say they have seen the grass as good, but as to
when they are not at all agreed.
The late heavy rains played skittles with the fences;
along Barrow’s Creek they are completely obliterated.
On the S.A. side of the rabbit-proof fence there are num-
bers of rabbits to be seen ; on the Queensland side I did
not see a sign of one—though there are some, I suppose.

The reputation of a wealthy member of
the Victorian squattocracv for kindness and
humane sentiments has been seriously damaged
by a recent event. Jumb, the squattocrat, owed
the excellent opinion which the surrounding dis-
trict entertained towards him to his generous
treatment of Old Bill. Old Bill was a tall,
venerable, hairy slouch, who had worked about
the Jumb homestead for some years. When he
became too old and rheumatic for any kind of
graft, he was permitted to remain in a log hut in
the home paddock, and he drew his rations from
the kitchen, and loafed about in the boss’s cast-
off clothes. He was a silent, morose sort of
rapscallion, who rarely washed, and had a hatred
of combs and other toilet requisites. Other
squatters would have turned Old Bill adrift, but
Jumb had too much heart for that, and although
the veteran was not a pleasant object for visitors
to stumble across, he was not cast aside. This
was remembered and counted unto Jumb for
righteousness up to the time of Bill’s death, which
happened the other day. Then, through an
inadvertence, it was discovered that Old Bill was
Mrs. Jumb’s father. Now the surrounding dis-
trict has taken its good opinions all back, and
there is talk of entering theJumbs for the mean
man and economical woman competition.
* * * *
“ A.P.R.” : The emu is certainly a stupid
bird, and a clumsy one, too. Its awkward,
meandering stride and senseless curiosity suggest
some quaint survival of prehistoric days. Its
idea of sport would be ridiculous, if it were not so
cruel. I have often seen a mob of emus careering
about amongst a flock of ewes and lambs, driving
them to distraction, and separating the lambs
from their mothers. These lambs either die of
starvation or have their eyes pecked out by crows,
and their piteous bleating through the solitary
bush awakes intense contempt amongst bushmen
for the clumsy, elephantine bird whose sport
means death. If we want a national bird, let it
be the picturesque brolga, with its romantic dance
at break of day, and its sweet trumpet call, and
its flight high up among the clouds. That is a
bird.
A girl who slaved behind the lace counter
of a leading Sydney frill establishment answered
an advt.—the usual sort of advt.—which appeared
in a Sydney daily a couple of years ago. He was
a wealthy planter down in the South Sea Islands,
and they corresponded some months, and finally
exchanged photographs. The photo, she re-
ceived was that of a middle-aged man, not
bad to look at. After more correspondence,
the planter sent a substantial draft to
buy the trousseau and a steamer ticket, accom-
panied by messages of undying affection. The
girl proudly exhibited the likeness and some of
the ardent letters to an admiring circle, and
spread a romantic rumor that her adorer had
fallen in love with her when quite a little girl,
and, now that he had climbed to affluence, wanted
her to come and be queen of his island. After a
cheerful “send-off” she got aboard with her
trousseau and wedding presents, and sailed
away. Upon arrival at the island the planter
came on board to claim his bride. He came
alongside in a canoe pulled by two semi-
naked islanders. His costume consisted of a
singlet-shirt arrangement that came to his knees
and exposed a pair of thin, bony shins and a
nightmare of claw-like toes. He was old and
grizzled, also somewhat unclean, and, in addition,
had lost most of his nose. The distracted girl
cried to the captain to save her from the Horror,
and the Horror flared up and ordered
her to get into the canoe, as he had paid
for her and wanted the goods. Finally
a missionary and his wife came aboard and took
her beneath their collective wing; and the jilted,
noseless one, with his shirt fluttering in the
breeze, was rowed off threatening to sue the
weeping girl for fraud, breach of promise, false
pretences, and numerous other crimes. Subse-
quently she drifted back to Sydney with her box
of wedding presents and a shattered illusion, and
is now filling a niche behind another lace counter
in another city. The planter’s offer is still open,
and probably his book-keeper will write more
ardent, luring letters to other romantic damsels.
At any rate, the old advertisement, wailing for a
plump, comely damsel to come and be his queen,
again appeared in an Australian daily not long
ago.

“A.P.R.”: Be over-pubbed townships.
The hamlet of Bugaldie (N.S.W.) should be a
first favorite, for it consists of two hotels and a
school-house. As a rule, with closer settlement
the old shanties disappear. Ten years ago, on
the 65-mile mail route between Gunnedan and
Coonabarabran, there were two hotels and three
wine-shops; now, one hotel and one wine-shop.
Along the main routes out back one continually
sees ruins of old shanties that have not been
supplanted by newer drunkeries. Most of these
buildings are inhabited by spectres that walk
about carrying their heads underneath their arms.
“Dux” : At Hokitika (West Coast, M.L.)
I struck a Bulletin par re over-pubbed towns,
and immediately resolved to enter Westland for
the belt. Hokitika (1200 people) boasts 23 pubs.;
Westport (2800) has 22; Kumara (1500), 18;
whilst Ahaura, a town of 12 families, has six
pubs., and within a radius of four miles there are
eight other hotels.

saw a free fight—in which many heads would have
been laid open only the New Guinea skull is too
solid —as to which crowd had the most claim to a
rather long-deceased alligator. Man, woman or
baby cometh not amiss, once you get inland.
Civilisation, however, is bringing its advantages,
and for an extremely high tin of
“ bulamakau ” (meat) the average
Papuan would gladly give a
short mortgage over his soul—or
his womankind.

“Plevna” heaves an unusual brick at
“ The Man with the Hoe ” :
“The Man with the Hoe” (B. 17/5/’O6) represents the
born hoe-wielder. There are any number of him here
in Australia to-day. They are simply born like that—the
easiest way is the best way with them every time. Bobby
Burns was a man with the hoe; but he knew how to
wield it. Abe Lincoln, and dozens of others—the hoe
didn’t get them down. Have working for me three
samples, brought up on farms. Should a swingle-bar
break, horses are unyoked, taken home, and Edgar, or
Leslie, or Ivan sits on the fence, waiting for the boss to
come along. Often as not the boss makes shift out of a
sapling. They never think of tightening a loose bolt ;
their first knowledge of anything wrong is a breakage.
They will be “men with the hoe” till the end of their
days. And who will be to blame? Nobody but them-
selves. They won’t think ; and the man who won’t think
must hoe. Sometimes he must hoe anyhow ; but that’s
another matter.
■» * * *
“Ku-Klux”: Be trapping (“Elmore,”
B. 17/5/'O6). For the information of “Elmore”
and any other inquiring aborigine, let me say
that I have often trapped black and tiger snakes
in Gippsland. In 1902, just outside of Drouin, I
found a black snake, 4ft. 9in. long, caught firmly
in two traps, which I had set close together at two
openings of a fence. He had evidently lashed
round on being snapped by the head and sprung
the other trap, some 3ft. away, with his tail. It
was no uncommon thing to catch goannas and
kookaburras.
GO ON THE LAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Eve had eaten fruit she didn’t ought,
And v/ith a woman’s usual luck been caught,
This as her punishment did God command :
“ Go on the Land ! ”
She drifted to the saltbush plains and ploughed—
No other recreation was allowed.
Thus was the primal immigration planned
About the land.
The serpent shared the fate of Mother Eve,
A footless horror he did Eden leave.
Accurst upon his stomach did he stand,
Upon the land.
After some toilsome years there came the Flood
Which mixed all Closer Settlement with mud ;
The rain supply exceeded the demand
Once on the land.
The law holds good through all the aeons flown,
At least, so far as any facts are known ;
So why not heed the exhortation and
Go on the land ?
Sniff at these verses, critic, if you will;
Sooner or later, all some ground must fill,
One day, e’en you will join the dusty band
Upon the land.
vie.
BAMDOO.

“Kaban”: Recent discussion in the
columns of The Bulletin over the derivation of
“shicker” draws attention to the cosmopolitan
nature of slang. Very few slang words have their
origin in Australia. Even “barracking,” which
the late Professor Morris was at such pains to
derive from the aboriginal “borah,” is more
probably akin to the old English slang “barrikin,”
which in turn comes from the French. Such
distinctly Australian terms as “swag” and
“damper” are Australian only in their appli-
cation. “Billy” certainly does seem to have
originated in this country, but “diggings” was
used by an English writer before Australia was
dreamt of. The larrikin’s term “push” comes
from an English prison, while “stoush” is a
slight modification in sound and meaning of the
English slang ‘ ‘ stash, ” to stop. About ‘ ‘ larrikin ”
itself sufficient has been said and written, although
it may be noted that “lary,” commonly supposed
to be a contraction of “larrikin,” is evidently,
from its pronunciation, the English thieves
“leary.” “Mash” is from the Romany of the
gipsy. “Donah” is from the Spanish, to which
language also we doubtless owe the recently-
introduced “ bonzer,” most probably a corruption
of “bonanza.” Ancient Greek has given us “spon-
dulix” as a slang term for money, and “tug”
comes from the Latin. “Prog” for food is
Greek; “lam” or “lambast” (to beat) is Latin;
and so on.
THE GREATER HALF.
UNCLE : “I hear yer gain to get married, Ned. How are yer
goin' to manage ? Yer can't earn enough to keep yerself yet."
