“ Itan” : Is there a town in the world with
a shorter name than 0.K., situated in Banana-
land? [The Bulletin suggests certain Chinese
towns—Ho in Kan-su province, and Yu and Ju
in Ko-nan. They are shorter because of the fact
that a small letter takes up less space than a capi-
tal. If rivers are eligible, the Y deserves an
honorable place for brevity.]
“ Houseabout ” : During
the big drought I was grafting
for a Victorian squatter. He
kept about 300 broken-mouthed
merino ewes in one of his pad-
docks, dry red volcanic soil with
a shallow clay-pan in one cor-
ner, fence sheep-proof, and no
other water within three
miles. Beginning of
September the water
failed, and not a
shower fell until the
middle of the follow-
ing April, nearly
seven scorehing
months. Sheep were
only watered twice
during that time,
yet 70 per cent,
of them pulled
through.
“ Darius ” : I was yarning with an old
cookie, the other day, and asked him what sort of
men he usually got to assist in getting in his
crops. “ Well,” said he, “ two saisons ago I had
three beautiful min from the Ould Dart. They
didn’t ait very much, and they wor always
workin’. Anny one of thim cud take a bag of
whate under aich arum and walk off wid it.
Dacent-tongued min they wor, too, and cud dis-
coorse on anny subject. But lasht saison I was
ruined,' horse, fut, and artillery. I had three
spalpeens from Sydney. They wor allways aitin’.
They wouldn’t work, and they cudn’t talk. All
they cud say was, ‘Bybuck,’ ‘Straight wire,’ and
‘ Bli’me ’.”
“Hiphen” : In the
bad old days, when Dayles-
ford (Vic.) was better
known as the Jim Crow
Diggings, a large Italian
colony settled there. It is
there now, bub merely in
name. Its descendants are
Australians —and good ones
at that. The names remain,
but everywhere are big blue-
eyed men, whose front desig-
nations are Bob, Jim, and
Bill, but whose surnames
end in “etti.” The pub-
keepers are Rolettis, Sper-
macettis, Garelettis. A
Garibaldi sells the bread, a
Spinozzari dispenses meat,
while most of the dogs
answer to the name of
Carlo. I was thither re-
cently with some pals, when
we happened across a
euchre party and dance.
We were debating as to the
advisability of parting with
our bobs, when the M.C.
informed us that the belle of
the district, one Giulia
Vanezetti, was inside. Be-
fore my eyes rose a vision of
some dark-skinned Italian
signorina, with flashing eyes
and a poniard concealed in
her corsage (whatever that
may be). In three seconds
iny bob was down and the
M.C. was intro-
ducing me. She was a
typical Australian (not that
she was any the worse for
that), with blue eyes and
the fairest of fair hair.
From among the ruins of my
romantic visions, I mumbled
something feeble. “Wishcher
meant it ! ” laughed the sig-
norina Giulia Vanezetti.
They wouldn’t even give me
my bob back, and I couldn’t
find a soul there who took
the least interest in the gon-
dolas of Venice.
• * *
Our black sister Mary
is learning a thing or two.
At Angledool (N.S.W.), the
other day, an aborigine lady
named Mina proceeded, un-
der the Infants’ Protection Act, against a casual
white person for leaving his illegitimate daughter
Emma without means. Certainly, after the P.M.
had nailed up the doors and windows and heard
the evidence in private, he turned little Emma
out with no more means than she had before ; and
that sort of failure is liable to discourage our
black sister.

“ C.X.G.” (from up among the northern
sand of S. Australia): A flock of 1200 Angora
goats passed through here last week on their way
to market at Adelaide. The flock was in charge
of a white man, accompanied by three niggers;
camp equipment was on two camels. But the
feature of the procession was a kangaroo dog,
owned by the white man, which had been brought
up from a pup with the flock, and understood all
their little differences. When the argument
between any two Williams got to butts, the dog
would move up quietly and seize one of the com-
batants by the hmd leg, and the argument would
be left for another time. The flock came
from the Peake station, and were those bred
up by the late E. O. Kempe, of that place.
The first Angoras brought into S. Aus. were
imported by the late Price Maurice from the
Cape in the early 70’s —eight nannies and two
Wms., and cost £4O a head. They were un-
trucked at Tarlee, and taken from there to
Pekina station in a horse dray, a distance of
about ICO miles. P. Maurice made a great
attempt to establish them firmly in that district,
even going to the length of taking away from his
shepherds their common goats, killing them, and
giving them Angoras instead. They were not at
all popular with the shepherds, however, as they
are very poor milkers, giving only enough for
their own young. From Pekina the flock was
shifted to Fourth Creek, another estate of
Maurice’s, where they were bred up for a number
of years. When the owner died, the goats were
sold in due course to realise the estate, and
portion of them were purchased by Kempe, who
raised the present flock at Peake station, near
Warrina, in the far north of S.A.
* * * •
“D.S.M.”: Ran across a traveller the
other day humping a Matilda almost as big as
himself. In fact, it was within a pound or so of
his own weight; when weighed it tipped the scale
at 1211bs. Carrying a swag like that is no lazy
work, yet this man was offered a job and refused
it. After a deal of persuasion he was induced to
show me the contents of Matilda, which included
a boot-last (weight about 81bs.), a hammer, two
chisels, four suits of clothes, an overcoat, prayer-
book, two old Bulletins, knives, pipes, and
various sundries, all of which had seen better
days. He even carried an alarm clock and
an old pair of lady's corsets. He maintained a
strict silence as to his intentions with regard to
the corsets, but I believe they were honor-
able. Some time back I met a man with
just such a swag, but he had his load packed on
two dogs, and all he carried was himself and his
pipe. His two billy-cans were hanging round the
dogs’ necks. “A good idea,” he said. The
dogs seemed to think differently.
• # • •
“ Gip ” : Is it usual for “ English ” goose-
berries to grow wild in parts of Australia ? Up
here, near the Snowy, where the Federal capital
is going to be, there is a mountain side, rough and
stony and far away from any homestead, that is
covered with the fruit. Creamy clematis in lovely
profusion clings to the bushes, and it is by tearing
this aside that the finest fruit is got at. About
Christmas-time the gooseberries ripen. They are
large and deliciously sweet, and thousands of
parrots and other birds feed on them. The oldest
and stupidest inhabitant doesn’t know how they
got there.
IN THE GOD-FOBSAKEN WEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Have you seen the latest papers, read the god-forsaken
news
That I’m travelling down the river with some god-forsaken
ewes?
Meeting god-forsaken bagmen tramping round the dismal
land,
With a god-forsaken billy in my god-forsaken hand ;
Sewing god-forsaken patches on my long-neglected pants,
Breathing nasty brimstone language on the god-forsaken
ants;
Calling at the Mulga stations on the god-forsaken mend,
Catching god-forsaken crawfish in the god-forsaken bend
While the god-forsaken squatter, with a god-forsaken dog,
Hunts the god-forsaken rabbits from the god-forsaken
log,
And his god-forsaken wethers bog and shuffle in the
tanks,
And his god-forsaken mortgage keeps on growing at the
banks;
Past the “ hardy, bronzed selector,” making god-forsaken
tracks
For the god-forsaken lawyer to raise money on his stacks ;
Read the god-forsaken papers, kill the god-forsaken flies,
While the station boundary riders tell me god-forsaken
lies
Of their god-forsaken horses and about their station girls,
And their god-forsaken beauty, till my kokum fairly
whirls.
But I think this news is musty in the god-forsaken West,
So I think I’d better give it a long, god-forsaken rest.
I could tell you more about it in this god-forsaken rhyme,
But I haven’t, on my honor, got the god-forsaken time.
N.B.W.
WALFRE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
One of the Bucolic Persons: There is one
weak point in your argument. The high rates on manu-
factured goods are paid by the local consumer, but he
only pays on what he uses. The loss caused by charging
unprofitably low rates on farm produce is a loss not only
on what we consume, but on what we export. In other
words, we are taxed to pay an indirect bonus on exported
wheat, so that it may be exported cheaply enough to
stand about lid. a bushel freight and other charges to
England and still compete in the English market. Of
course, while the manufacturing population that buys it
is in England instead of here, that can’t be helped, but
it makes the agricultural industry a parasite none the
less. You say that yourself when you allege : “If the
charge was the same on wheat as on machinery there
would be no wheat to carry.” The wheat industry, you
say, depends on this indirect bonus—which Tub Bul-
letin objects to mainly because it is Protection put
on by Freetraders who lie about it and deny its
existence, and because the progress of the assisted
wheat industry is always quoted as showing how
an industry can rise under Freetrade conditions
George 11. : Tns Bulletin’s words were: “ The repay-
ment of the publio debt is the transfer from private
capitalists to the people of all Australia of some
£235,000,000 of property yielding an annual income
of over £8,300,000.” And The Bulletin can see no
reason why that repayment should be impossible.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is definitely announced now that
Kingston will seek re-election at the next Federal
contest.
A Queensland official document shows that
H. Beak recently leased 28 square miles in Hades.
He can have them as far as this paper is concerned.
If a new Judge is appointed to the Federal
High Court this session, the choice will probably
rest among four men—lsaacs, Wise, Higgins, and
Inglis Clarke.
Over £6OO is now in hand as a testimonial
to the Owe’Sullivan, N.S.W. ex-Ministcr for
Works. If you are going to Face the Dawn,
you can do it much more confidently with £6OO
than without.
SENATOR DAWSON.
Mr. Francis Beresford McMahon, actuary
of the Hobart Savings Bank, retires on pension,
with 52 years continuous service to his credit.
About 37 successive officials like that would have
seen a bank all through the Christian era.
W. H. Duncan, M.L.A. of S.A., who died
on the Ormuz on May 12, shortly after the vessel
left Colombo, and was committed to the deep, is
the third S.A. legislator who has died at sea in
the same neighborhood. The first was George
Kingston, and the second J. C. Bray.
It was left to a presumably educated Syd-
ney medical man to plumb the lowest depth of
slavish adulation on Hempire Day. The medico
in question, who probably depends wholly on
Australians for his livelihood, had a flagpole on
his suburban lawn. At half-mast floated the Aus-
tralian flag, higher up the Union Jack, and above
all the Crimson Sun of Japan ! Banzai!
Book-Arcadian Cole, of Melbourne, has
occupied the last few months, in conjunction with
his tame editor, in compiling a Book of Liars, to
be published in about a fortnight. The volume
will begin with Ananias and continue to De
Rougemont. A special chapter might be devoted
to the Six Hatters Lie and the Petnana Myth—
not to mention Tozer’s Maid. But The Bulletin
bets it won’t.
“ Kotuku ” : The death at Wellington
(M.L.), at the age of 85, of Amos Burr recalls an
interesting incident of Pacific history. Burr
arrived in 1840 in the New Zealand Co.’s surveying
ship Cuba. A fortnight later the Cuba was sent off
to the Chatham Islands with the co.’s attorney,
R. D. Hanson (afterwards Chief Justice of South
Australia), to endeavor to complete a pur-
chase of the group from the Maori owners,
the Ngatimutunga tribe, who had
almost finished eating the original
proprietors. Hanson completed
the job to his own and the
co.’s satisfaction, and
the directors in Lon-
don had almost
agreed with a
Hamburg firm
to colonise
the Chathams
from Ger-
many, when
the Colonial
Office waved
its arms with
aloud whoop,
and warned
the co. off
the grass, in-
asmuch as the
Chathams
were outside
the latitude
and longitude
of its charter.
The storm
raised then did
not subside un-
til the co.’s
activity ceased
with the surren-
der of its charter.
Amos Burr had
both arms blown
off when the Cuba
was firing a salute in
Port Nicholson, prior
to leaving on the expedi-
tion to the Chathams, but
that did not prevent him run-
ning the first ferry and house of
amusement on the Manawatu River,
and living a jovial life for 06 more years.
An interesting heavy-weight championship
event took place in Ballarat on Saturday, Premier
Seddon, of Maoriland, and Premier Bent, of Vic-
toria, being the contestants. Seddon got on a
penny-in-the-slot weighing machine, and turned
the indicator at 19st. 51b. Bent followed suit, but
the indicator only showed 18st. 131 b., so Bent re-
tired defeated.
Hugh Dixson, who gave £5OOO to Sydney
Y.M.C.A. the other day, is in the habit of throw-
ing about cheques of £lO,OOO, £5OOO, and similar
amounts when a cause hits him in the right place.
He is president of a tobacco-manufacturing busi-
ness, which he and his father worked up from
very small beginnings, is an Australian by birth,
and was schooled in Sydney.
The unkissed Gladstone Dowie has been
appointed by Yoliva “Colonel of the Zion
Guards,” and, as such, this dutiful son (who seems
to suspect Pa of having kept an injurious and
selfish monopoly of the kisses) was lately threaten-
ing that “if the First Apostle set foot in Zion City,
he would lock him in the secret vault of the Zion
chamber, where he could howl at his heart’s con-
tent.” It is his own parent that the unkissed
specimen is alluding to.
Senator Dawson, once the stalwart Andy
Dawson whose seven days Premiership of Queens-
land was an oasis in the desert of the Continuous
Ministry, cuts a poor figure standing on a public
platform as the apologist and sycophant of
Melbourne tote-man Wren. Wren may—or may
not—possess many of the untheological virtues,
but his business is an essentially illegal one ; and
is, moreover, one of the two great curses afflicting
the workers of the community.
Hua, the Maori upstart, who recently
blossomed out as a prophet, is fast losing his
mana, and has now but a handful of followers
who are helping him to eat various tribes out of
house and home. The police have discovered that
the dusky gentleman has “ done time ” for theft,
but that isn’t the worst trouble. Rua has
guaranteed that Jesus Christ and Edward VII
will be at Gisborne this month, and the fact has
leaked out that Edward YII hasn’t even started
yet.
Died in Sydney, a few days back, during a
visit, F. H. Browne, a Victorian Congregational
parson, who worked in the S.M. Herald office
over 50 years ago. Browne and Sam Cook, now
general manager of the Herald, had been in the
same office in England, Browne as boy, Cook as
apprentice. Browne came out first, and Cook,
when he arrived, found a familiar devil on the
premises. It was largely owing to John Fairfax
that his devil went on to Camden College, and
threw up the inky business. He was a “white
man ” all through.
Professor David, who tries hard to breathe
life into the very serious-minded Sydney Univer-
sity, has been granted five months off to visit
Mexico, where there is a geographical congress.
He promises to inspect the Indian coal fields
cn route, and also to endeavor to soothe Mount
Vesuvius. Further, it is his intention to visit
Frisco and demand a reason for recent happen-
ings, and the earthquake will have to offer a
satisfactory explanation or come outside.
Some men manage to compress a lot of
incident into quite a small amount of time. To be
on a steamer when it was run down and sunk, and
to escape just in time to dodge the fate of the
foundering ship is a good enough “crowded
hour” for most men. But to come ashore and
within another 12 hours to get drunk, and fight
three policemen, and get fined 10s. for the drunk,
and to get three months for the fight, is rushing
things. Yet a Sydney fireman recently did all
this. Run down, taken off, taken up and run in,
so to speak.
The recent passing of Vic. mining man
Bailey recalls, the fact that David Syme had a
notion of making his pile on the diggings when he
first struck Victoria, and, in ’56, he was one of a
shindykit of six that pegged out a quartz claim
almost at the very spot where Bailey and co.
afterwards made their wealth. But the Syme
partyhad no luck. After it had spent £l3OO in
prospecting, buying machinery, and so forth, its
claim was jumped, and the Warden, after much
heated argument, adjudged the jumpers to be in
lawful possession. That Warden got a few for
himself in the Age from the hand of David’s elder
brother, Ebenezer, but possibly the house of
Syme now thinks it has cause to revere his
memory. Many mining investors who have met
the wild cat in all its aspects wish that they, like
David, had been driven out of the cat-infested
industry when they were young, and driven into
a profitable newspaper.
’TWAS IN THE LONE PACIFIC.
“ He died of worry about a dinner.”
“ What! Was he the chef!”
"No—the menu!”
If Irvine tries for a Federal seat in Vic-
toria he’ll probably find that he isn’t Iceberg size,
but only a hailstone.
Old George Lansell’s Victorian property
has been valued for probate purposes at £695 00U.
The old man spent a lot of money during the past
few years in sending shafts down to record depths
after gold. Fifteen years ago his estate would
have been proved at over a million. He was
always a clever old man, and he managed to die
just at the stage when his property will pay least
in death duties. The chances are that in a year
or two, a whole barrow-load of his scrip, now
worth mere shillings, will be saleable for pounds—
that is unless old George has taken all his luck
with him.
Charlotte Gibson Tyrer, the “ Melbourne
heiress,” who ran up bills at many shops in the
city, got old man Belson involved in numerous
legal difficulties, and got three months herself
from the Brighton magistrates, was last week
“run-in” on a charge of plain, unromantic lar-
ceny. She had been working as a barmaid, and
numerous missing articles are alleged to have been
traced to her possession. Charlotte is a pretty
little girl with a saucy, turned-up nose. She is
6ft. 3in. high, plump and glib, and has large,
honest eyes that anyone would trust with cash
down.
“ Pressman ” : I was once present in the
Criminal Court at Darlinghurst when the late
Charles Bull got a man off with two minutes’ im-
prisonment without “hard.” The culprit was
put in the dock for doing “ grievous bodily harm.”
“Your Honor,” said Bull, “the prisoner pleads
guilty. The injured man made base proposals to
prisoner’s daughter; he went in search of him,
and floored him right off.” “I don’t see how he
could have done less,” said the late Judge Coffey.
“ Two minutes’ imprisonment.” The proper
official started to take the prisoner to gaol to
work out his term, but the sentence expired on
the stairs, and he was disgustedly turned loose.
The late Edmund Spicer, of Adelaide, left
behind him plenty of visible reasons why he died
rich. These were his business accounts, made
out on scraps cut off the ends of the accounts
which other people sent in to him. The writer
has one of them before him —a flabby bit, about
the length of a cigar, on which Spicer’s ill-paid
clerk had written out an intimation that £534 was
due to his saving boss for fencing wire.
Malcolm M'Eacharn, just back in Mel-
bourne from Japan, is a big shareholder in one of
the Tokio tramway companies. The Japs gave
three foreign shindykits tramway concessions in
that city, and smiled placidly when the three
competitors, as is the custom with Private Enter-
prise, started cutting each other’s throats by
lowering their fares. Presently the three com-
panies came to an understanding, and set out
to recoup themselves by raising the fares. But
Yoshiwara wasn’t taking any rise. The Govern-
ment merely passed an order-in-council that,
the companies having reduced their fares of their
own freewill, they would remain reduced till
further orders. Now, supposing anyone in Aus-
tralia treated foreign capital one-tenth as badly as
these brown swindlers —our chivalrous allies—have
done, think of the language of Stinking Fish and
Co. ! !
Concerning Dowie :
Hair-raising yarns were told, with more or less
truth, of Profit Dowie when the Zionites of Sydney
and Melbourne, with much objurgatory formula,
renounced the devil and all his works. It was
alleged that out of 3,578,000 dollars sent to Dowie for
investment in Zion stock only 1,000,000 dollars were
really invested, the rest being Dowie-ised ; and that only
250,006 dollars out of 2,884,000 dollars subscribed for the
Zion City laceworks went to help that factory. Dowie
was said to have arranged to take to Mexico as his wives
“ the seven most beautiful girls in Zion.” Most of Zion’s
maidens are lean, but probably Dowie picked the plump
ones. Another weird story concerned a vault beneath
one of Dowie’s houses. A double door of chilled steel
stood at the end of a secret passage, of which Elijah
alone held the keys. Within, the vault was luxuriously
carpeted (ah !)—its walls were silk lined (o-o-oh !) —and
there was in it a magnificent double bed (00-ooh !)—with
the downiest of pillows and cushions. This dove-cote
cost 25,000 dollars. The dwindling Zionites (all save one
who, being named Smith, does not count) renounced
Dowie with a fierce Whereas and a vituperative There-
fore. The Profit, who recently alleged that his legs were
decaying in Mexico but that they had been divinely re-
paired, is selling at a very low figure these days.
There is a sad degeneracy in abusiveness
among the leaders of Zion. Even Voliva’s foetid
fury never quite reached the rank, malodorous
picturesqueness of Dowie’s flights. Now the Aus-
tralasian mantle has fallen upon Pastor M’Cullagh,
a once-upon-a-time roarer among tl»3 Seventh-
Day - Adventists. M'Ctillagh’s attempts at
vituperation are poor pale things. “Damnable,”
“abominable,” and “adulterous” -are the worst
words he knows. The Zion battle in Australia
has become about as colorless as a controversy be-
tween two Archbishops. Even in the stormy
Melbournejmeetings, the most truculent epithets
were those flung at M’Cullagh by a mere un-
trained outsider—a light-weight amateur who
ought to have been struck on the jaw by the first
curse and knocked right out.
The death of Michael Davitt, the Irish
patriot, removes one of the finest characters of his
generation. He was the son of a little Irish
farmer who was evicted by a grasping and loafing
land-owner because he couldn’t pay an impossible
rent. When he was less than six years old his
mother begged to support herself and him in the
streets of London. When he was eight he was
toiling hopeless hours for an almost invisible
wage in a Lancashire cotton mill, and there
he lost his right arm in a machinery acci-
dent while still a child. Yet, with all his
disadvantages, he grew up to be one of the con-
spicuous figures of British politics. England sen-
tenced him in 1870 to the usual reward of the Irish
patriot—ls years’ penal servitude—the same Eng-
land which always had a casual tear to spare for
Garibaldi and Kossuth and Kosciusko, and other
foreign insurrectionaries with strange names and
strange hats and loud red shirts. Yet Ireland has
a better case than Poland, and there was more to be
said for Davitt and his friends than for Kosciusko.
England has made Ireland a place that is unfit to
live in, if there is any evidence in the fact that
half its population has vanished. Russia has
made Poland a place where existence is possible,
if there is any evidence in its fast-growing popu-
lation, in its ever-rising industries, and in the
fact that it has become under Russian rule
the most densely-peopled and richest part
of the Czar’s empire. Ireland has had
some semblance of political liberty and no
victuals—nothing but rack-renting English land-
lords and industries ruined by Freetrade; the
Russian policy was to give Poland no semblance
of political liberty, but to build up its industries,
and to see, as far as the limited Russian intelli-
gence knew how, that Poland had a water-tight
raff over its head, possessed its own bit of farm
la ad, and enjoyed its three square meals a day.
B at England could never understand why Ireland
o’ ijected to being rack-rented, evicted, and starved
v hile it enjoyed the priceless blessing of sending
105 members to the House of Commons ; and when
1 dichael Davitt failed to see things from Eng-
land's point of view, it sentenced the one-armed
patriot to 15 years.
OUR FRIEND GEORGE AGAIN.
Several complaints have been received from the suburbs
of the practice indulged in by some wanton or careless
individual in dropping poisoned morsels on the public! from the suburbs
thoroughfares. —Sydney TELEG[?]APII
There has been a remarkable fatality
hanging round one Australian life-insurance office,
the Colonial Mutual, during the last few months.
First, one of its London directors (ex-Premier of
Queensland Herbert) died ; then, one after the
other, its general manager (Leslie Park), its
actuary, its London manager, and two or three
officers. Next the strain told on the assistant
general-manager, and he had to retire; and finally
(the whole office has daily prayers that it is
finally), one of the head-office directors, J. F.
Levien, died the other day.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 5, 1906.
My Dear Arini.—
The attempted assassination of the Spanish
King and his bride had awful consequences to
very many, but the luck of the Bourbons—and in
the matter of avoiding assassination they have a
freat deal of luck—stood by Alphonso and Queen
!na. Probably no royal race has been more shot
at or made the target of more miscellaneous explo-
sives, yet they have come off reasonably well.
The first of the family who climbed on any throne
worth mentioning, the Henry IV. that Wallace
Brownlow used to do so well, was murdered —I
don’t remember very distinctly how, but it was
done at close quarters. Louis XVI. was shot
with an axe, so far as I can recall. But every-
body who aimed at a Bourbon sovereign, at
20 yards range, has missed him, though there
were three royal families of the race to shoot at,
and 317 years in which to take steady aim. The
last Alphonso, father of the piesent King, was
fired at twice, and if the germ of consumption had
been as bad a shot as his other enemies, he might
have been living to this day. The present
Alphonso begins early to dodge the homicidal
maniac ; he is only a few days over 20.
On Saturday morning Spain’s Consul in this
State (Major Freehill) held a reception at the
Australia in honor of the Wedding, but the bomb
cables had their effect, and at the explosion of the
first champagne cork the guests started nervously.
As all the horde of Consuls were talking of the
King’s escape, nerves were “ in,” and had anyone
entered carrying a bouquet, a waiter would have
run him through with a corkscrew on the spot.
One doesn’t know (or forgets) how numerous are
the Nations and Nationettes until one sees a well-
attended Consular reception. By their clothes ye
shall know them—the Consuls. Besides the roll-
up of Consuls, there was a great muster of R.C.
clergy, beginning with the crimson-clad Cardinal.
Mrs. Freehill was present, and received a fairly
large number of ladies, who, if it hadn’t
been for the bomb, would have talked royal
trousseaux and fair Ena. Consul Freehill is one
of the most uniformed men in the State with his
consular, church, and army dignities. He didn’t
have them all on this time, however—was, in fact,
in plain morning dress.
At Monday’s races at Randwick Mrs. E. P.
Simpson took the vote for art-like dressing. Her
achievement was a black velvet, trained, with
horizontal bands of wide silk braid on the skirt,
bolero of velvet and white furs. Her black
straw hat with black feathers was said by an
expert to be the top-note of style, but I thought
it had been in the awful tram collision on Satur-
day. Mrs. Ewan Frazer, in petunia chiffon and
velvet, with a tea-tray hat all humility on the top
surface (no trimming), and all pride and a wealth
of shaded feathers under one side of the brim, had
a right to a front stall, in Vanity Fair. One
winsome stranger was all in soft crimson,
from toque to toe, with white fox furs.
The Northcote Ex. came out for a while.
Mr. Willie Kelly, in ti-tree green clothes,
formed one of a tableau of three, of which Mr.
G. H. Reid and Colonel Stanley were the other
two. “An interesting composition,” remarked a
painter person of them, “but some of the figures
are out of drawing.” Antique ornaments are
“in” again and Mrs. Severn wore one —an odd
amber necklace—with a white gown. A small
child, dressed in a frock with six inches of white
silk skirt and much costly lace, had neither shoes
nor stockings on its mottled violet limbs. The
mother who would dress her offspring in such
guise on such a nipping day deserves a monu-
ment of jackass skulls raised in her honor, with a
kookaburra eating a mule on the summit thereof.
Mrs. Shipman, the brown-eyed, fair-haired
little American who is here with her husband,
Marie Narelle’s pilot star, was playing in “The
Man from Mexico ” (she has played every woman
part in it) when her husband first saw her. Talk-
ing of Miss Narelle, the visitor says she looked
real cute going shopping in New York, wearing a
jade-green velvet walking suit and toque of black
and green. The singer has ebony-black hair,
and when singing at the Waldorf she
wore in it several white combs set with tur-
quoises. Like Ada Crossley, Marie Narelle has
developed and perfected a taste for artistic
dressing, coiffing and posing. The Shipmans’
meeting with Marie, and consequent taking-on of
the Narelle tour, was accidental. Mr. Lemmone
couldn’t see his way clear to manage the cam-
pa!gn to its sweet end, and the singer had just got
a letter from the flautist to that effect.
But it is on the Earthquake that Mrs. Shipman
chirrups best at present. “We had got into the
St. Irancis Hotel only the night before, and at
8 o clock I was sitting in the grand corridor watch-
ing the millionairesses sweeping out, all frou-
frou and jewel gleams, to the opera. It was
grand opera night, you know, and every woman
that trailed out to an automobile looked as if the
St. Francis was just It as a background for Her
Distinction. The ’Quake occurred at 13 past 5
in the morning. Another nail in No. 13’s coffin !
All the official clocks in the city stopped at that
figure. All elevators in the hotels fell with the
first shock, so one had to run down those miles of
staircases—they seemed like miles. I saw one of
the bejewelled women of the night before rush
out of the hotel flushed with triumph because
shed saved three pairs of shoes. But she’d left
a j precious stones on her dressing-table.
And the bell-boys were bumping steel-clamped
trunks down the marble staircases and knocking
out chunks of marble at every bump. Two of our
big trunks, with a lot of Miss Narelle’s property
were m the basement, and my husband offered a
lift boy twenty dollars if he would help in getting
them out. When pay-time came he found he had
nothing smaller than a hundred dollar bill, but
the boy loftily said ‘I can oblige you,’ and gave
him change. Those boys reaped a full-dollared
harvest. We didn’t lose anything but a ham
sandwich.
“The lossesoftheCollier company, though!— one
of the ladies lost 16 hats. Mr. Collier’s 11 trunks
are gone. He is a great dresser, and al-
ways wears his ties, socks and fobs to match. His
hats and ties are on the same color terms, and he
must have felt a Human Discord in the out-of-
tune clothes in which he made the voyage with
us. Talking of men’s clothes—do you know the
street boys here guy Fred for wearing the latest
in hats—little round ones with a low crown ?
Collier wears them also. No, the guying doesn’t
make the men a bit hot about the collar. They
just keep on wearing them. Haven’t you got the
‘pony coats’ here yet ? They’re being w r orn in New
York, and are awfully smart, made of silk or velvet,
perfectly fitting, short on the hips and often
elaborately braided. . . . Haven’t you heard of
Roselle Nott? O, she’s just a peach. My
husband’s brother is manager of the ‘ When
Knighthood was in Flower’ Company in which
she’s leading woman, and he’s thinking of bring-
ing it out here. Ernest Hastings is leading man.”
From a bookstall I picked the April copy of
“ The Smart Set.” It had a prose sketch by Mrs.
Poultney Bigelow. You remember Prof. P. 8.,
the historian, who was in Sydney with his little
canoe nearly a year ago, looking for infor-
mation about Lola Montez for one of his numerous
histories. The writer of the sketch is fortunate
enough to be his wife. He wore very smart ties
and, in spite of the height of the Chair he occupies
at the Boston University, he was a sympathetic
and engaging superman. Well, this sketch is
entitled “Merely Players,” and tells of two
ladies who, to test a theory, wrote to two
London star actors, making appointments for
a meeting. “ I shall wear a large bunch
of pink roses and an ermine toque, and
be walking at the end of Sloan-street, ” wrote
one of the ladies to an actor who is called in the
sketch Fortescue Orme. Then she drove slowly
up and down in her brougham, and as soon as she
saw Orme winding up to keep the assignation, she
dashed off to tell the other coy deceiver. This
one had written to “Peter Bowles,” the
other star, asking him very prettily to tea. In-
stead of going he wrote saying he had a charming
wife, and his home was at such an address, and
asking the writer to call. Now, the curious thing
is that the sketch is an incident from real life.
“ Peter Bowles” is Mr. Hugh Ward, and Mrs.
Bigelow prints his letter slightly disguised. The
lady of the story did call, and she and Mrs. Ward
became great friends.
The sensible soft shirt is catching on. All
the Americans who come here wear it, and
several “ Silvertails, ” as the man who leans
against lamp-posts calls the club man, have
adopted it. Gilded Willie Lawson, who is said to
be now circling the earth in a motor-car, was very
previous with the fashion. A friend of his says he
was fashionably limp about the collar and cuffs
some years ago.
The only daughter of the Mackays, of Wallen-
been station, was married last week to Dr. A.
Campbell, at the Australia. They have taken
the Lambs’ house, at Randwick. She is a sister
of Colonel Kenneth Mackay, and is a pretty,
rather dark little bride. An authority tells me
that her ivory-satin wedding gown was remark-
able for its quality. It could have stood up on its
own merits without any girl inside it, and the
train, when not in action, was like frozen pearl-
billows. There is such a sameness about the
love-honor-and-obey gown that one is glad to
hear of a robe that shows such self-reliance as to
stand alone. One firm made nine glad gowns for
this bride (who is moneyed), and—significant item
—not a corselet robe amongst them. Nearly every
woman who has a corselet skirt and bolero is will-
ing at her afternoon tea to drink perdition to the
style.
Mr. Hugh Dixson, of tobacco and orchid-liouse
fame, is said to be the giver of the £5OOO towards
the building fund of the Y.M.C. A. When Igo
on the land I think I’ll grow tobacco and cigar-
ettes, and wood for pipes. I did intend to raise
onions and potatoes because a man whose land
adjoins mine has rabbits infesting his place, and
my vegetables and his bunnies would dovetail
nicely in the matter of stews. But the Dixson
donation has given me other views.
News of Nora Long’s three years’ engagement
by the Moody Manners Opera Co. will make her
birthplace in Maoriland throw up its hat and
never care whether the head-garment comes down
again or not. Nora came over from her island
home some four years ago and began the strenuous
life of study and struggle in Sydney. Some 18
months back she went to London at the same time
as Eva Mylott. The contralto is about to be mar-
ried, but the soprano is in for grand opera. Miss
Long is a thin, dark-haired girl, with (what is rare
in a thin girl) some dimples and a large store of
energy and optimism. She isn’t more than 20 or
so, yet her portraits make her look older than her
own mother. That mother, by the way, was as
good a singer as her daughter is, only her voice
was never published.
Accounts of Miss Edyth Latimer’s marriage in
London to Mr. W. Haviland, an actor-manager,
tell of a brilliant wedding-breakfast overflowing
with precious gifts, and a list of names thickly
punctuated with Australian appellations. Minia-
ture silver paper horseshoes were thrown for luck
instead of the usual Chinaman’s food. Bride is a
girl from Manly, and beautiful is a word that fits
her better than pretty.
Mr. Atlas Samson Goliath Seddon, Premier of
Maoriland, backs his belief in full cradles with
his sovereign purse. A trefoil of little baldheads
arrived to an amazed parent in M.L. recently,
and the dynamic Richard sent three guineas to
the enterprising mother. If the shamrock hasn’t
shed a petal at the end of a year he will repeat
the golden offering.
A teacher in a Sydney school, run by some very
religious women, lately gave her class of well-
grown girls an essay on the subject, “Freetrade
versus Protection.” She first gave them a few
points to go upbn, stating, among other things,
that no Christian could be other than a Freetrader,
and that farmers were leaving this country in
great numbers on account of Protection. Next
day only two girls brought in essays. One frankly
owned up that she had got her father to write
hers, and a glance at it showed it was very like a
fiscal article in The Bulletin. She was ruled out,
on the ground that her work wasn’t original. The
other girl rose manfully to read her effusion. She
got through the first sentence : “ Protection is be-
lieved in by thoughtful and educated people, only
the lower class and ignorant ones being Free-
traders.” Then the teacher waved a shocked arm
in the air, and said that the geography lesson
would now start.
For the last month I’ve not been able to enter
Paling’s without some pianist or soprano or con-
tralto or just plain crank skipping up to me to
say that Miss Jean Ramsay, who was a popular
singer in Melbourne just ten years ago, and who
accompanied Ada Crossley from the Bluff to
Auckland, married Mr. A. E. Donne, sec. of the
Wellington Woollen Manufacturing Co., not, as I
wrote, Mr. T. E. Donne, chief of the Tourist
Department. These gentlemen are brothers.
Then another Duck, with a portfolio of drawings
under her arm and her hair done with a bellows,
would rise to remark that the other Mrs. Donne
would have won fame as a seascape painter, only,
like the musical Mrs. Donne, she extinguished
herself under that great early Entinguisher—
matrimony. I hope these few carefully-selected
remarks will sort out the respective ladies.
The Fresh Air League Ball occurs on July
3rd. Excellency Rawson and Miss Rawson will
attend.
Lady Le Hunte and her two sisters, the Misses
Clowes, who have been helping Lady Northcote
to live in Government House here, left last
night for Adelaide. The Clowes girls think
Australia “ charming,” and the Birthday
Races “ great fun.” So they were. The
Lady of Many Tails alone would have
made them deserve the description. She is an
Australian girl, quite good-looking, and married
to a naval officer, but she wore an inartistic fur
toque that might have been ’possum or snark or
gazeka, with several furry tails dangling about
her ears. Then there was a nice girl who played
asoubrette part with Johnny Sheridan, and played
it very well. She had a long coat with two great
pockets on the back, and coat and skirt were in
such audible, big check pattern that other women
said she was trying to signal Mars with the cos-
tume.
In spots Her Majesty’s on Saturday night
looked like an Easter parade—not the one they
have at the Show Ground, but the other one they
have at the place where they spend days and
nights calling out “Eyes Front!” and “ Shol’
Ump!” In J.C.W.’s box were the double G.
of the Commonwealth and the single G. of S.
Australia, the short and long of it so far
as Excellencies are concerned; and there was
consequently much military on the premises. In
the circle another dazzling figure was the wife of
Haigh Jackson ; the baritone, out of the cast, sat
beneath the lady’s shadow, and for once looked on
from the front instead of from the stage. Rose
Musgrove was also out of “ The Little Michus ” ;
but she is tripping to Europe.
Early in the proceedings the last lingering doubt
•about the all-wool way to speak the name
of the new Messager piece was routed,
and we knew that it wasn’t all the same as if it
\yere spelled “ The Little My-Cuss.” Something
like a peripatetic sneeze, was how George Lauri
described the real thing. There isn’t as
much philosophy as there is music in the
piece. The most warmly approving nod that
the late Solomon would have given wouW most
likely have followed the remark of Aristide Percy,
grocer’s assistant, that, when a man has to have
three meals a day with a woman, it’s most impor-
tant to know how she eats before he sets out for
where the Voice will breathe.
The recent stranding of the French barque
Vincennes at Manly to the accompaniment of
“Home, Sweet Home ” and the “Marseillaise,”
played by the local band, recalls some weird
Manly music of former days. When the Co-op.
and Port Jackson Ferry Companies were cutting
the return fare from Sydney down to 3d., the
band came down to the pier to welcome the
Co-op.’s one-time greyhound of the harbor, the
Conqueror, when it arrived on its first trip. And
of all things in the world the melody that band
played was “The Ship that Never Returned.”
Its capacity for the inopportune seems something
above the average.
“ The motor car has begun what will be for me
a liberal education in Australian natural
beauty.” This sounds sententious. It’s
Ramaciotti, of the Williamson-Tallis Combine.
He and Mrs. Ramaciotti took Mrs. Tallis for a
sprint in their little De Dion to Medlow, 3400
feet above fish level. Got there in three hours
and 24 minutes over perfect roads, and ran back
in two hours and 50 minutes.
The two birds of passage, Sir Evan James and
Mr. Bethell, who were appearing enfamille with
the Northcotes, have gone to blow bubbles in the
South Sea Islands. Former has been P.M.G. of
Bombay, and held ever so many other Indian ap-
pointments. Latter is a young man, a son of Lord
Westbury. (This peer’s complete name is Richard
Luttrel Pilkington Bethell.) They did M.L. with
rod and gun and hoof.
Handsome Lillah M‘Carthy, who, when last
here, was Wilson Barrett’s leading woman, has
married the (possibly) most intellectual actor in
London. He started the Court Theatre and put
on high-class English and foreign dramas that
otherwise would never have seen Fogtown’s day-
light or gaslight. Barker is a scholarly-looking
man and Lillah —well, you remember her in the
“Sign of the Cross” looking the beautiful Early
Christian martyr to the life. But she spoke her
lines in fearsome basement tones.
A Sydney woman journalist is writing a book
on etiquette to the order of a local publishing
firm, and this city pauses with its knife half way
to its mouth and waits for the publication thereof.
Miss Grace Joel, the artist, has left Sydney.
She intends to stretch her canvas permanently in
Melbourne, and mix her colors for better or worse
on Yarra’s moderately smiling shore.
A. doctor of repute has written on spinal curva-
tures, and speaks quite reverently of the corset,
Says its extraneous support is beneficial. Why?
Because man is built to go on all fours:—
All his internal organs are arranged for progress on
four le » rs instead of on two. By morphological rights,
, too ’ he ?h° u . ld be a hairy quadruped. But as he
hanHno-p foutld ’ by experience, that
BOme ® orfc bandage support is required in order to assist
a n abdominal mechanism that is inadequate for biDed
progression.
If this be true, most men are dressing most un-
wisely. Let us start a Compulsory Male Corset
Society. Then will the drapers hang specimens
in their windows : “ As worn by Mr. Bruce Smith,
Ver y chic.” “ The Peach. Mr. B. R. Wise wears
no other.” “ Distingue . As laced by Mr. J. 0.
Williamson.” Johnnies like the late Marquis of
Anglesey are sane then, and scientific when they
practise closer settlement on their innards. But
if man is naturally a quadruped, as alleged, going
on all fours with his eye-glass gazing at the mud,
what animal is the natural biped ? Or is there no
natural biped ?
A Sydney man’s wife came home from a world
tour and ordered him to dismiss the “niece” he
had acquired during her absence. He declined,
and has gone off with the adoption to Japan and
other countries where the Ten Commandments
are dead.
Yours Affectionately,
AKRNEHI.
“ A.C.D.” writes :
The Tasmanian Government, under the leadership of
ex-mariner Jack Evans, has defeat staring it in the face.
Weakened by the Labor and Liberal victories at the last
general elections, the Tory Premier sought to strengthen
his party by stealing from the Opposition Messrs. Prop-
sting and Urquhart. This was accomplished by appoint-
ing the former Attorney-General and the latter Treasurer
in the Jack Evans Ministry. For a glaring example of
political immorality the change of front on the part of
Propsting would be hard to beat. As Premier, and later
as leader of the Opposition, he denounced as utterly
rotten everything suggested by Evans and his clique.
Now he plays second fiddle to his late political enemy.
But having bought the support of Propsting and Urquhart
(who secured their election by posing as Oppositionists),
the gain to the Government is neutralised by the loss of
Stewart and old melancholy Bird. If the Labor party
throws in its lot with the Opposition, there will be
nothing left for Evans but to take the most dignified
departure he is capable of.
Premier Bent is now buying 1400 acres
of land near Maribyrnong (Yic.) racecourse,
allegedly for workmen’s homes—and only five
miles from a station! These land purchases
are beginning to have an odor. Of the 97
Footscray allotments 20 are still unsold ; of the 44
at Moreland 12 are not yet taken up; the Dal
Campbell estate alongside is hardly touched as
yet; and the brickyard at Thornbury (30£ acres)
lies absolutely idle. Working men don’t want
these half-acres away at the end of everything and
about two hours journey from their work. Mean-
while Bent, as a consistent buyer at anything but
bargain prices, raises the price of other and more
suitable nearer lands. A tax that would fall on
ununecl land would do far more towards enabling
workmen to acquire homes than all these trumpery
purchases.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade lumin-
ously expounds the bearing of the Liquor Law in
cases where a doctor considers brandy absolutely
necessary for a patient who has been foolish enough
to fall ill on Sunday and who hadn’t the foresight
to have drink in the house. The exposition comes
to this : The doctor, or someone else, may, or may
not, find a publican who will sell the liquor on the
doctor’s order. If he does, the police, supposing
they hear of the case, will summon the publican
and the purchaser; and the magistrate will fine
them. Then the fined parties may appeal to the
Attorney-General, who will go over the facts if he
has time, and if he discovers that the brandy was
ordered in an actual life-and-death case, he will
remit the fine. The costs and the worry will re-
main. If the Attorney-General, in the depths of
his medical ignorance, reckons the brandy wasn’t
required, he won’t remit the fine.
HOW TO BE BEAUTIFUL.
Imitation may be the smcerest form of flattery,
but at times it must be very annoying, as in the case of
“ Valaze,” the wonderful Russian skin-food. Three years
ago it was introduced to the public of Australia by Mdlle.
Helena Rubinstein, and so speedily were its good qualities
recognised that quite a big demand sprang up almost
immediately. Instantly dozens of imitations of “ Valaze ”
appeared, got up to closely resemble the Russian skin-
food. Often the food bore a name so nearly resembling
“Valaze” that it deceived buyers. All this was very
flattering, but at the same time decidely aggravating, for
often not only worthless, but positively harmful so called
skin-foods were trading on the reputation of “Valaze.”

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 5, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie, —
There’s a mighty army of clerks, typists,
secretaries, advisers, and handy men following in
the wake of the Maoriland Chief. To see them
and hear them at the Grand, tapping at the
machines, whirling along with cablegrams, rushing
through the corridors at the call of the ’phone,
dictating, conferring, and all the rest of it, is to
secure a view of the elaborate Yankee-like hustle
that dazzles the throng in occasional stage scenes.
This is the
way of startling the community. Mr. Donne has
his Tourists’ Bureau and Exhibition forces in full
swing. Mr. Owen Cox lias his shipping clerks
going at full blast. And the other Grand residents
wander about in a dazed state as if portion of an
exploded ’Frisco had been suddenly thrown at
Melbourne.
Prime Minister Deakin, Bigjoh’n Forrest, and a
brace or two more Federalists went a-frivolling
on Friday night to “Dr. Wake’s Patient,” having
as special guests Mrs. and Miss Seddon. Mr.
Seddon himself was banqueting with the Bent
Brethren and the State Talbottle at the Exhibi-
tion quarters. Mrs. Seddon rose to the occasion
with a mauve garb that knocked spots out of all
the Princess flutterers.
The swarm of politicians and vice-regals with
their staffs has staggered the Seddon corps more
than any other Australian possession. “The ex-
pense ! Think of the expense ! ” says even Miss
May Seddon, who is splendid and energetic
enough to govern several States herself. In vain
the feminine portion of Emperor Dick’s retinue
endeavor to remember who among the Babel
brethren are State and who are Federal. They
can’t do it. They give it up, and shrug their
shoulders helplessly.
I saw the other day the Bent Premier, the
Falstaffian Forrest, and the heavyweight Em-
peror from the hot-spring province, and I prayed
for Georgie Porgie Reid to lne along. He didn’t.
And then I remembered that after all he is a man
of no real weight in the community.
Some of the most interesting chiffons exploited
this Seddon season of gush are owned by Miss
Bonsor, from Sydney. I saw her one night in a
wonderful hand-painted zouave arrangement
imitative of an old Louis brocade. A clingsome
voile skirt in milky tones went with the zouave.
The same Miss has an admirable way of rigging
up her pale honey thatch. She secures a smart
effect with a soft Regent set of puffs.
The hips that wobble have entirely gone out—
blown out —collapsed. A French corset-designer
fell upon an old patient—or patron, rather —the
other day, and uttered a broken-hearted protest.
“It ees the feegure of 1902. Alas! that you
could be so weecked 1 You can be round—yes, a
leetle; but small! Oh, please—please do not
wobble ! It ees too terreeble ! ” Of course, the
fashionable patient had her wobble instantly
removed. But it’s a trying thing to move the
waist-line about in this capricious manner, and to
dismiss one’s hips at a day’s brief notice. The
next proclamation of Tyrant Fashion may be to
mangle out the entire figure and make it an abbre-
viated telegraph pole. That’s all very well. But
next week there may be a recall of the curves.
And how mangle them in again ? These are try-
ing times.
An Excellency in a State much slower than
this is taking painting lessons from a she artist,
and so far none of the local tongues have been set
a-wagging. Now, imagine the Talbottle sub-
mitting to, say, carving lessons from a giddy
young carvess !
Gossip on this side is busy with the story of a
Harborville beauty of much account having given
the direct public cut in Sydney’s flashest hotel
to a professional visitor of profound coun-
tenance. The story came from an informative
male; and several sentimental friends of the
hero are enjoying anticipatory thrills of conquest.
Dr. J. Harbinson publishes a second edition of
his rather fragrant verse this violet season. The
Brighton medico fills in time between social
whirling and pill-practice by spinning on poetic
feet. The volume in preparation contains some
verse that has already had a baptism of ink, supple-
mented by many lyrics and sonnets.
How the Irish-throated score, oratorically and
vocally ! There are innumerable Erin-Australian
tjo tiers on the war-path, Miss Eileen O’Brien
being one of the fittest. Now Miss Ivy O’Shan-
nassv is listed as a platform recruit. This young
thing is a fine contralto, with deep, organish
notes.
Hal Cohen was to get a farewell show at the
Theatre Royal of his native town (Ballarat) on
Tuesday the sth. All his musical worshippers,
including the Pinschof girleens, Dr. Gray’s
daughter, and Miss Elmhurst Goode, were going
up to repeat the performance given recently at
the Hawthorn Town Hall.
The Stuart Balmains are determined to give all
Melbourne a sight of the trousseau. So they
hang out at the parent nest, and are about town
most of their time, taking tiffin in popular cafes.
Every girleen lets her crumpet and chop grow
cold while she seizes the points of Alison’s
tailoresque suit, or her toot-tooting rig, or her big-
feathered hat, or her acute-angle toque, or her
rings, or her shoes, or her appetite. A brand-new
bride is such a curio.
Toorak quite suddenly took to entertaining the
Fitzpatrick couple. Mrs. George Cliirnside, who
only comes out of her shell for a big splash, set
the ball rolling with a Sunday tea, whereat the
Stonington Aides and the Very Best People
tiffened in their best bib and tucker. Mrs. Robert
Harper and Mrs. Charlie Ryan followed suit.
Finally, the newly-hitched pair set off amid fare-
well blessings on Friday for Warrnambool, where
their retreat starts. There they will watch the
crops and the cattle, and speak kindly to the baby
pig on occasions.
The Malcolm M'Eacharns hie away again in a
week. They’ve had enough of Australia for a
while. Besides, the second Miss of the family,
who is rather a charmer, gets launched into the
gay and giddy vortex this year, and the family
regards this as a dazzling event.
The Stonington Dame has been soothing the
swagger element, who are usually not bidden to
a breaking of bread at the weekly feasts, by a
random tea or two. At these little consolation
rallies the Cream find comfort, and are able to
quote her Ex. in thatfriendly-cum-private fashion
that is the hall-mark of real frill. Very
thoughtful of the vice-regal. But, then, thought-
fulness is her forte.
At the Ormond Women’s Graduate Club
annual dinner the toast-list was honored more
than usual, allowing to several ringleaders a large
talk apiece. It’s a delightful experience to gaze
on a hushed and tongue-tied set of women heark-
ening to one privileged spouter. The suppressed
propensity to interrupt and then pitch a tone
higher than the first starter leaves the face con-
torted and convulsed. I have seen the counten-
ance of a talkative She turn apoplectic purple in a
mental fight with the talking impulse. You
never can get a skirt gathering absorbed in a she-
speaker. The very fact of one of the sex holding
the floor tranquilly and without contradiction is
enough to set all the rest frantic. Now when one
of t’other sex starts to hold forth, all the rest
settle down to a cheerful smoke, thanking their
stars it isn’t their spouting turn.
Professor Osborne, one of the latest importa-
tions to the ’Varsity, has a brainy wife who is
continuing her science course despite domestic
demands. She is now entitled to wear a brilliant
academic gown, and still burrows into learning,
probably to achieve a Joseph coat of divers
colors and high diplomas.
A bold, bad Benedict is flaunting his amorata
in the face of the town. He’s a big swagger
identity, and this reversal of his usually decorous
state is knocking Melbourne silly.
Miss Maggie Mather infuses some personal
magnetism into her piano playing. A large
audience (filling Glen’s Hall better than it had
been filled for many a chilly eve) hearkened in
glee to warm, throbbing melodies of Chopin,
Brahms, and Schumann that seemed for the time
to give life to the ivories. The performer is young,
sweet-faced, and her magnetism is beyond dispute.
Miss Mather hasn’t been trumpeted much, but
she deserves to be. Herr Scharf has the credit of
instructor.
Miss Winter-Irving followed in the Mackinnon
steps, and introduced a Rickardian element into
her afternoon spree last week. The Miles-
Stavordale quintette raised its joint and several
banjos, and a comic man gave funny proceedings
without the usual burnt-cork effects.
This city chews now to slow music. In vain the
business-man searches for a corner where he can
bite a grill in quiet. If the band isn’t making
frivolous music inside the cafe it is stationed out-
side. Menzies started the scone-serenading move-
ment. Now all the bun-coops and grill-resorts
have rounded up an orchestra to deaden the appe-
tite. One day a hustling citizen, who looks to the
lunch-hour for cogitation or consultation with a
client, will rise up and slay the band in cold blood.
The Old Girls of the Fitchett Methodist College
had a spree the other Saturday—a musical-chair
spree with the tops cut off their party-rigs.
Old Girls never foregather with that dash and
diablerie that mark any rally of Old Boys. The
Old Girls are mostly grey and largely wrinkled.
Care and the family whooping-cough or Care and
Single Wretchedness have soured them. Only
now and then does a jolly she-bachelor happen
along to brighten the planet and then she isn’t the
sort to attend the severely formal Old Girls’ even-
ing party. That sort forgets the cats of her youth,
and their middle-age purring interests her not.
A recent arrival from the Cold Country relates
how one of the most expensive and highly-prized
camera men in the Great Smoke sometimes be-
comes so lost in the beauty of the subject as he
has posed her, that he faints standing, stumbles
out of the studio, and sends in an assistant to state
that Mr. Photographer is overcome with great
thoughts, and cannot take any pictures that
morning. Usually the sitter departs with a vague
but beautiful impression of the man’s overwhelm-
ing' genius and a large respect for such an artistic
soul. But sometimes a strenuous lady breaks
up her handsome pose to demand an
explanation. She may even ask if the
expensive artist is drunk. I remember a
rather famous camera-man visiting these parts a
few years ago, and indulging his aesthetic caprices
and emotions in somewhat the same way. In
the midst of arranging a drape, or the lady’s
skirt-edge, he would fall on his knees, possibly
clasp her hands or feet, and murmur brokenly,
“Madame, I cannot photograph you. I love
you.’’ This sometimes sent the surprised beauty
into hysterics or made her shout for the police.
It was only now and then the avowal moved a
Grace to divine pity and forgiveness. But for
the sake of this occasional happening, the less
emotional women were frequently inconvenienced
by the “artist’s” strange behavior.

line of the figure—not merely to picture
clothes. It was the most difficult task
going. And by the time I had manipu-
lated the frills and secured them so that

The Bohemian Club, after a long immunity
from outside gaiety, is going to give a dance in
the Prahran Town Hall, and the announcement
two months ahead argues that the brotherhood of
gay sparks intend to do the thing well—like
Jenkins did.
Some of the senior sticklers at the Athenaeum
Club have never recovered from the blow of that
mixed afternoon tea early this year, when giddy
girleens and staid dames rallied to the refresh-
ments. It met with their cordial disapproval.
The younger fry were strong and won the day,
but ever since the conservative veteran has gone
on his way grimly with a load on his club con-
science.
The Clive Baillieus have now taken a turn at a
grand gad. The numerous Baillieus are always at
sea—in a sense. One never farewells a voyager or
greets one without knocking against a tall, superb
Baillieu gripping a dressing-case.
The youngest of the three acrobatic and con-
tortionist Lees at the Opera House bears an amaz-
ing resemblance to the Vic. Chief Justice dame.
The Cream feel almost indignant about it, and
erect the lorgnette several times to make sure that
the young person in question is still bearing the
resemblance. Occasionally a cheeky grin or a
lightning wink breaks up the likeness and the
Cream breathe freely again. It ought to flatter a
Society marin and her kin to have a young and
beautiful vaudeville girl looking somewhat like
her. It is only when a woman grows to look old
that girls decline flatly to resemble her any longer.
Kinking—this time on bona-fide ice—is the
ecstasy of the cold hour. Or is to be. It.is now
in anticipation. The opening is to be effected
this Saturday (9th) in ceremonious fashion. The
whole of this frivolous community is preparing to
drag on the ice-boots and get a-capering.
Mr. Dash, a Melbourne gentleman who has a
neat office in the city, had the misfortune to receive
an unexpected visit from his wife, at his little
place in Blank Buildings, one pleasant Monday
afternoon recently. The lady found Mr. Dash
somewhat distraught and unnerved, and he
ascribed his agitation to toothache and business
worries, complicated with an overdraft and a
touch of sciatica. The gentleman tried hard to
make his wife go, without making it too apparent
that he wanted her to go; but she realised that
when a man is in pain and adversity a true wife’s
place is at his side, and she stayed there to console
him, and tell him about all the things that she had
ever heard of or read of that were good for tooth-
ache, sciatica, and a nagging overdraft. Suddenly a
very, very far-away voice interrupted her flow of
solicitude, repeating the plaint of the starling.
The voice said it wanted to get out. Dash looked
at Mrs. Dash, and Mrs. Dash looked at Dash, and
the eye that quailed was not the lady’s. “ I want
to get out,” repeated the voice, which seemed to
be strained to the utmost but yet was very still
and small. Mrs. Dash insisted on having the door
of Dash’s strong room opened instantly, and at
length, driven to desperation, he made an effort.
To his horror he discovered himself unequal to the
task. He had never had occasion to use hisburglar-
proof cosj -corner before, and now the door was
closed for the first time, and it resisted all Dash’s
Objurgations and all his ingenuity. Meanwhile,
tears flowed out under the door, while Mrs. Dash
mounted guard with a face like Fate. In the end
an expert opened the door, and the young person
who had inadvertently wandered into Dash’s vault
fainted out before Mrs. Dash and witnesses. This
is as far as the story has gone to date.
They’re keen on the “Juliet hair-net” just
now. It’s a tricky jewelled affair that has to be
set on the most eminent hair-billow or bump, and
is designed to catch the local Romeo quicker than
any other net, visible or invisible. Of course
only fresh young things are supposed to flaunt
the hair-net; but, as usual, advanced ladies of
the solus variety are hoisting it, more probably as
a bait than a decoration.
One divorced lawyer in this State has a mar-
vellous regard for the mate who blighted his
affections and cut the bond. He seems to blot
out from memory that cutting act altogether, and
retains only recollections of when they two were
jolly good pals in the long ago. Only a man
could do that. Fancy any deserted She speak-
ing, under any circumstances, with pride and
delight of the brute who fled with another ?
“ Lerli,” from London :
These fine spring afternoons I betake myself to Hyde
Park, where the aristocracy and plutocracy take the air
in carriage and automobile. I am on foot, and I gaze upon
the elite of England, the flower of that Empire which
has proved too large an egg for the sun to set on. Being
still young, I scan the various vehicles to see what the
aristocracy or plutocracy has of lovely women to
offer to my gaze, and I am disappointed, almost
disgusted. In all, I have seen in an afternoon half-a-
dozen women whom I might, in a rash mood, be prepared
to ruin myself for—if they were seeking my ruin, of
course. The others are commonplace. They have’the
features of the suburban tradesman and his wife, over
which they have tried to spread the real Yere de
Vere look of dignity and polite ennui. But most
of the people in the carriages are old—dowagers,
widows, and such-like —and it would seem that
old age with these wealthy, privileged people is
more hideous than with the poor. See the old Chelsea
pensioners—they are hale and hearty old fellows ; but see
the old Earl of Something, or the' Countess—they are
decrepit, shrivelled-up, and pitiful. I am not one of those
narrow-minded Democrats who can see no good at all in
an aristocracy. The pity is that England’s privileged
class should make such a hog of itself, and miss its g'reat
chances to do something, for the good of the people and
as a for itself.
Yours Affectionately,

A CUKE FOR ASTHMA.
A Noted Physician will prove this to ail
Sufferers.
It is but natural that the majority of sufferers
from asthma, after trying doctors and numberless
remedies without avail, have come to the con-
clusion that there is no cure for this most dis-
tressing disease. These same persons may still
have doubt when they learn through the columns
of the press that Dr. Rudolph Schiffmann, the
recognised authority, who has treated more eases
of asthma than any living doctor, has at last
achieved success. He has perfected a remedy
which not only gives immediate relief in the
worst cases, but has positively cured thousands of
sufferers who were considered incurable. These
persons had previously been just as sceptical as
some of our readers now are. Dr. Schiffmann s
remedy doubtless possesses the merit which is
claimed for it, or he would not extend an invi-
tation to every sufferer to make a free personal
test of it. He authorises this paper to announce
that he is not only willing to give free to any
person suffering from asthma and bronchitis in
Australasia one free liberal “trial package” of
his Cure, but urgently requests all sufferers to
send him their name, to whom a sample will be
posted free of charge. He fears that in making
the claim he does for his Cure, a strong doubt
may arise in the minds of many, and knows that a
personal test, as ho offers to all, will be more
convincing and prove its merit, than thousands of
testimonials which he could publish from persons
who have been permanently cured by the use of his
remedy. “Dr. Schiffmann’s Asthma Cure, as it
is called, has been sold by most chemists for
several years, although many persons may never
have heard of it, and it is with a view to reaching
these that he makes this offer. This is certainly a
most generous and fair offer, and all who are
suffering from any of the above complaints should
send to Dr. R. Schiffmann’s Depot, Nos. 5 and 7
Barrack-street, Sydney. He requests that you
send him simply your name and address plainly
on a post-card, nothing else.* '

POLITICAL
POINTS
Adelaide, though unable to run electric
cars itself, is proud of being able to make other
people’s. One firm, which has just sent over ten
cars for theEssendon Tram Co., has had four con-
tracts of the kind for Victoria within the last two
years.
Sydney Telegrabh casts laborious sneers
at the Commerce Act. “Is this the glorious ideal
of promoting Federal unity ? ” it asks. , Well
“ One people, one continent, one honesty ” isn’t a
bad bond of fraternity. And that’s what the Com-
merce Act aims at.
Prime Minister Deakin, in reply to a
voluble pest who infested the top gallery: “If
wise forethought is nervousness, then I am ner-
vous. I can’t lose sight of the fact that to-day
Australia is the only large, practically unoccupied,
piece of habitable land on this planet.”
Britain now seriously reduces the status
of the Cape Colony naval station, the ships being
withdrawn to England. Quickly the legions are
being concentrated to guard the heart of the
empire. And still politicians like W. Kelly urge
the limbs to make no effort to protect themselves,
but to rely on the “ mother country.”
While the Japs were in Melbourne they
were shown over the local Safe Deposit, and its
intricacies were explained to them. This is true
courtesy. They have been shown the safe de-
posits and the banks. They have gone round the
pubs., and have located the cash registers. And
they have seen the defences. What more do they
want ?
Bigjohn Forrest, Federal Treasurer, has
declared himself definitely opposed to a Federal
graduated land-tax. Bigjohn reckons that the
Commonwealth doesn’t require the money, which
suggests that he knows very little about his own
cash-box. Also, Bigjohn is surprised at anyone
suggesting a Federal land-tax, “considering that
we have Customs and Excise exclusively our-
selves,” which suggests that Bigjohn has quite
forgotten that the States get three-fourths of the
Customs and Excise money. One way and an-
other, the necessity for the Australian Ministry
to throw Bigjohn overboard begins to loom pretty
large. He is very lfttle good as crew, and'he is
too troublesome as a passenger, and he shifts
about too much to make useful ballast.

Sydney Telegraph’s London correspon-
dent, dealing with farming life in England
The N.S. Wales farmer, who often is heard to bitterly
bewail his hard lot, would regard himself as the victim
of a terrible nightmare were he dumped down on the
Golden Plain to farm under the same conditions as the
Huntingdonshire Fen man. The man who rents on the
Golden Plain the farm of 300 acres, which is so often
regarded in Australia as an insufficient living area, would
find himself set the task of making £lOOO a year to clear
his rent and taxes. He would find his railway rates just
about three times as much as he had been accustomed to
pay in N.S. Wales, and he would find that unless he put
expensive manures freely upon the land his returns would
not be worth the garnering.
These sort of things creep into the Telegraph by
accident. As a matter of general policy in that
paper, the N.S.W. farmer is the unfortunate victim
on whose tariff-burdened shoulders fall the
exactions of the greedy protectionist manufacturer.
Victoria now sweats its shire secretaries
and other officials so severely that it is almost
impossible to make a living by working for one
set of municipal bosses only. The sec. and
engineer of a shire which has just found out that
it is insolvent acts in a like capacity for two other
shires, is engineer for two more, valuer to two,
and rate collector for one shire and a railway
trust. Besides this, he is “agent for contrac-
tors, architects, and surveyors.” Over this
last part of his occupation trouble has natur-
ally arisen ; and, in the dust that is being
raised in consequence, the inherent
meanness of the landlords who boss
the shires escapes notice —likewise
their folly in splitting the country
up into ridiculously small districts.

of London Times is gloomy
about the Kitchener military
regime in India. The position
in India, he says, “is fraught
with grave danger.” Lord
Kitchener has “ aroused so great
an indignation that we are on the verge

of knowledge and lack of personal
courtesies.”
A Bairnsdale (Vic.) parson has drawn
attention to the high educational value of
Hempire Day—God bless it!—by quoting some
extracts from the essays composed by local
school-children. One infant Imperialist wrote:
“ Of all the sovereigns that reigned on the throne,
Queen Victoria was the only or.o that reigned
longest. God save the King.” The ardent
loyalty of another youngster took a statistical
turn—“ King Edward rules over about 400,000,000
people, more than one-fourth of whom are of
mankind.”
The S.M. Herald’s great joke about
the Australian capital:—
It. is doubtful if the active co-operation of the (Aus-
tralian) Government will be accorded to the representa-
tives of the mother State in their desire to settle this
momentous question.
The desire of certain representatives of the Ma
State to settle the momentous question by doing
all the selecting of the capital themselves and
letting the rest of Australia have no voice in the
choice of its own capital, has been so earnest as to
be quite noticeable.
Bigjohn Forrest has pointed out that it
would be useless to appoint an Australian High
Commissioner who could not scatter gold in the
Cold Country. The Commissioner must be in a
position to leave his oof marks all over the place,
or he will “make no impression.” Now Malcolm
M‘Eacharn, who just answers Forrest’s descrip-
tion of an ideal High Commissioner, comes along,
and has no hesitation in saying John is right. He
holds that “the success of Canada is due to Lord
Strathcona being an extremely wealthy man.”
According to Malcolm, the Canadian lord gives
rich, bloated dinners to 400 people at a time.
Neither John Forrest nor Malcolm M‘Eacharn
says a word about the necessity of any kind of
ability or patriotism or energy in the High Com-
missioner, and this seems to strengthen the sus-
picion that they are after the job.
The Commerce Act, taken in connection
with the regulations gazetted under it, is the only
really serious effort made in the Parliamentary
history of Australia to compel the commercial
classes to recognise the existence of moral
laws. There have been State Acts which
diffidently hinted that commercial
thievery is thievery, that commercial
lying is lying, and that obtaining
money by commercial misrepresenta-
tion is plain false pretences, but the
hints in the Acts were very diffident,
and the regulations under them very
vague and inconclusive. Some raw
amateurs might fall in under these
Acts, but the reasonably capable com-
mercial thief, commercial liar, and
commercial poisoner, flourished in
every State, despite Acts and regu-
lations, and often found them rather
an assistance than otherwise. The
Commonwealth Commerce Act lines
up the whole band of professional
robbers, and tells them that if they
say their goods are of a kind they are
not, of a size they are not, or of a
weight they are not, they will be held
accountable for the lies. This Act
alone is sufficient to justify the crea-
tion of the Commonwealth.
Of the 1 2 members elected to
the Queens’ and Assembly in 1890,
only 13 are i a the present Assembly.
Nineteen art dead, five have gone to
the Australi m Parliament, two are
Ms.L.C., anc 33 have retired or been
passed out b;' the disgusted electors.
In this wreckage, the Labor party
has had its share of casualties. It has lost
three members by death, four have been passed
out, four have gone to the Australian Par-
liament, while six remain in the Assembly. Truly
a procession of shadows.
A letter from Western Victoria :
The pathetic emptiness of Eeid’s alleged policy was
made strikingly evident by the “campaign” which Joe
Cook and the scrag end of the Tory brigade have just
conducted hereabouts. The campaign was a humorous
affair right from the start, and before it concluded it had
become a screaming farce. The performers were Joe
Cook (in the lead),. Grattan Wilson (who mis-represents
the Warrnambool district on a minority vote), and
Robinson (who mis-represents the adjoining constituency
of Wannon, also on a minority vote). The troupe opened
at Geelong, and afterwards appeared at Colac, Camper-
down, Warrnambool, and Koroit. By talking loudly and
fiercely, Cook usually managed to get through his part
without the audience discovering anything ; but as the
milk-and-watery Wilson and Robinson began to speak the
electors started to ask questions. At every meeting
Wilson explained, with tears in his voice, that though he
followed a Freetrade leader he was “a Protectionist to the
backbone ”; and right to the end of the tour he continued
to be amazed that the electors should see anything incon-
sistent in his position. Both Wilson and Robinson were
“ opposed ” to quite a number of things ; but so far not a
sign of their being in favor of anything has appeared.
At time of writing the meek pair (Cook having been lost
on the voyage) were in the wilds of Glenelg, where the
bee-farmers live, and I hope no one will throw a hive at
them and miss.
