“V.” : The press cable monopolists are
giving us too many of the trivialities of English
politics. In a newspaper this week I find 14 lines
devoted to informing Australia that Chamberlain
accusedC. Bannermanof ‘‘impertinence.” Above
the 14 lines there are big black headings. What’s
the good of cabling young-ladyish repartee like
that to a community that has possessed a M'Elhone
and a Dove of Ararat ?
May 24, being Empire Day, and being
marked by the visit of the Japanese fleet to
Sydney, evoked numberless speeches, articles, and
clerical addresses on the glories of the Jap, liis
chivalry, his magnanimity, his bravery, and his
religious feeling. And all this applause went to
Buddha on the vital feast-day of the Christian
religion, Ascension Day. Any casual stranger
might have thought it was Buddha who ascended.
Said that the Carruthers Government
hopes to settle one or two tangles in this way :
Chief Railway Commissioner Oliver will go to the
to-be-formed Lands Commission ; his subordinate,
Kirkcaldie, will go to Victoria ; his other subor-
dinate, Fehon, will retire; and the imported
Canadian, Tait, will come from Victoria to act as
N.S.W. Chief Railway Commissioner and to make
things as cheerful and lively as he has done in
Bent’s State.
Queensland Land Court is a cantankerous
Tory body with a great love for big estates and
sparse settlement. Several large areas which
have lately fallen in, and which were wanted by
the Department of Lands for closer settlement,
have been again leased for a term of ten years in
spite of official protest. The court did not even
give the Minister a chance to put his case before
it. The Kidston Government intends to Do
Things in this matter when Parliament meets.
N.S.W. P.S. Board invites applications
for male teachers for small up-country schools,
applicants to be unmarried, to be less than 35
years of age, and to be paid £72 per annum.
Granting the probability of the successful ones
being selected from the young candidates, it is
putting a mighty poor premium on “ gentility ”
to ask an educated man of anything up t 035 to go
into the bush, and be a learned and patient celi-
bate for less than 30s. a week. He could make
much more than that rabbitting.
G. H. Reid, at South Botany, t’other
day :
The highest ideal for an industrial peace was that in
which the two great powers of Labor and Capital should
come to some arrangement, not by compulsion, hut con-
scious of their own rights and of their own power.
The highest ideal of social peace is that in which
Yes-No might sleep peacefully in Hyde Park any
night, and find his watch where it ought to be
next morning. But, because we haven’t that
ideal yet, does Yes-No advocate abolishing the
police ?
Sydney, as occasionally mentioned in this
paper, has 41 mayors and sets of aldermen, and
the alarm of these useless functionaries lest they
should be abolished to make room for one good,
vigorous, modern municipal system is pathetic,
The city of Sydney has an electric-lighting system.
Paddington and the other municipalities (bar
Redfern) have none. Many Paddington people
who want electric light and can’t get it from their
own municipal insects, have asked the City
Council for a supply, and lately the City Council
greatly alarmed the small municipal potentates
of Paddington by offering to carry the electric
light into their gloomy region. Paddington
aldermen agreed that “ the enemy was insidiously
creeping upon them,” and that unless they woke
up and resisted outside improvements they might
one day find themselves improved out of exist-
ence. The prospect was so terrifying that the
aldermen postponed their decision and went home
to think the matter out.
N.S.W. Public Service Board is supposed
to have its quinquennial regrading of the service
ready by June 30, the last day of the financial
year. It has not made a personal inspection of
the departments either personally or by deputy,
so the regrading will be little better than a farce.
It is freely stated that the Board will increase
only a few salaries in the service, mainly because
it has no money, and also because it has so much
to do, or else is so slow in doing it, that it has not
time to investigate individual cases. The remedy
officers have, the Board states, is an appeal against
their regrading, but, if practically everyone is left
out in the cold and everyone appeals, the Board,
which can’t consider cases for want of time, will
be just as short of time then, and things will be as
they were. It is a Board of old men—an old,
tired, prosy, long-winded Board, and it lingers
and hesitates, and holds up its ear-trumpet and
dusts its spectacles while the service toddles to
the devil.
The Prohibitionists of Maoriland lately
published a map which professed to show that at
last local option poll there was a No-License
majority all over the country, and the only thing
which prevented an overwhelming victory was
the nefarious law which requires a two-thirds
majority to carry the abolition of der-rink. The
reasoning seems curiously inconclusive. The No-
License party, it is claimed, can raise a majority
(though not a two-thirds majority) almost every-
where. Now a bare majority is good
enough at a Parliamentary election,
so it should be possible to capture
Parliament. Then the bare
majority in Parliament could
enact that a bare majority (in-
stead of a two-thirds majority)
will suffice at a local option
poll, and after that the thing
is done. Why isn’t it done?
It looks easy enough.
“Itan” (indignantly) from
Queensland: —
Sydney Telkgrapii persistently misrepresents the posi-
tion of the real Labor party in Queensland by quoting
Boot’s Worker. As a matter of fact, there are only seven
Labor members who have not openly refused to subscribe
to the “ objective.” Two of these seven own land, lately
acquired, while another favors a syndicate railway in his
own electorate, and a fourth advocates sales of land in
his own district for settlement purposes, while yet
another, who advocates one-man-one-billet, keeps someone
out of a job by helping to run a weekly newspaper. The
policy of the Kidston party, which has a large and in-
creasing following, is very simple: (1) Queensland must
stop borrowing and pay its way ; (2) no large areas of
land to be sold to one owner; (3) all proceeds of land
sales to be used in the construction of public works ; (4)
assistance to people without means to become primary
producers ; (5) bursting up of big estates, by a progressive
land-tax; (6) assistance in mining and industrial centres
to workers to get homes of their own so that they will not
have to pay rent. What objection has the D.T. to this
platform ?
Premier Bent’s intimation to the Aus-
tralian Government that Victoria will in future
require rent for Melbourne Government House,
now occupied by the Governor-General, gave
Carruthers a chance to rise up, with a local
patriotic expression in his eye and an injury to the
Ma State in his right hand. “ If,” he said, “ the
Federal Government pays the Victorian Govern-
ment rent, it must pay us rent, too.” This method
of putting it left it to be inferred that there was
some sort of “Victorian conspiracy” afoot, by
which Bent was going to obtain £3OOO a year from
Deakin ; but that Carruthers had his eagle eye on
the scheme, and if Victoria got anything from the
Federal Treasury he would see that N.S.W. had
her “rights,” and got something, too. Then
Bent explained that the whole affair had been
rigged up at the late Premiers’ Conference ; that
it was arranged that he was to demand rent for
Victoria’s Government House, and Carruthers
was to do the same for N.S.W. Now why didn’t
these two State Premiers put in their bills
simultaneously, and show that it was a joint
arrangement? Or why didn’t Joe put in his bill
first? And why did -Toe look as if he had been
suddenly and unexpectedly “druv” to ask for
rent by the unexpected action of the grasping
Victorian when he meant to ask for it in any
case ?
Maoriland farmers’ unions have a righteous
detestation of spending money, even to assist
their own cause. Lately, the I aimers’ Con-
ference at Christchurch was asked to approve of
a vote of £lOOO a year to the leader of the Opposi-
tion, so that he might be better able to travel the
State per motor-car and preach the anti-Sosli
propaganda. But the delegates showed no
enthusiasm. They thought the billet would be
“too comfortable,’ and that increasing opulence
would infect Massey with the microbe of inertia,
andthat-tlie noble cause of Toryism, anti-Sosh
and land-grab would suffer in consequence. So
Opposition-leader Massey, who is a farmer and
not rich, is expected to neglect his hay-crop in
the landlords’ interests at his own expense, as
heretofore.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Premier Pent has heaved a bombshell into the
cricket dispute. A while back, to fill up vacancies, Bent
appointed six new trustees of the Melbourne Cricket
Ground—the Minister, Secretary of Lands and four
M’s LA As there were only three of the original trustees
left—Roderick Murchison, J. M'Laughlan, and T. F. Mon-
crieff—the new crowd bossed the situation. The M.C.C.
kicked, one of its amusing, self-made bye-laws being that
al'. trustees of the ground must be members of the com-
mittee of the club, but Bent had the law behind him and
stood firm. Since their appointment these six trustees
have been investigating the deed of grant of this lump of
public land, and they find that, under the said deed,
made June 21st, 1862, the land is granted “ to provide
a site or place for playing at cricket within the
city of Melbourne,” and shall be used for no other V !!> ~
pose whatsoever. If the trustees fail to keep the land and
buildings in proper order, or if they sell or lease any poi-
tion thereof, the State has the right to re-enter and take
possession forthwith. The deed makes no mention what-
ever of the M.0.C., wherefore the six new men set to
work to discover how the cheeky club came to be there at
all Thev found that it had a sort of permissive occupancy
of the paddock before ’62, but of course the deed of grant
annulled that, and there is no trace whatever of the
trustees having at anytime given the club a permissive
occupancy. Further, under this deed the trustees
have no right to delegate their power to anyone
else, and they alone have the right to use the
ground and collect revenue from it. There is
as vet no hint that the new trustees propose to kick
the' M.O.C. out neck and crop; still, they say they
“intend to fulfil the duties of their trust ’ —which is to
say, they practically own the ground and the buildings
thereon, and they can let it to whomsoever they darn
well please, and on such terms as they please, for cricket
only. So the Melbourne Cricket Club may presently find
itself a mere push on a street corner, without a ground,
or club room, or grand stand, or bowling green, or any«
thing. * If, f’rinstans, these six—a majority of the existing
nine trustees —choose to let their ground and buildings
to, say, the V.C.A. to play inter-State or international
matches, they are legally entitled to do so. As to where
the M.C.C. would be then, that is a matter that concerns
its members only. *
At the Sydney end of the scrimmage the N.S.W.
Cricket Association is proceeding quietly on its triumphant
way. On Tuesday afternoon last Trumper, Cotter, and
Hopkins were passed out to join Noble and co. At the
coming monthly meeting the Association will proceed
to vest itself with new powers, and, ®‘ter it is
dressed in its new fighting suit, Noble, trumper
and co. will find themselves quietly but firmly
shelved for at least 12 months, although a strong party
will make a fight for two years. If the suspended players
think they are the hub of N.S.W. cricket, they are likely
to have their mistake revealed very plainly before long.
In a little while they will realise, with some bitterness,
what a deuce of a lot opportunity plays in the affaus
of this life. Already lists of players have been
made, from which the next State Eleven and the N.S.W.
portion of the next Australian XT. will be chosen. The
earthquake that has struck the M.C.C. in relation to its
inability to prevent the V.C.A. having the use of
Melbourne Cricket Ground for inter-State and inter-
national cricket has practically hroken-the back ot the
insurrection.
* * *
Dwyer, who was a member of the ground staff
of the Sydney Cricket Ground a few seasons back, re-
cently went to the Cold Country, and is now a regular
member of the Sussex eleven, for which county lie has
qualified. In his own land but few ever heard of the
professional ground bowler. Now someone cables the
fact, at considerable expense, that Dwyer, in a county
fixture, captured nine wickets for 35 runs.
* * * *
So far in Sydney the oaf at the goal has not been
muddied, except when he mixed his own nose blood with
the dust of the touch-line. Saturday s round brought
about something like a levelling up, Eastern Suburbs,
owing to its defeat by University, losing its lead. Glebe
and S with Sydney managed to escape getting to the top
of the list by making their match a draw, and the result
is that no less than six clubs (Eastern Suburbs, Glebe,
South Sydney, Sydney, University and Newtown) are now
level with seven competition points each. North Sydney
and Western Suburbs follow close with six and five points
respectively. ,
The match of the day was that between Eastern
Suburbs and University at the Oval. Ten thousand
people rolled up to watch it, and did not come away dis-
appointed. Immediately play started the ball was kicked
over the Urn. line, and an exciting race was won by the
Uni who forced. Almost immediately afterwards Mes-
senger of E.S., marked at the centre and kicked a beau-
ful goal. Then the Uni. backs let loose a passing charge
that went through E.S. like the wind, and scored a try,
and thereby equalised the scores. Messenger played a
o-uileful game, and although several of his ruses failed
he kicked a penalty goal, and set the Suburbs in
the lead by three. Again the Uni. machine broke
through but its pass was smothered, the tack-
ling of the Suburbs hacks being very effective.
The play became brilliant, though somewhat inch-
vidualistic, and then the University pulled its com-
bination together again and, hurling the Suburbs down
on its line, attacked like wild cats. The Uni. forwards
heeled beautifully, but the Suburbs backs came rushing
round the scrum and smothered with great energy.
Twice the Uni. men took shots at goal, but the flying
E S forwards bustled the kicks. With a desperate effort
the Suburbs forwards cleared the line, and then someone,
in his anxiety to avert danger, got off-side and preci-
pitated the disaster that he was so anxious to
prevent for the University kicked a penalty goal, and
made the score level. The Suburbs took a deep breath
and went in to do things. A dribbling rush swept clean
over the struggling University forwards, who were
demoralised by the speed and combination of it. Oxen-
ham came at the line with a mighty rush, head down,
an d ri'ri't off their toes he took the leather, dashed
through the startled line, and was away towards the
Suburbs’ goal. He flew between the halves, he ran round
rhe three-quarters, and tried to go over the full-back, but
D’Alpugct held on grimly. With a wrench the University
back tore himself free as Hughes raced up .and shouted
for the pass. It went, and next moment Hughes had it
across the line. A splendid run, and as splendid a
defence by the full-back, but he could not block both
men That made the scores nine to six in favor of the
University, and before E.S. could get going again the
whistle sounded. It was a great game, and the first
defeat the Suburbs team has sustained this season.
* * * *
At Sydney Sports Grounds the Glebe and
South Sydney seemed a perfect match. The advant-
age of ' weight in the pack looked slightly in
favor of Glebe, while the backs of the S.S. equa-
lised things by their accurate line-kicking. Ihe full-
back in particular repeatedly sent the game inside
Glebe 25 by most accurate shooting. Tins led to a
series of Wild struggles right on the Glebe line, but
the tacklin<>- of the Reds was very deadly, and
that was where the illegalities happened. South Sydney
was determined to cross the line and didn t care how it
did it. Glebe was just as determined it shouldn t, and
didn’t care how it prevented it. The first half ended
without either side having scored. The second half found
both more determined than ever, and the leather lolled
hack and forward from 25 to 25. Neither side got its back
division going, and the match presented the curious spec-
tacle of all the passing rushes being carried out by the
forwards, and blocked by the backs of the opposing
team. Bleeding noses were the order of the afternoon,
and at one stage of the game South Sydney was playing
with no less than three men short, owing to casualties
and suspensions. Time ran out before either side could
score.
* * * *
At last, and none too soon, Sydney Metropolitan
Ru"-by Union has decided to suppress rough play with
the axe. Glebe-South Sydney and Balmain-Western
Suburbs matches were melees on Saturda} r last, and every
afternoon has seen the players “ mixing it ” in response
to the frenzied shouts of the barraekers. The explana-
tion is that a new species of bookmaker has arisen—a
brand of the beast that lays the odds on football matches
alone—and large sums of money have begun to change
hands. So far the bookmakers have apparently not risen
to the affluence of squaring the players, and the
men who have big wagers on won’t allow any trilling
regulations to come between them and victory.
There have been a lot of ugly “accidents,” and the pre-
sence of footballers in bruising clubs and halls settling
“ old scores ” has not made for the credit of the game.
Unless the Metropolitan Rugby Union comes down with
its lethal weapon, and passes out a few very bad cases,
the rough play and gambling that are becoming features
of the game will ultimately lead to its downfall.
Glorious autumn weather helped the “gates
of Melbourne footballers last Saturday, and the customary
surprises came along to maintain interest in the compe-
titions. Last year’s premiers, Fitzroy, went down before
Essendon by 46 points to 51, a circumstance that demands
more explanation than it is likely to get; while Colling-
wood returned once again to its proper form and wiped
the floor with St. Hilda to the tune of 89 points to 31.
The other two League matches were played on the Mon-
day, when Geelong beat Melbourne (75 points to 56 was
the score), and the unbeaten Carlton met the redoubt-
able South and just saved its record by four
points (69 to 65). The chief surprise of the round,
however, was furnished by the Association ex-pre-
miers, the Richmond men, undefeated so far, who
either took matters too easily or got their tails between
their legs. After letting Williamstown score 39 points to
nine in the first half, they plodded along in a bedraggled,
dispirited fashion till the bell rang, with the scores 10
goals 11 behinds to Richmond’s 6 goals 8 behinds. An
inter-State team had arrived from Adelaide that morning,
with intent to play the Vic. Association on the Monday ;
and as Richmond had done most of the reception honors
perhaps that accounted for the debacle. Two St. Hilda
players retired from Saturday’s argument with ’Wood,
one with a badly sprained ankle, the other with a small
bone in his instep broken. These casualties will appear
in the balance sheet as “medical expenses” and “new
boots.”
* * * •
Sydney British Association clubs have made a
few slight alterations in the paired appearance of their
competition list. Pyrmont is now on top with 10 points,
Glebe and Rozelle tie with eight each, Balmain is third
with six, and Granville and Ultimo have four each.
In Saturday’s match, in spite of the fact that it
started with the sun in its eyes, Pyrmont was soon
attacking* the Balmain goal line, but the Balmain full-
back worked like a Trojan and saved his side repeatedly.
Balmain forwards made gallant attempts to break
through, but the Pyrmont half-back line was firm as a
rock, and back again Pyrmont came to the goal line, press-
ing pressing till the opening was made and the goal won.
Balmain made wild efforts which were quite unavailing.
On resuming Balmain got more combination into the
attack, and a really brilliant shot had the misfortune
to strike the cross-bar. Pyrmont got its head down and
cleared the line by sheer weight, and, getting the Balmain
men on the trot, kept them going till the Pyrmont score
was increased by another goal. Again Balmain hit the
post, and Pyrmont swept down once more and took the
third goal; and then, with a very fast rush, Balmain had
its third shot at goal, and managed to score at last.
Pyrmont scored again, and Balmain made a second
goal. So the game ended—Pyrmont four, Balmain
two. Other matches were : Rozelle’s defeat of Glebe by
three to two, and Granville’s victory over South Sydney
by six to two. Out at St. Peters, Ultimo trod on Sydney
to the tune of two to nil.
Monday last was the first day of the Rugby
Country week, and resulted in a practical draw between
Sydney players and the men from Ironbark. New England,
hard as nails and full of wind as a liquid air com-
pressor wiped Sydney out by 11 to five. The
Northern team trod on Glebe to some purpose,
downing the bright reds by 14 to six. University and
Eastern Suburbs upheld the honor of the town, the col-
legians beating the Western team by 28 to 16, and the
E.S. men beating the Western team by 16 to 9. Country
teams may not know much of what the Telegraph
calls the “ finer points of the game,” but for grit and
stamina they are all there.

First and second steeplechases at A. J.C. meet-
ing- were depressing spectacles Saturday’s event saw
seven in commission. Four —Rise Up, Probus, W.H.A.,
and Kaffir—failed to complete the journey, and Auri-
culum. Up-to-I)ate, and Dancing Joe got home as named.
Up-to-Date fenced faultlessly, though carrying 591 b. in
excess of the winner’s load. Monday’s display proved a
sorrier exhibition still. Of four starters, Aurieulum
came a cropper ere traversing six furlongs, and four leaps
later saw Kaffir and his rider sprawling, while the same
obstacle brought W.H.A. down in the next round, leav-
ing Up-to-Date (the safest conveyance seen in Sydney for
many a dav) to finish solus. Thus was £9O (second and
third money) of the £3OO offered saved to the club.

Tasmanian jumper Mercury’s win of A.J.C.
Second Hurdles, Monday, was a staggerer for punters
generally, but someone gathered a good haul, for Ikey
Mo laid his coin from 20’s down to 3’s, the latter taken
while the bulk of the crowd was watching the start.
Sheer grit and remarkable stamina kept Mercury in front
from first six furlongs to the end of a 2J miles’ run.
Inside two weeks, the visitor showed poorly indeed at
Rosehill, and improved little at Randwick on Saturday,
but, let down 41b. in the weights, he was a widely dif-
ferent animal on Monday.

Rev. Fitchett, talking to a Melbourne City
Mission the other night got excited t P won
subject than his dear old Empire and the bleeds that w on
it Fitchett this time invited patriotic abomination foi
“ the scandalous tote shops, that bear to the bo <ty social
the relations that the pustules on a small-pox patient
bear to the body human.” Somebody has supplied him
with details which have a bad odor of truth about
them “In connection with one tote-shop says the
reverend crusader, “ thirty-seven men are employed, ana
all their records are in Pentridge. Twelve of them are
distinguished pickpockets. Four have been under
sentence of death—commuted to 12 years imprisonment
in each case—for outrages on women. These are the
forces for evil amongst us. They are a menace to eve y
mother’s son. They have their secret police their own
detectives, they wage an open shameless war-
fare against society, and they have allies in
high places,” &c., Ac. Fitchett, Judkins and Co
are on a good wicket just now, and the
harder they slog the faster they will score. But,
unfortunately, they don’t know enough about the tote-
punting to put in some effective strokes in the £ s. a.
direction. If Fitchett wants to rouse the virtuous in-
dignation of Young Australia he must be able to talk
about the way the tote returns are faked, and he must
tell his Christian friends what proportion of the nominal
tote dividends is represented by the odd threepences
that are retained by a grasping management. The loss
of the odd “trays” is a social evil that touches the
pockets of thousands of infatuated punters. It has been
calculated that tens of thousands of long beers are thus
annually diverted from their natural channels.
. * » *
It is no good going against your luck. A large
punter, who lives at the Oriental Hotel (Melbourne),
rushed out in his pyjamas and overcoat on a recent
Tuesday morning, fell into a cab, and made a wild dash
for the Bendigo express, to get to the races at that golden
city. He got to the gates just in time to see the tram
leave. Every favorite won. Next day he got up at half-
past six, got to the station half-an-hour too soon, lost
£3O going up in the train, and when he got there the
second races were postponed till the following
Wednesday.

“Gun”: Was surprised to read in Tuesday’s
Svdnev dailies that from the press stand (in front of the
post) Kirrinoor (placed third) appeared to win Second
Nursery at Randwick on Monday. Writer was close to
the judge’s box, and hasn’t the smallest doubt but that
the horses were placed right—Yabba Gabba, Banzai, and
Kirrinoor. In fact, an accumulation of Moses on the
slope offered twos against Kirrinoor after they passed the
post. So much for what pressmen see from their position.

The Argus people certainly know how to
advertise cheaply. A year or two ago they presented an un-
imposing challenge cup for competition among . public
schools—not State schools, but the kind of institution to
which the moneyed citizen sends his sons. Iwo
meetings have been held, at which half-a-dozen
schools went through all kinds of sports merely
that the winning school might hold The Argus
and the Australasian Challenge Cup for a year.
Weslev College won it this time. But the holding of the
utensil is a mere circumstance beside the chance the con-
tests give for pages of illustrations about “ The Argus
and The Australasian Challenge Cup ’ —leadeis, sub-
leaders silly season letters, and columns of fashion notes,
aU <,-iven rise to by “The Argus and The Australasian
Challenge Cup,” and having the clumsy title of that vessel
blazoned at frequent intervals down the column. AM all
this fuss about one poor little slop-basin seems destined to
continue, even unto the day of the great-grandsons of the
youths who are at present unconsciously advertising the
Argus. „ *

According to the latest reports the Victorian
police were still busy issuing warrants against J. I. Baly,
whom they affirm is identical with “ Professor Kemp,
the rough-rider. In the meantime the buck-jumping
show' meanders on the uneven tenor of its way, and last
week the Age arose in its wrath and cursed its ladies
contest as an “ unedifying ” spectacle. A similarly un-
edifving ” spectacle had taken place the previous week,
and y was reported by the Aom without any
rin<>- of horror in its voice. One of the com-
petitors, Miss Macdonald, is a Victorian girl,
and the other competitor was Miss Kemp, a Queens-
lander. Neither lady was thrown on that occasion, and
the judges were unable to decide the owmer of the
championship, and ordered the competitors to change
horses and ride again the following Wednesday night.
To give the women a clear fall in the case of then being
unable to sit the horses, the stirrups (and not the ladies
feet as the Age man seems to have thought) were
fastened underneath the horses, so that they could
not flv up. Both horses were urged to buck by
the use of a flank rope, and “Black Bob,” after
a tremendous exhibition of bucking, managed to unseat
the Victorian girl. This was the same horse that had
previously failed to unseat Miss Kemp. Miss Macdonald
is a wonderful horsewoman, but she had essayed the
almost impossible task of riding two savage outlaws m
the one night, and, being a rather light woman, her
strength gave out before that of the animals she rode.
Miss Kemp managed to ride both her horses, and also
Black Bob, but of course he was more or less bucked
out ” after his bout with Miss Macdonald.

British naval experts have said that the Jap
big gun shooting at Tsushima was anything but accurate,
and that on an average something like 1 5 per cent, of the
shots missed. So apparently the Russian shooting must
have been absolutely damnable. When the Jap squadron
was here its riflemen were so badly beaten in Mel-
bourne that out of courtesy to the feelings of our brave
brown allies ” the scores were not published. At bydnej
the squadron sent a team against the Council of the
National Rifle Association, and the result was another
Jap debacle. Teams of 15 competitors fired at the run-
ning man,” who travelled 100yds. in one minute. The
Australians averaged 11’6 shots per man, and loaded
sino-lv. The Japs used the magazine, and averaged only
10-2 shots per man, taking much longer time to aim than
the Australians. Yet the visitors scored only 186 to the
Australians 309. Banzai! Also, whoop 1

The really up-to-date motor-car is a thing of
beautv, and instead of being shot at by irate:*» r ™ ers
other scoundrels on the land, will be haded as a boon
and an ornament. “ Motocones, an Austrian inven-
tion, are little cones of some chemical mixture costing
about lOd. each, which, when dissolved (one to 10 gallons)
in petrol or benzine, give motor-car
odor of heliotrope. In course of time the means of vary
irur the odor will be discovered, and the modern motor-
car with its eight-note horn making sweet music as it
sroe’salonv, will breathe forth a fragrance of new-mown
hay, and horses will pursue it instead of flying in terror
and asses will try to head it off, and cows will gather
round it at every stopping place.

The League of Victorian Wheelmen has granted
permission for the Dunlop Warrriambool-Melbourne road
race to be run on Sept. 22. As usual, the company
arranges for supplying competitors with solid and liquid
fuel in the course of the journey. The on y new condi-
tion is a rule which forbids the use of single tube racing
tyres • this was interpolated for the purpose of placing
the city and country racers on absolutely equal condi-
tions. Competitors are forbidden to utilise pacers oi to
change machines during the race. T' e ll " f^, rn , L au
these forbidden tactics is strongly illustrated by an
account received of the recent Rans-Roubaix road race.
Single bicycle pacemakers were permitted, and the race)S
were allowed to change their mounts as often as they
pleased. The consequence w'as that the man with the
deepest pocket won ; he arranged for. a light machine,
with special hill-climbing gears, to await him at the foot
of every pinch, and had relays of single pacers along the
road These, although they were probably not in the
same street with him for the journey, could take him
along at a merry pace for a few miles, and their nuuibeis
did the trick. As a matter of fact, the best man did not
win for the winner beat by only half a wheel a man who
could not afford hill-climbing gears.
The latest idiocy is an undertaking by an Eng-
lish cyclist named C. C. de Finney to ride 100 miles per
day for 365 consecutive days on a motor-bike. Ine last
person who attempted a similar long and dreary enter-
prise was Teddy Hall, who seven years ago rode an ordi-
nary bike 100 miles per day, six days per week for 12
months, and covered 32,479 miles. This sort of thing
strikes one as the bedrock of inane futility.

The old bucks who frequent the golfing links
are agitating for girl caddies and the brazen portion of
the daily papers is echoing the demand. No club that
intends to retain the membership of married men will
dream of allowing the innovation. Up to the present tne
w7t.u. Social Gimlet Brigade, and the Ebenezer
Society for the Elevation of the Home and Safeguarding
of the Marriage Tie have not recovered their breath after
hearing of the scandalous proposal, but when they do
i i i the Bulletin also raises its voice in oppo-
sition, not because it fears that the system would lead to
the downfall of the home, but on the ground that a man
who would let a girl carry his golfing sticks would allow
his wife to clean his boots, and the man who does that
isn’t a man—he’s hardly even a bloke, and he doesn t
amount to a cove. * *

Good tobacco can only be grown on virgin soil. This is the reason that most Havana
and Mexican cigars are now so poor in quality and lacking in aroma.
Artificial manures have been used to force the growth of these tobaccos, and this
entirely spoils the quality.
The tobacco from which the “MARSUMA” cigar is made is grown on virgin soil—
that is, land which has been covered for all known time with rank, wild undergrowth.
This is burnt, the land tilled, and tobacco seeds sown, and only in this manner can good
tobacco be obtained. Every few years the ground is allowed to return to this wild state
but m Cuba and Mexico this is not done, and consequently these tobaccos have
deteriorated year by year, and must now give place to EAST Indies tobacco, the finest
the world produces.
Quite different from any cigar offered before, the peculiarity of the East Indian
tobacco is that the dark leaves are equally mild as the light-colored ones.
Only ripe tobacco being used in the manufacture, “MARSUMA” cigars are always
a rich brown or dark brown color.
Being made from picked sweet-scented leaves of the young tobacco plant, the cigars
have a most fragrant, pleasing flavor, peculiarly their own.

“ Before this terrific demonstration of the power
of gold any private company might have quailed, the
hard-up raiiroads were cowed at once. The Trust bought
up their scrip, or their scrip-holders. It bought up the
majority of oil wells, and it came down with an iron hand
on distribution. People who still struggled against the
grinding monopoly were crushed ruthlessly, reduced to
destitution, their wives driven to kitchens, their children
to charitable institutions, and themselves to lunatio
asylums and premature graves. It is even said that men
who could not be crushed and wouldn’t be bought were
murdered. So the rebate went on, secretly in spite of the
law. The Trust had got practical control of the U.S.A.
oil supply. Fresh oil fields were being discovered, but
owing to the Trust’s control of the means of transport
they could be worked profitably only by the Trust.”
From “ On the Trail of the Trust,” now on sale at Tub
Bulletin office.

S. A. lacrosse men are getting to work early.
Sturt, the premier club, which single-handed beat Vic-
t >;ia and Westralia in turn last year, has already piled up
27 goals against decent opposition in one match. This
tops the 26 which S.A. once made against N.S.\V., but
falls short of the club’s own record of 29 in an inter-club
match. Sydney players never dream of putting up scores
like this ; the good players seem to be too evenly distri-
buted around the metropolis to make it possible. On
Saturday Marrickville fell on North Shore, and, contrary
to expectations, overwhelmed it; but, in comparison to
the S.A. precedents, it was an even game, the winners
scoring only nine to the losers’ six.
» * * *
On Saturday Sydney followers of the ancient
game of hurling (which is really the oldest game in its
present form) were treated to a splendid exhibition at
Moore Park by the City and Annandale Clubs. City,
having won the toss, decided to make first use of the
slope of the ground. But the slope didn’t avail City
much, for, from the puck off, the Annandale crowd swept
the leather down the field and promptly scored a point.
The Annandale game consisted of a series of good passing
charges, some of which were successful. Then City
levelled things up somewhat by scoring a goal
from a free, and the first half ended with Annandale
leading by seven to four. The second half found the
out-matched City playing up hill, and, strange to say, it
played better, scoring a couple of points in rapid succes-
sion, and then another, which made the scores equal.
Over anxiety on the part of the City full-back in his
defence gave the Annandales a free within a few
yards of the goal line, and they scored a goal off it.
The City put on its business aspect, and played up in a
manner that would have made success certain had it
been indulged in from the opening of the game. But the
Annandale defence was too solid, and, in spite of great
efforts, City was unable to score again before time ran
out, leaving the Annandale team boss by 13 to 11.
The Griffin-Murphy mill, at Sydney National
Sporting Olub last week, was a mere roughing match.
Murphy was outweighted, and Charles Griffin, who re-
fused to go on the scales, looked not only much heavier,
but also much stronger. From the start he made use of
his extra weight and strength. Murphy is as pretty a
side-stepper as is in the game, but he was dazed after
having somewhere about 10 stone odd of Maoriland
meat burled against him for three consecutive minutes,
and he never side-stepped worth a cent. Still, he
fought a plucky fight, and although over-matched
and crushed by the roughing tactics of the
Maorilander, the champion featherweight jabbed
and stabbed away, and groped through the shower
of blows in a vain endeavor to find the Maori-
land point. But the Maoriland blows fell like Canter-
bury rain, and the Sydneyite saw only a'sheet of falling
fists, most of which fell on him. And, as the Scriptures
say, “he marvelled greatly.” His common sense told
him it could not last long—even the whirling machine
he was up against must get tired in time; but, unfor-
tunately for him, the Maorilander kept it up for five
rounds, and the Sydney boxer was beaten and roughed to
the floor over and over agaip. He came up gamely, but
at the end of the fifth round, after he had been floored
three times, the referee declared Griffin winner.
* * * *
George Towns has been challenged by English
sculler Harding to defend his right to the title of Cham-
pion of England, and to the Sportsman Cup—distinctions
which have been won on two occasions by Towns, and
held for six years. As the race must take place on the
Thames or Tyne, it is extremely likely that Towns will
forsake the title, and leave Cold Country scullers to
scramble amongst themselves for it.
“Huffin-Guffin” : A day amongst horsey men
and their nags has convinced me that the plea of im-
proving the breed of horses as a reason for racing them is
utter rot. One never hears anything among these human
weeds about the quality of the animals. The only mat-
ters discussed by the “inner” crowd are whether any
particular horse is a “ trier,” whether the “ push ” run-
ning it means to “go,” whether the jockey and trainer
haven’t been “got at,” and the like. There al-
ways seems to be quite a crowd engaged in
running each horse, and the crowd's unanimous
decision about its collective nag is the information every-
body desires. If it is found that the “push” itself is
divided, there is trouble. The racehorses seem for the
most part poor, worried-looking creatures ; they look as
though they felt their positions, and were always
conscious-stricken about their dishonest lives. They
seem to only need a leader to start a rebellion and an
eternal swearing-off “stiff” practices. They are
rushed into a railway horse-box, jolted and jerked
in the train, rushed out of the train, rushed
on the course, and then bustled about generally. Then,
when they feel that it is the business of their lives to race,
they are as likely as not, or more so, held back by a
narrow skulled boy who has but one idea—“riding to
orders.” The towm order of horse engaged in the racing
business, the cab horse, is even more badly treated than
his superior. He is rushed to and from the course as
hard as he can tear, each additional trip squeezed in
meaning the more shekels for his owner. Bah! the whole
business of horse-racing is a fraud and a cruelty to
animals. I wish I could draw, I w’ould steal Fred
Brown’s idea of the place w'here things are levelled up,
and would give the whole range of horse-frauds particular
Sheol.
From tlie Bent country
Vic. Premier Bent sa.vs he is going to sell the lands of the
Ivew Asylum, irrespective of the fact that those very
lands form the security of a special loan raised by Duncan
Oillies some 18 years ago. Even apart from Duncan’s
loan, the suggestion is absolute madness. The land
should be leased and form a perpetual endowment for the
State Lunacy Dept. It is strange how the Australian Fat-
man slavishly follows every British precedent which suits
his own purposes, and denounces every sensible English
practice as a “modern Socialistic fad.” Nearly all
the big charitable institutions of Great Britain
have behind them the solid foundation of a land endow-
ment settled on them ages ago by the foresight of
mediaeval founders. Hardly any of the great colleges of
England would exist to-day but for the revenues ob-
tained from land given when it was worth a fraction of
its present value. St. Bartholomew’s Hospital is a notable
example. This immense institution, one of the five largest
of the sort in the world, is maintained almost wholly
by its rent-roll and, not long back, was refusing contri-
butions and legacies on the ground that it had no need
for them. The Vic. Education Department has a vast
area of land along Fishermen’s Bend which should
become the centre of a great manufacturing district
when the Commonwealth provides a decent Protective
tariff ; and not long ago T. Bent suggested that this area
should be sold at its present value, Which is about
nothing. The force of public opinion was dead against
him then, and he dropped the string of his little kite.
But he seems determined to go through with the Kew
scheme, which is just about as bad.
BOODLE FIRST.
“Unfortunately good sound mercantile men say that
business is too important to neglect for politics.”— ARGUS.
“A much better yame on! Ah—well! — yes,
thanks! ”
About the end of April last 26 blocks of
land situated in the Tweed district (N.S.W.) were
thrown open for “ conditional purchase lease.”
For these blocks there were 108 applications.
The local Land Board sat at Mur willmnbah for
two days for the preliminary examination of
the applicants. On the second day the Board
announced that, owing to the land being thickly
timbered, and consequently difficult to clear, it
intended to take Capital into consideration in
coming to a decision. On the following day the
Board, wallowing at the shrine of Capital, reduced
the number of applications to GO—eight appli-
cants had withdrawn in disgust, and the other 40
had been refused a chance at the ballot owing to
insufficiency of bloated affluence. Poverty,
although not a crime, is apparently something
nearly as bad in the eyes of a Land Board.
Goldsbrougli, Mort and Co., Limited, report.
The week’s business in wool has been practically confined
to private selling which, however has not been of an
extensive nature owing to the limited supplies now
coming forward. Notwithstanding the easier tone that
marked the closing of the last series of sales in London,
the market here continues firm, and any lots available
are readily taken at rates fully equivalent to those ruling
at the auctions last week.*
*’The purpose of the Trust system is ever the
same. Monopolise the trade and production of a city, of
a State, of a continent, of the whole earth. Only when
you’ve got the whole earth in the clutch of your monopoly
can you bleed with thoroughness and in safety. To
bleed is the purpose of a Trust. It is not in business
for philanthropy’s sake. It does not weave its webs to
amuse the flies. It is after blood and spoil.” From “On
the Trial of the Trust,” now on sale at The Bulletin
office.
There is lying in Hobson’s Bay just now
the only six-masted brigantine afloat; it has just
brought hither two million feet of Oregon timber.
When first built it was rigged by its German
owners as a barque, but rolled its masts out. The
underwriters traded it off to a Yankee firm, and
the Yankees have made a success of it so far. It
is a queer-looking object, with its foremast square-
rigged and the other five schooner-rigged, but it is
easily handled, and travels all right. Its name is
the Everett G. Griggs. The first thing the
American does when he buys a ship is to change
its name to something with a naked initial in the
centre.
A letter published by Dr. Arthur, M.L.A.,
throws sortie doubt upon the latest tale ol
Stinking Fish and Co. The tale concerned
one Colchester, who sold his business in Eng-
land ; took up, through hfie Agent General,
a farm at Myall Creek (N.S.W.), and came
out to go on the land. Then in some un-
accountable way he found himself deceived, or
betrayed, orsomething, and went back to England,
where it is understood that he is likely to say
things injurious to this unfortunate country. The
nature of Colchester’s injury was not at all
clear, but Stinking Fish and Co. decided
off-hand that Australia had been at it
again. Dr. Arthur, however, says Colchester was
a man with only £7OO ; that he selected on his
own responsibility an area remote from the rail-
way (this despite the fact that he had a
map before him); that he intended to
be a large sheep-farmer on a mere dairy
farm, and didn’t realise till he arrived that it was
impossible ; that he was offered a more convenient
farm and urged to wade in at the highly-profit-
able dairying industry, but decided that his
sons were too delicate to wrestle with the coarse,
uncouth cow ; and finally this gentleman took
his futile way home again and was seen no more.
Which story leaves one wondering what harm
Australia did the aggrieved Colchester. It
couldn’t help his sons being delicate.
Take a little Wolfe’s Schnapps morning
and night to keep the stomach, bowels and kidneys
in good order. And it does the business .*
A start is about to be made with the
erection of the Maoriland Government’s first set
of workmen’s homes, tenders having been let for
25 at Petone, near Wellington. Houses are abso-
lutely unprocurable in that city, and rents are
outrageously high. People of the houseless sort
are glad to get accommodation of any kind at all;
so long as they are under the shelter of a roof they
don’t care. A woman who applied to the Benevo-
lent Trustees for aid said she rented a bathroom
and cooked food for herself and child on an oil
lamp. Wellington, with a population that is
growing like a high wind, is jammed in between
the sea and the mountains, and the inhabitants
cut shelves in the mountains where they are not
too steep, and get up home by zig-zag paths, and
spend the evenings looking down their neighbors’
chimneys.
The wonderful anti-Socialists of a Victorian
shire up Kyabram way have been begging again.
They wanted to make a new road, and
making it involved the purchase of
24 acres of land. The owners demanded
£4OO (£IG 13s. 4d. per acre) which the men-
dicants said was a fair thing. They also
said, however, that the local rates couldn’t
provide the money, and came cadging
to Bent. Then Bent said in effect:
“ You say land in your district is worth
£l6 13s. 4d. per acre. Yet you only value
it for rating purposes at an average
of £4 7s. 4d. per acre, and levy on that a
miserable Is. in the £ of rental value,
which seems to be about 3gd. on the
real rental value. If you abolished
this swindle, and valued your lands
honestly, you would have nearly four
times the revenue, even with your pre-
sent low rate, and you could make your
own roads. Good morning. Mind the
step.” And another barefaced swindle
found itself out in the street.
From Clifford, Love and Co. The Bulletin
has received some samples of their corn-flour—a
local product, manufactured on one of the delight-
ful Lane Cove River reaches. The flour comes
from the “ Chicago Mill,” but this Chicago mill
is in Australia, and the'pwrity and quality of its
products are unchallenged.
Walter Duncan, who died at sea the other
day, and was buried under the waves close to the
spot where Charlie Kingston’s father’s body lies,
was about the wealthiest man in S.A. Assembly,
and one of the most popular. His vote was
always solidly Conservative, but he was a really
good fellow and always a gentleman. Although a
big pastoralist, he was a strong supporter of the
Shearers’ Accommodation Act. He never wob-
bled, and never sought office, and the longest
speech he ever made in the House did not exceed ten
minutes. He was alwaj's at the head of the poll
in his district, but that was a personal matter
and Murray will now probably be captured by the
Government.
REFORM WITHOUT REVOLUTION.—The great
and particular merit of Hunyadi Janos is that it
relieves without discomfort; in other words, it effects
reform without revolution. This is because it is the
bust and safest natural aperient.*
Like Coghlan, who might have secured the
job had he wanted it, G. H. Knibbs, the new
Federal Statist, was born in Sydney. He is 48 ;
Coghlan is 51. As a youngster Coghlan went
into the Works Department; Knibbs joined the
Lands. When Knibbs was acting-Professor of
Physics at Sydney University, the under-grads,
used to marvel at the things he knew—all sorts of
things. Privately he would tell them some of
those things in five minutes. Then he would tell
them the same things in a lecture two hours long,
and when he had finished, they would try to
grope their way back out of the wilderness of
words to the real solid information they had got
in the five minutes.

On the Trail of the Trust
Price 1/3
POST FREE.
The anti-Socialistic Gippsland farmers
give the public something fresh to gasp about
every day. Allan M'Lean’s request for abso-
lutely free carriage of beet to Maffra (Vic.)
beet sugar factory was not consented to, but a
liberal rate of payment for beet and an almost
invisible freight-rate were fixed which (even the
farmers admit) will make it possible “to grow
beet profitably within a radius of 50 miles,” and
it was promised that all the pulp (which is suit-
able for cow-feeding) would be returned free of
cost and freight. Still the private-enterprising
farmer is not quite satisfied. Those who live
closest to the Maffra factory point out now that
they are “not properly equipped” with horses
and carts to drag their beet in, and they ask for
Government horses and carts to do it for them.
This staggering proposition looks like what U.S.
people call “ the limit.”

UNLOCKING THE LANDS.
The Midland Railway Company of Western Australia
LIMITED
are now subdividing their concession of
2,500,000 Acres,
Comprising some of the finest AGRICULTURAL and GRAZING COUNTRY in the State of
Western Australia, into FARMS and GRAZING PROPERTIES.
THE FIRST SALE of
ONE HUNDRED SPLENDID FARMS, and
MOORA and KOOGAN TOWN SITES
Will be held by Public Auction at the QUEEN’S HALL,
PERTH, WESTERN AUSTRALIA, on
FRIDAY, JUNE 22nd, at 2.30 P .m.,
BY
JAMES GARDINER, Auctioneer.
THESE FARMS are situated from 96 to 108 Miles from PERTH, the Capital City of Western
Australia, fronting and served by the Company’s own railway. None of them are more than
4 miles from railway station or sidings—with a very convenient train service.
RICH AGRICULTURAL and GRAZING LANDS. 19 to 22in. RAINFALL.
Good Roads. RED FRIABLE SOIL, Easily Tilled. GREAT FATTENING COUNTRY.
The Concession is situated between the METROPOLIS and the GREAT MURCHISON
GOLDFIELDS.
Western Australia imported last year £1,500,000 worth of Land Products.
FARMERS—COME AND SEE ! and let the Land speak for itself.
AVERAGE YIELDS for Country separated from this by only the Railway Line, for 1905-6
WHEAT, 20 Bushels; HAY, 30| Cwt.
WATER easily obtained and conserved. NO HEAVY CLEARING.
UPSET PRICES fixed from 6s to 40s per acre. ALL UPSETS marked on the Plan.
The whole of the Blocks will be offered by Public Auction, and sold to the highest bidder at or
above the Upset Price fixed.
TERMS —Ten per cent. Cash Deposit; Balance by 15 equal Annual Payments, bearing
Interest at 4 per cent. The balance of the purchase-money can be paid off at any time, and a rebate
of interest will be allowed at the rate charged ; or purchaser may pay Cash.
On payment of the whole of the purchase-money the TITLE is guaranteed by the Western
Australian Government. POSSESSION given the day after the Sale.
If you intend inspecting, send a wire either to the office of the Company or to the Auctioneers,
stating by what steamer you are coming, and you will be met, and every facility will be given
for inspection.
If you have not received an Illustrated Pamphlet, giving the fullest particulars, send a Post
Card addressed to GORDON AND GOTCH, SYDNEY, and one will be sent you by return of
post, free of charge.
JAMES GARDINER, Auctioneer,
Howard Street, PERTH, W.A.

293 St. George’s Terrace, PERTH, W.A.

This is a cold, cold world. Not long ago
a South Coast (N.S.W.) man sent a bailiff a few
miles out of the township to take possession of a
debtor’s house for a milk bill. When the bailiff,
having faithfully fulfilled his duty, returned home
he found another bailiff in possession of his own
house, put in by the same man for another milk
bill.
The luck of drunks held good to an amaz-
ing extent at Port Melbourne the other Sunday
afternoon. The drunk and a mate, feeling joyful,
hired a boat and set out to catch whales. They
had got as far as the pier-end, when the least sober
whaler, disputing as to the correct way to navi-
gate the craft, tumbled overboard. The pier-end
was crowded with idlers watching some sizeable
sharks nosing around, and they immediately and
strenuously urged the drowning man to get on
board again. They also threw him the handiest
implements available. One eager rescuer tossed
him an oar, which landed on his head and nearly
slew him. Some one else threw him a lot of iron,
and told him to hold on to it. Ultimately his
mate hauled him aboard again, and the pair
gradually sculled shorewards, and capsized within
about 20 yards of the beach. Fished out again,
they meandered towards an hotel to obtain medi-
cal comforts, turning at intervals to ask, with a
dismantled expression, what all the fuss was
about.

People who had given evidence in various
cases at Melbourne City Court one recent morning
subsequently rushed to the nearest chemist and
disinfected themselves. A witness in the last
case of the day, after being sworn and kissing the
dirty promiscuous book, spoke so inarticulately
that the magistrate inquired why. He was
suffering from cancer of the tongue, in so malig-
nant a form that the member was almost useless !
Then, for the first time, the other people realised
the vicissitudes of that book—the diseases that
are breathed on it, blown on it, rubbed against it,
pawed on to it—and they wanted to die.
Just now when the chrysanthemum men of
the Monkey brand are getting unlimited credit for
virtues they mostly don’t possess, there is a chance
for the Japomaniacs to score a point by denoun-
cing the national spirit of vandalism, which Aus-
tralia has in common with all English-speaking
countries. Melbourne Aon reported lately
that “ complaints of wilful damage to plants
in the public gardens in the city have been
of frequent occurrence during the past few months.
In many instances the damage has been the work
of school children. Recently about 20 boys were
caught breaking valuable plants in the Fitzroy
Gardens.” An infusion of Japanese public senti-
ment into Australian State-school education would
cure this complaint, anyhow. The youthful Jap is
taught to regard a garden vandal as an enemy of
the people and a fool to himself. The most essen-
tially immoral Monkey in the visiting squadron
might be safely trusted to refrain from destroying
a pretty foliage plant for destruction’s sake, and
not to chip the nose off a plaster image.
“ Wanderer ” writes :
The American’s genius for advertising is marvellous.
His latest outbreak is in the shape of a gorgeous cable-
gram about the proposal to construct a “ boulevard ” 3300
miles long—“ a sort of Appian Way from the Pacific to
the Atlantic.” Now, first of all, it may be safely assumed
that there is no intention of forming any Appian Way,
Appius Claudius, being a bull-headed man with abund-
ance of cheap labor, built his road of carefully-fitted
stone blocks resting on a stupendous foundation ; he
hewed his dogged way through mountains with cheap
labor, and filled up valleys because he didn’t know how
to build bridges, and filled in swamps by sheer quantity
and brute force. He didn’t live to finish his work ; in
fact it took 280 years to complete the whole 350 miles of
it —rather over a mile a year on an average. The
U.S. people simply propose to make a boule-
vard, and a boulevard, in this connection, is just a
plain, ordinary, every-day road. And the reason why
they are going- to make so much road all of a sudden is
that the U.S. is a good 50 years behind the times with its
roads, and has to catch up arrears. In some of the
Western States there are practically no roads, and over
a great portion of the old settled country a huge propor-
tion of the highways are just plain dirt or mud, and have
never heard of road metal. In England or France or
Germany the proposed highway would have been built
long ago as a matter of course, and it wouldn’t have been
called a boulevard or an Appian Way, and no one would
have dreamed of cablegram mi ng about it. It would just
have been built (as the road that runs from Western Vic-
toria, through Melbourne, Sydney, and Brisbane, into
Central Queensland, was built), and probably built a great
deal better than the Americans will do it, for Jonathan’s
economy in road metal amounts to a disease.


A GOLD MINER’S PLAIN STORY.
A Victim of Indigestion, and Mother Seigel’s
Syrup was Worth more than Gold to Him.
W. 0. Cousins, of King-street, Bendigo, Vic-
toria, is a gold miner by occupation, and is widely
known in that city, where he was born, perhaps
30 years ago. _ Some years ago he was a great
sufferer from indigestion, and as a result of it
became so ill that he was unable to give attention
to his business. On the advice of his friend, Mr.
W. Hunt, the manager of Springvale, a sheep
station between Melbourne and Ballarat, Mr.
Cousins used Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and was
cured, as he tells in a letter, dated August 9th,
1905, as follows:
“For more than three years my health was a
constant source of anxiety to me. I was costive,
bilious, unable to digest food, and troubled with
headaches and racking pains all over the body.
My complaints were sufficient to render half-a-
dogen men miserable. Such Was my state in 1894,
When my friend, Mr. W. Hunt, manager of Spring*
vale Station, advised a course of Mother Seigel’s

medicine, which had been of untold benefit to him
Upon several occasions, and said that he was sure

statements soott, appeared. The constipation from
iyrlieh I had so long suffered was brokett up, and
the biliousness, headaches, and othet digestive
troubles began to abate. By the time I had taken
Six bottles pf the syrup, all Of these symptoms had
left me, and I had become quite .a different man.
“ My health hits rem lined all I coif’d wish it to
be; a b!< .sing for which I am wholly indebted to
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup. But for its
timely aid, I bplievb my constitution would have
been ruined. It will always bb a pleasure to me
to make known the sterling merit of Mother

LIFE.
(I3y “Viva.”)
No living things from man to a microbe, can exist unless it is continuously dying. It is not merely
necessary for man to absorb nourishment in order to live j it is equally necessary that, simultaneously
sotnfe portion of his body, bone, blood, or tissue, must waste away and die. It is essential, also, that
a balance be preserved in the two processes of replenishing the body and the removal of the waste or
dead material which js constantly accumulating within it. An uneven action of either means disease
A total suspension of either means deatln
Of course everyone knows that an adequate supply of suitable food, pure water, and fresh air is
requisite, to build up the body, but, unfortunately, most people are not as well aware of the compli-
cated action of the mechanism with which nature has endowed man for dealing with the used up
dead and waste material always being produced within us, and which, unless regularly extracted from
the blood and expelled from the body, must cause disease or death.
Upon the skin, lungs, kidneys and liver rests the main responsibility of retnovitig waste matter
from the system -The skin usually requires little attention beyond regular washing with good soap
and water, and it will m the form of perspiration, rid the body of a quantity of dead matter. A fit
o coughing or a choking sensation will speedily call our attention to anything which hinders the
lungs in their work of removing, in the form of carbonic acid, an amount of used-up material equiva-
lent to eight ounces of pure charcoal every day. The kidneys and liver, on the other hand, are
delicate organs, and it is often not until we begin to suffer from some serious complaint that we
become aware that their action is impeded. Rheumatism, gout, lumbago, neuralgia, backache
sciatica blood disorders, indigestion, biliousness, jaundice, sick headache, general debility, gravel
stone, bladder troubles depression, low temperature, anaemia, bright’s disease, are all caused by
disease, or indifferent action of the kidneys or liver, or both of those organs, permitting the body to
retain urinary and biliary waste poisons which it is their function to eliminate from the blood and
expel in a natural manner.
It will therefore be seen how important it is that the vigorous health and activity of the kidneys
and liver should be maintained. A person suffering from one or more of the many complaints men-
tioned should not regard them as diseases, but should adopt the rational course of attacking them at
their source, by restoring a proper and regular action of the kidneys and liver, when, the cause of the
disorders having been rectified, the waste matter which produces the trouble is removed and health
ensues as a matter of course.
It was the comprehension of this fact which resulted in the discovery of Warner’s Safe Cure
Certain medical men-being aware of the difficulty of treating the disorders referred to, and knowing
that they w_ere due to inefficient action of the kidneys or the liver, but being unable in most cases to
accurately diagnose just which organ was at fault—set themselves a task of compounding a medicine
which would act specifically upon the kidneys and liver alike. After long research they succeeded
and introduced the medicine to the world under the name of Warner’s Safe Cure. This was about thirty
years ago, and since then many millions of bottles have been sold, and millions of people have been
saved from pam, suffering and death by taking a course of this invaluable specific for all kidney and
liver diseases and disorders arising therefrom. [gj

CLAUDE JRWRLL.
London Family Herald lias reached its 32S8th number
—in other words, it has more than completed its 63rd
year. A British reviewer says of it: “It is exactly the
same paper as it was when it and the London Journal
were the only sources from which our village derived its
knowledge of how earls looked in their evening clothes
and governesses wormed their way into the
squire’s heart.” *
<«****
“I have often wondered,”says a writer in the Satur-
day Review, “why a town hall can never be built, but
must always be ‘ erected ’; why a church must always be
, a sacred edifice ’; why you cannot turn a corner without
, negotiating ’ it ; why you cannot play a solo without
, rendering ’ it; why you cannot go to a wedding without
attending it; why you cannot shoot a goal in football
without placing the sphere in the rigging *; why you
cannot be married to any but a ‘ charming bride’; and
why you cannot die without your death 1 taking place.’ ”
Also, why should a gathering be “ very representative,”
and an occasion “ most unique ”? Above all, why should
the parson be the only man who says “ vouchsafe,” and
why should you “ wend your way ” when vou never wend
anything else ?
* * * *
David Syme has no use for poetry of any kind, and verse
is conspicuous by its absence in all the Syme publica-
cations. “ Dawvid’s ” Scotchness is apparent in this trait;
he didna see the sense o’ cripplin’ reason for the sake o’
rhyme,” and when verse is mentioned he smiles in-
dulgently and shakes his head over it as a fond frivolity.
It is interesting, too, to hear from Australian black-and-
white artists who have had professional dealings with
David They groan despairingly when he is mentioned,
and tell you. that the Great Scot’s idea of a drawing is
something with all the crudities of photography and none
of the graces of art.

A newspaper, typewritten from end to end, is now
published in the vicinity of the Victoria Falls, in Central
Africa. It is called the Livingstone Pioneer.
* * * *
“ Pen ” : The Melbourne Argus is sorely in need of a
little new blood. The editorial staff has for long shown
signs of staleness with the weight of years and overwork,
the result being leading articles of the merest platitude!
baldly expressed. But then the Argus is run on economi-
cal lines, and the newspaper business is one in which
economy of that sort doesn’t pay. Everyone knows the
story of the London newspaper proprietor who attempted
to drive out his partner by increasing expenses. The op-
posite effect was brought about, the paper speedily
doubling its circulation and advts.
* * *
“ Hiphen ”: For some time past I have been making a
deep study of the various phases of our poets. I find that
in the spring we have the Girl poets to the front. Under
this heading the “ Her soft blue Eyes ” variety, the “ Her
rounded Limbs” species, and the “Her snow-white
Breast ” class are most prominent. As the year speeds on
into summer, and the green gives place to the grey, the
Beer poet spills his ink like water. In the “ Beer ” class
is the “ Wine, Women and Song ” scribe and the “ Nights
we Squandered ” bard. November, of course, is the time
when our great “ Horse ” poet lays in his stock of
stationery, and burns the midnight oil at both ends, and
then the Biff bard, who tells “ How Mickey Bourke Won
the Scrap ” also emerges from his winter obscurity. With
Winter comes the Melancholia minstrel with the cold on
his chest, which he takes for a pain at the heart, and so
hands out his regrets for the “ Past that is Gone.” Also,
he has a few words to say about the girl who was untrue’
and about his mate, “ Poor Drifted Dan.” The Political
poets have no set season. They’ve always something to
say. And I’m not sure that we have any Dog poets, though
I have recently seen one or two Cow poems in The
Bulletin.
* • » *
“ Falder ” : Am in training to take over the “ Dame
Durden ” column in the T. and C. Journal, or anywhere
else I can get an opening. This will be about my average
gait and performance :
“ Looney ville, April 1, 1906.
“Dear Dame Durden,—l hope you are quite ill. We
have two pet cats, one dog, and a poodle. The cats’
names are Moses and Jehoshaphat. When not stealing
about home they are raiding the neighbors. We keep a
boy called Joe. Joe says he has to work too hard here.
Father says Joe wants oil—plenty of whip-oil well rubbed
in to make him active. Joe hates colored people. He
always calls any of them ‘ those blinded Tndoos.’’ One
sold him a mouth-organ last month. Joe played on it
for three days. Then cook told him the Hindoo had been
taken up for leprosy. Joe buried the mouth-organ at
once. He spends all his spare time now washing his
mouth out. I have two pet foals. One of them kicks at
you with both hind feet; the other with only one. When
the,} grow up the}' may bite as well. I have a pet galah
The cook gave it to me. It swore dreadfully at first, but
now I have taught it to sing hymns in between. We have
some nice drives into town sometimes, as father often
gets summonsed for not paying his shearers. This is all
I have to say for the present, so good-bye.
“ Yours dearly,
“ Ella Googoo (aged 1J).”

Once again it is chronicled that a military-
spy, acting for a foreign Power (not specified), is
perambulating Maoriland examining its defences,
At latest he Was consuming whisky and haggis at
Dunedin, where a small-arms magazine is to be
erected at St. Clair. But a start hasn’t been
made yet, and, presumably, won’t be until the
alleged spy has gone. Maoriland has no defences
worth speaking of, and what it does possess in the
Way of shooting irons is known to every race in
the world, bar, perhaps, the Hottentots or the
Laplanders, and the like. The spy business is
Ponderous joke so far as Maoriland is con-
cerned, but the conceited Pig Islanders take the
yarn quite seriously,

sydney rag shops. The law allows 7s. fid. per

Model 1 90 S Self-Loading Rifle
This rifle is a six shot, hammerless take-down, made in .32 and .35 calibers,
the cartridges being of the modern smokeless powder type. The recoil of
the exploded cartridge ejects the empty shell, cocks the hammer and feeds
a fresh cartridge from the magazine into the chamber, leaving the rifle ready
to shoot upon the operator’s pulling the trigger. The self-loading system
adds much to the pleasure of rifle shooting, either at target or game. The
list price of this model is $28.00.
These Rifles For Sale By All Dealers .

Typhoid’s After Effects
A Relapse and a Radical Cure.
The Case of Mrs. T. Vaughan.
(BY A LOCAL REPORTER.)
Probably many who have hitherto played the
onlooker’s part have been sharply taught by ex-
perience that the trials of life possess an arm of
far-reaching propensities, and in such instances,
where a personal interest has been aroused, much
pleasure will be found in reading the conversation
which took place between our representative and
Mrs. Teresa Vaughan, of No. 39 Elizabeth-street,
Camperdown, who said :
“ The first sickness I ever had in my life came
to me six years ago, and it was a very severe ill-
ness, too.”
“ What was it?” asked the writer.
“It was an attack of typhoid that had
laid me low, and I had taken a turn for the
better, and was getting on fine, when I
made the mistake of leaving my bed too
soon. A relapse was the consequence, and,
of course, I had to take to my bed again. I
believe I was worse then than before, for the pains
which came to my head were frightful. Those in
the house told me that often I was quite delirious,
so that will give you an idea of how bad I was.
Well, I had been losing and losing flesh till at
last there was no more to waste away, nothing
being left of me but a portion of my former self.
Everybody who saw me thought that my case was
hopeless, and I’m sure I thought so, too, but the
old saying that ‘ while there is life there is hope ’
proved to be a true one in my case. It was when
I was so ill that I couldn’t possibly have been
worse—scarcely able to move myself, in fact, that
a friend of mine, a lady about seventy, told me
what a grand thing Clements’ Tonic was, and
that it would be sinful not to give it a trial.
Well, what could I say ? Nothing had done
me any good so far, so could I expect
this one remaining remedy to benefit me ? I
asked myself. My friend’s persistent ‘ Give it a
trial ’ answered the question for me, so I sent for
some Clements’ Tonic that day. I don’t suppose
you’ll believe this, but it is perfectly true—the
very day I first took Clements’ Tonic the retching
stopped, and if that didn’t make me joyful nothing
would. Oh ! I was so thankful, for I knew then
that so long as I persevered with Clements’ Tonic
there was still a hope for me left. My friends
were almost spellbound with amazement, and
when I told them that Clements’ Tonic had also
created a feeling of hunger within me they could
hardly believe their ears, they said. But I had
still more to boast about, for this latest medicine
of mine had brought me such general relief from
pain, that it was as much as I could do to
realise that I was the same woman who so
recently had endured such a great amount of
torture. My chest had been very badly affected
with the weightiness that was almost unbearable,
but in a week or two I was quite free from it.”
“When you had taken some solid food what
were your feelings like ? ”
“Well, at first I did not feel too comfortable,
but after a while, and by taking Clements’ Tonic
regularly, the overburned sensations passed away,
and eventually my stomach became so strong that
I could oat anything, and never had the least
unpleasantness to contend with afterwards.
Why, the agony I used to endure from windy
spasms was something terrible, but Clements’
lonic eradicated them for good. Several
times a day, too, I was subject to fits
of uncontrollable giddiness—the room seemed
to be turning round and round, and then
everything would go dark for a space ;
but even to this trouble Clements’ Tonic proved to
me my staunch and only friend. My eyes used to
feel much larger than they should have been, so
heavy, and so defective in sight, too—and you
only wanted to catch a glimpse of the whiteness of
my tongue to understand wnat a disordered state
my internal organs were in. And what a terrible
taste I had in my mouth ! As though I had been
drinking gall, even after I had rinsed it out with
vinegar and water, which I often did. But there,
what a small matter Clements’ Tonic made of these
things, besides which it soothed me so well that I
soon got a sound rest every night without any of
the fearful dreams that used to terrorise me.
There is no doubt when I first used Clements’
Tonic I was as near the portals of death as I could
be. I earnestly hope this account of my recovery
will come under the notice of delicate women, so
please publish this in any way you may think fit.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION. •
I, Teresa Vaughan, of No. 39 Elizabeth-street,
Camperdown, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have read the annexed
document, and that it contains and is a true and
faithful account of my illness and cure by
Clements’ Tonic, and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my state men ts—
which I give voluntarily, without receiving any
payment—and I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true and
by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) TERESA VAUGHAN.
Subscribed and declared at Camperdown this
fifth day of September, one thousand nine hundred
and four, before me,
C. S. MALLETT. J.P.

ECZEMA, is a disease of the epidermis or outer layer of the skin, which
does not mature, and which allows the watery portion of the blood to
exude. Scabs or incrustations form on the surface, and these, on being
removed, expose a red, angry, and extremely irritable, itching surface.
Sufferers from this terrible disease have to contend with constant, wearying
irritation, days of weakening pain, and nights of sleeplessness. Wonderfully
soothing and efficacious is the treatment of Eczema with Zam r ßuk, which is best
applied on a fresh piece of lint, night
and morning. The burning, itching
pain will be allayed, and with each
fresh application of Zam-Buk another
step towards complete recovery has
been made. The skin which was
once so full of disease will eventually
present a soft and wonderfully smooth
surface.
“I have suffered for years with
Eczema on my hands,” says Mrs. L.
Walker, c/o Mrs. J. Lane, of 116
Cobden-street, South Melbourne.
“ Being in service, this complaint
handicapped me greatly. I tried
all sorts of so-called remedies for
Eczema, but nothing I tried seemed
to give me any relief. On the con-
trary, the complaint appeared aggra-
vated, and was especially severe as , ,
the Spring season of the year opened, when the complaint would spread, and
cover my hands completely. Seeing advertised that Zam-Buk had proved suc-
cessful in curing bad cases of Eczema, I purchased a pot, and applied the Balm
as directed. The results of this treatment were a revelation. After
five applications the Eczema disappeared, and has not since returned. That
was five months ago, and at the present time my hands are as smooth and
healthy-looking as one could wish, for which I am indeed grateful.”
