RUB IT m r
The City Where Work was
Slack.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS is the story the white man told to the
Children of Black and 1 an ;
In the days when his heart was hard and
old he spoke to the Piebald Clan,
In the days when the white man bowed his head,
long after the first great war,
Long after his bubbled pride was dead and his
nation ruled no more !
“The land smiled back to her laughing lords, and
the soil was soft and wet,
And the big barns bulged with the golden hoards
where the grain and the rafters met,
And the laden boughs of the fruit trees fell to the
kiss of the rich brown loam,
And the rivers sang to the sea to tell of the joy in
the farmer’s home.
“ The fat flocks fed on the grassy plains, and never
a sheep that parched ;
The streams ran high with the blessed rains
where the wandering cattle marched ;
And the white wool won by a white man s son
the white-world factories fed,
And our hearts grew bold as we gleaned the gold,
and the man and the white maid wed.
“Long over the land the warm white stars of pros-
perity shone serene,
And the sunlight smiled on her many scars and
nourished the fields of green.
And proud with the pride of a well-filled purse
were the big brave men outback,
Nor recked they the weight of the deadly curse
of the city where work was slack.
“ They came to the city where work was slack ;
they gaped on her gilded domes.
‘Of the wealth we’ve won we would barter back
for gear to adorn our homes.
Ours is to shepherd our sheep, to weed, and
winnow where we were born ;
Yours is to weld and weave for our needs, and
sell us your wares for corn.’
“They laughed as they propped up the greasy
posts and sucked at their cigarettes.
(Oh the vacant minds of the loafing hosts in the
cities of beer and bets !
The wonderful wit of the city brat; the sneer of
the man who knows
The proper shape of a Bourke-street hat or the
cut of a spieler’s clothes !)
“ They sniggered and drawled, ‘ We have left off
toil, for little there is to do.
Go look on the wharves ; there the big cranes
moil, unloading the ships for you.
In the big blind stores the white girls wait on the
nod of Calico Jim.
The importer man in the land is great; you may
buy what you like off him.’
“ They turned from the city where work was slack
—from the leer of her fair, false face,
And her wantoned wealth ; they took the track
to the sun-sweet open space ;
But they spent their gold on some foreign fake,
at the word of the city fools —
Some foreign trash that would wear and break,
like a youngster’s kit of tools.
“ And away in the city of Don’t-Know-liow, in the
mansions of Let-It-Slide,
Her people lived with unfurrowed brow, and
laughed in their empty pride.
And they spent their strength in the fields of
sport —in the chase of a puffed-up ball.
They weaved no web, and they built no fort, nor
raised they the sheltering wall.
“ There came a time when the Red King woke,
and stalked in his wrath abroad ;
The pent-up hate of the centuries broke to the
fury of flame and sword.
Your fathers looked on a world aghast, and the
crumbling kingdoms swayed ;
And we knew that Shem was awake at last, and
Australia saw and prayed.
“We prayed the prayer that the sinner prays at
the hour of his parting breath ;
(Man seldom whines o’er his wasted days till he
stands at the door of death).
For your fathers came in their armored hells—
‘ England, we look to thee ! ’
And she sent us guns, and she sent us shells, but
the ships went down at sea !
“The ships went down in a reddened sea, out-
numbered and undermanned.
No forts, nor guns, nor shells had we, and the foes
were on every hand.
Our horsemen were but a broken mass —a tumult
with pike and sword,
And onr unarmed ranks dissolved like glass in the
furnace of the Lord.”
This’ is the tale the white man said as he stalked
through the Hall of Dreams
On a day when the piebald children played by the
side of the haunted streams.
And the flag that flew from our gilded domes, and
our people of brown and black
Told the fate of the men who had their homes in
the city where work was slack.
TAS.
C. LOUIS RANDALL.
By Rail and Semaphore.
THE LEPER.
[For The Bulletih.]
WHEN Jones arrived at Romahapa
Traffic Office one Wednesday morning
he found Traffic Clerk Flu ter complain-
ing of incipient grippe. In those days the grippe
was a serious matter, and had to be treated
promptly to ward off a serious illness. The place
reeked of eucalyptus, and another odor which the
overwhelming stench of bluegum could not dis-
guise proclaimed that the Traffic Clerk had been
trying the rum remedy.
“Jones, old man, I think I’ll leave you to it.
God only knows where Mackenzie is, but if any-
thing serious should happen don’t hesitate to ring
me up, and if necessary I’ll come down in a cab.”
“ Right-oh ! You go home and sweat the blessed
thing out of you. Besides, you want a rest badly.
I’ll get on all right. Hey—Jimmy, get a cab for
Mr. Fluter.”
Five minutes later Mr. Fluter, who was peri-
lously close to being drunk on eucalyptus and
rum found himself in a closed-in carnage which
his subordinate had paid for. and being driven
rapidly home.
Jones watched the carriage till it disappeared
round the coiner of Burenda-street, and then
turned back to his own desk.
“ Guess I’m the bloke in charge of this machine.
One blessed day of power, I suppose. Anyhow,
I’ll know what it feels like to be a I rathe
Manager.”
And then he sat down and plunged into Jus
work, till Jimmy laid a telegram on his table
corner. Jones glanced at it. It was from Burning
Plains and ran :
Chinese leper hoarded No. 2 North at Uranium or
Havelock, and when requested to pay said he had no
money. Please wire instructions. P. Gill, S.M.
“ Wire instructions—good lord ! ” snorted Jones,
disgustedly. “Jimmy, call up Burning Plains
till I tell Gill what I think of him.”
When the telegraphist got Burning Plains
Jones opened out.
“ That vou, Mr. Gill ? ”
“ Yes, sir.”
“And you don’t know what to do with a man
who won’t pay his fare ? ”
“Not when lie’s a leper, sir.”
“Well, chuck him out. No good running a
sick man in.”
“Very well, sir. Will I instruct guard or
porters ? ”
“ The whole crowd if necessary. Chuck him
out yourself.”
Mr. Gill made no reply. Presently he came
back.
“Can’t get him out, sir. He is a leper, and
threatens to bite first man who lays hands on
him. He says one of his fingers has dropped off
in the carriage.”
“That finger’s a gag, Gill. He’s pulling your
leg. Sling a sheet over him, or a blanket, or
something, and rush in and gag him.”
“ Shall I purchase a rug for the purpose, sir ? ”
inquired Gill.
“ o'n, go to Hades ! ” said Jones, in a sudden
burst of wrath. He realised that he was up
against a dead wall of funk that was going to be
buttressed by every device that a mind well-
trained in red-tape could think of. Presently
another wire arrived :
Have sent No. 2on with leper on board. He wants to
go to Romahapa, and can be arrested there. P. Gill, S.M.
At Paisley the leper was hungry, and alighting
on the off-side of the train he got into the refresh-
ment-room while the guard was telling the Station-
master about him. He bought a lot of refresh-
ments, asked for a brandy-and-soda, and tendered
a pound-note. The proprietor of the refreshment-
room gave him the change himself, and as the
traveller pocketed the silver he leaned over and,
laying one finger on the note, said impressively :
“ Better you be careful—me leper ! ”
The landlord gave a gasp of horror and then
flung the note from him. The leper recovered
the note. When the landlord revived he rushed
out and howled for the solitary policeman.
“He can’t come,” said the S.M. “ I want him
to arrest a leper who is travelling without paying
his fare.”
“What! Another one?” shrieked the landlord.
At this moment the leper came out of the
refreshment-room, loaded with spoil, and ex-
tended a friendly hand to the S.M.
“You likee shake hands with me—me poor
leper, goem down Romahapa see doctor. Welly
ni’ man, him,” pointing to the landlord.
Next moment the invalid was standing alone,
and he quietly got into the first-class smoker
which he had affected all the way down. The
S.M. suddenly returned with the policeman.
“There he"is, sergeant. Take him in charge.
He’s a leper, and they are forbidden to travel on
the railways except in ambulance cars. Seize
him.”
“Be gob, Oi loike yer style. Oi’ll no seize him.
Devil fly away wid you. Would you have me
conthract the dreadful disase ? ”
“ Well, someone must seize him ; and it is
clearly the duty of the police, isn’t it ? ”
“ ’Tis not; ’tis the Health Authorities tha,t look
into such matters. How the devil can Oi take
him in charge ? Oi didn’t see him refuse to pay his
fare. ’Tis by summons ye must proceed, me
mahn!”
At this brilliant inspiration the policeman felt
quite relieved. The crestfallen S.M. declined to
have anything further to do with the leper, and
told the guard that time was up.
Hardly had the train started when the Traffic
Office called up and Jones inquired what they had
done with the leper. The S.M. denied having
done anything with him, and went off into a long
rigmarole about the cowardice of the police.
“You’re all cocktails!” yelled the wire from
Jones’ end. Then he cut the discussion short by
calling up Kilmarnock.
When Kil. was in attention Jones informed the
S.M. that there was a leper on board the train,
who had travelled close upon a hundred miles on
the strength of his disease. He expected that the
patient would leave the train at one of the subur-
ban stations, and ordered that he should be se-
curely locked in, and, if necessary, the windows
should be battened up to prevent his escape. The
S.M. acknowledged the instructions, and then
went out to draw his distant signal, for the train
was already whistling.
Five minutes later the S-M. called up the
Traffic Office and ticked of irtling story.
The leper had objected to bei.. 0 Je a prisoner,
and tried to break out while they were battening
up the windows. The constable, while trying to
push him back into the carriage, had been bitten
somewhere on the head, and also on the hand, and
was now in a state of unconsciousness. Before he
became unconscious, he had stated that he felt the
disease coming on. The leper had been struck on
the head with a batten as he tried to burst out of
the carriage, and pushed back with a mop. He
might be dead for all Kilmarnock knew to the
contrary; anyhow, he was somewhere in the bot-
tom of the carriage. Kilmarnock wanted to know
about the insensible constable. J ones thought he
had better come in by train and have the bitten
parts amputated. Kilmarnock wired back that
you couldn’t amputate a man’s head. J ones wired
back that Henry VIII. had done it frequently.
Kilmarnock wired again that the disease was
making rapid strides in the constable, and that the
leper had revived, and was using such fearful
language that it held back the train. Then J ones
cut Kilmarnock off with a bang.
Ten minutes later Jones was on Romahapa
platform anxiously awaiting the arrival of the
train with the afflicted character on board. It
came smartly up to the platform, and just as it
stopped there was a rush of cadets and porters to
get a look at the wild animal in the nailed-up
carriage. Among the crowd was an elegantly*
dressed gentleman, carrying over his arm a beauti-
fully-finished cloth overcoat.
The porters knocked away the battens, and
Jones opened the door and looked at the leper
steadily, trying to mesmerise him. The China-
man blinked back and kept on repeating:
“Better you look out, me leper, me bite
.you allee same p’leeceman. Him leper too now.
Biemby you allee same leper. See ? ”
“Be damned for a yarn,” said Jones, “Now,
you come quietly to the lock-up, and you’ll get
off lightly.”
“No fea; me no go lookup. ’Opitle much
better.”
“ Well, pay your fare.”
“No money!”
“Oh yes, you have. You made the better part
of a quid at Paisley, and got back your own.”
“Alii —my countlyn. .a pay to-mollow.”
“Here, come out of this!” and Jones seized
him by the clothes with both bands, and dragged
him out. The Chinaman tried desperately to bite
him. The crowd yelled to Jones to take care,
for it had just seen the policeman carried away
on a stretcher —nearly dead with fright. The
Chinaman struggled desperately, and Jones sud-
denly grabbed the beautiful cloth coat from the
spick and span gentleman’s arm, and tied it, bag
fashion, round the patient’s head. Then he picked
the Chinaman up and bumped him into a cab.
The cabby protested, begged, and swore. “Drive
to the police station,” yelled Jones, “before he
gets loose.” • The cab went off at a bound, closely
pursued by the gentleman who wanted value for
Iris coat.
At the station, the sergeant said he would see
Jones dead before he would take the object in
there. “ 'Tis the Board of Health office ye mane,
sure !” he said to the disgusted Jones.
“ Go on to the Board of Health office ! ” yelled
Jones, drowning the voice of the perspiring man
who was demanding the price of his coat. Off
they went again, and the perspiring man followed
in another cab.
At the Board of Health office there was only a
small boy on duty. All the officers were out,
examining crews or passengers, or something.
They had no place in which to keep lepers. The
boy thought for a moment, and then remarked,
brightly :
“ Bring him back at 2 o’clock. The chief will
be here then.”
Jones tried to speak, but his words died in his
throat, and the Chinaman struggled desperately,
and tried to bite through the cloth overcoat.
When he found the words would not come, Jones
contented himself with landing several vigorous
kicks on the counter and spoiling its beauty.
Then he kicked the Chinaman and struggled back
into the cab.
“Drive to tbe hospital!” lie yelled. On the
way there he talked to the Chinaman, and the
latter ceased his struggles. The man whose coat
John wore round his head still followed in the
other cab, and the vehicles entered the hospital
grounds together. Jones staggered with his
burden into the casualty room.
“Something wrong with this bloke. Came in
by this morning’s train saying there was some-
thing desperately dreadful about him.”
The man of the coat stood at the door, and
watched Jones uncover the scared Chinaman’s
face. Jones, with the coat in his hand, walked
towards the door.
“He seems very scared—what did he complain
of?” asked the young doctor.
“Leprosy.” Then to the' man at the door,
“Your coat, sir; many thanks,” and he gently
placed it over the stranger’s arm.
The stranger dropped the coat as though it was
red-hot iron and clamored at Jones. The doctor
gave a gasp, and also clamored at J ones.
“You mustn’t bring lepers here —take him to
the Board of Health office.”
“Look here,” said .Tones, “he’s no relation of
mine, and I’ve wasted about 7s; 6d. in cab fares
on him now. They can’t take him at the Health
Office till 2 o’clock, and I’m a busy man. Just
give him a few cigarettes and some marbles to
play with, and he’ll rest quietly till 2 o clock, and
walk down by himself.”
“ Well, it’s against the rules,” said the doctor,
“ but I’ll have a look at him. I’ve often longed
for a real live leper, to see what they are like.”
“Well, I believe he’s a fine specimen. He
says he’s something extra.”
“Lovely,” said the doctor, ecstatically.
“Where can I let you know the result.” And
he jotted the telephone number in his notebook.
* * » *
Two hours later the doctor rang Jones up.
“ Sorry to say your leper is a fraud. Hasn’t a
mark on him. All a dodge to get a cheap fare.
Just found out in time to let the man he bit
know as he came out of the effects of the
chloroform. S’long.’
“Well, well,” reflected Jones, “all my time
and heroism thrown away. Wonder where the
man with the coat is—he’d like to know.”

East and West.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE came with fair woi-ds on his lips,
And goodly greetings without end ;
We lit our ports and dressed our ships.
And bade him welcome as a friend.
Yet while we hailed him as our guest,
This truth through all our thinking ran—
There liesf a gulf ’twixt East and West
That many arches may not span.
The fault is neither ours nor his,
We follow each a guiding star;
Long ages made him what he is,
And ages made us what we are.
His gods are not the gods we serve, •
Our need is alien to his need ;
Each holds the course, and may not swerve,
Which ancient habit has decreed.
We may not make our life-blood flow
In one congenial mingling stream.
The Christ upon the Cross says “No !”
And Buddha murmurs “ Yain the Dream ! ”
His temples grey from floor to roof,
With lamps and carven shrines of gold,
Preach other creeds, and stand aloof
From our cathedrals vast and old.
The stars that fill our nation’s skies,
The songs that thrill our nation’s years—
He sees them with unseeing eyes,
And hears them with unheeding ears.
The Quest of brave Sir Galahad
To him were but an idle tale,
And Juliet, pale and sweet and sad,
Might sigh, and sigh, without avail.
All Shakspeare, Milton—all the throng,
Who fill the heart and fire the brain,
To him sing but an alien song,
And speak in accents vague and vain.
We sailed the seas while yet he slept,
And strove in realms of toil and thought,
Till slowly through his slumbers crept
Strange news to him of how we wrought.
Like one tired out with labors vast,
Who falls asleep beneath the moon,
And slumbers long, he woke at last
And rubbed his eyes, and found it noon.
The world was working hand and mind
With anvil-crash and furnace-glare ;
He heard its challenge on the wind,
And hastened forth to do and daie.
And, toiling on from rear to van,
With tireless hands and feet he pressed :
But all his toiling may not span
The gulf that lies ’twixt East and West.
We may not mix howe’er we meet;
Our ways lie charted far apart ;
We differ not in hands and feet,
But in the soul and in the heart.
IJ.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Peter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JN cattle camp and shearers’ hut, in
shanty and waterside boarding-house, the
name of Peter Jacltson stands level with
Carbine. The name holds good among white men
whether Governments are falling or the country
is in flames.
The other night I was sitting beside a crowd of
wharf lumpers near Circular Quay, and a bow-
legged Hercules was telling, in a new way, how
the immortal Peter was discovered by his mentor,
Larry Foley.
se ?’ was alwa ys on the look out for
noo blood. He was a better judge of a fighter
than Mace. Fancy Larry nursin’an’ coddlin’ a
big waster like Herbert Slade, an’ backin’ him
against a buster like Sullivan—not much. Jim
JVlace liked a man with a nice figure an’ plenty of
style. Larry always said that a gorilla would be
an ideal fighter if you could supply it with enough
sense.
“ Well, Larry was walkin’ along the Quay, one
day, an he sees a long black nigger sittin’ on a
plank swipm the hull of a wool ship with a paint
brush. Up an’ down went the nigger’s long arm,
reachm arf across the hull at every stroke. First
he’d paint with his right hand, an’ then with his
long left, an Larry stood watchin’ open-mouthed
for 10 minutes.
“ Th e nigger’s muscles was jerkin’ like shanghais
under his black skin. He always got the job to
paint the hull because he could reach from the
anchor to the donkey engine without ’avin’ his
plank shifted.
“ Larry strolls round an’hands up a bis: citrar
to the young nigger.
Punchin’ grease into the hide of a ship’s a
poor game, nie lad,’ sez Larry. ‘Come round to
the White Horse an’ see my gymnasium.’
“The nigger slings down his tools an’ goes
home with Foley. About a month after I spots
Peter cleanin’ the pub winders.
“ ‘ That black boy, ’ sez Larry to me, ‘is a
fightin machine. Just now he stands a bit ugly
and is heavy on his feet, but in six months he’ll
be able to punch a pigeon feather across the
ring.’
“ Peter an’ Larry used to chase each other about
the ring like a pair of kittens, Larry showin’ him
how to make a pancake with the other bloke’s
face when it started to breathe in his eye. He
showed Peter the ixact way to avoid the 8.49
express when it was travellin’ for his jaw an’ ribs.
“ Talk about footwork ! It would have taken a
black-tracker a lifetime to pick out Larry’s pads
- " hen Peter got a grip of the situation. In six
-months there wasn’t a kid or man in Sydney who
: could hit the atmosphere when Peter happened to
be in it.
“ ‘ The chap what beats him will have to use a
gun/ sez Foley one night. ‘He’s goin’ to dust
the rafters with a few reputations.’
Peter was a bit of a gentleman, too. Always
ready to’elp a woman or thump a bully. I see
him stop a Hindoo, one night, in George-street—
one of them gaudy, long-bodied sailors what had
run amok because the head serang had eaten all
the stoo. He tore up the street among the China-
men an’ kiddies wavin’ three feet of knife in the

bedgown was all over froth as if he’d been racin
through a cask o’ beer.

side the pub smokin’ a cigar, an’ when the Hindoo

hisself an spat out his cigar.

ALBERT DORBINGTON.
A “Native Industry.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TIMES were very bad on the Oastlereagh.
The weather was hot, and the lambs were
dying in hundreds. Dan and I were
camped about fifteen miles from Coonamble
The cookies refused us tucker any longer and
when we went to a station the boss had the cheek
to offer us work.
Dan told me that he knew how we could earn
some money easy. I looked surprised, and he
said all we had to do was to catch crows. The P
and A. Association was giving fid. each for their
heads m Coonamble. When I remarked that we
had no gun, Dan said it wasn’t necessary.
Dan was one of these theatrical blokes He
had been a tree or a piece of an elephant or
something once, in a pantomime, and knew all
about making up. So he started to make me into
a sheep. He said it was all a man of my mental
capacity was good for, and that I had met my
destiny at last. It took him three or four days
gathering up old skins, and fixing in eyes and
things. At last, I was ready, and Dan said I was
a much better specimen than most of the sheep on
Heehaw s run, where we carried on operations.
I he first morning, I went among the sheep with
a bag concealed m my wool, and began to graze
I grazed over slowly to a place where there were
four or five dead lambs, with dozens of crows pick-
ing at them. The crows didn’t mind me at all-
only one would now and again strike at my eye
but that didn’t hurt, as my eyes were glass
marbles that Dan had stolen from a schoolboy
As soon as I caught a crow I choked it, before it
had time to make a row, and put it into mv bag
Business flourished the first three days" and I
captured over 500 crows. When I wanted to get
them extra quick, I would lie down and pretend
to be sick, and cry “ baa.” Then they would rush
at me, and go for my eyes.
On the fourth morning I hadn’t got more than
50 crows when a cocky rode along and stopped to
look at me. He scared all the crows away but I
went on grazing. He looked round furtively a
few seconds, and then grabbed me, chucked me
across his horse, jumped into the saddle behind
me, and rode off When we were passing the
bush behind which Dan was concealed I bleated
loudly to attract his attention, but the cocky
punched me viciously, so I had to keep quiet.
Arrived at the house, the cocky jumped off,
lifted me down, and called to his wife to bring
hnn a knife. But just then Dan walked up
accused cocky of stealing Mr. Heehaw’s sheep!
and said he had been watching him the whole
time. Dan squared it for £5, and drove me away.
But we hadn t gone far when we met Heehaw
himself in a buggy. He stopped and said to Dan :
i<Tini ie s lee P that ; where did you get it?”
What the devil has that to do with vou ’ ”
asked Dan coldly. “It has everything"to do
with me. Its my sheep,” said Heehaw. Then
Ban used words that meant sanguinary and
illegitimate and untruthful. The squatter jumped
down and there was a rousing fight; but the fun
was stopped by the arrival of a mounted police-
man. Dan was given in charge for sheep-stealing.
He was hand cuffed ; he and I were thrown into the
back part of the buggy, and we all started for
Coonamble.
At the police station there is a little paddock
and they took me out of the buggy and tried to
drive me through the gate. I resisted, and when
the policeman caught me by the horns and the
squatter began to push me behind, my head came
oft in the policeman’s hands. I never saw two
more surprised men.
When Dan was released he talked big about
illegal arrest (he’s a bit of a bush-lawv er ) till
Heehaw stumped up a tenner to square it. Then
we went out of the crow-catching industry.

An Amazingly Wonderful Recovery.
THE "VICTIM’S EXPERIENCE.
“ Would X rather lose every possession in
the world than return to my former condi-
tion ? My word, I would ! ” Thus Mr. D.
McGregor, of Jindabyne, N.S.W., expressed
himself when questioned as to his illness in
the past compared to his present good
health.
“ You would hardly credit it to look at me
now, but it’s a fact. Three years ago I was
a human wreck—a derelict on the sea of
humanity. Rheumatics proved my undoing,
and so bad did I get that one of my hip joints
was drawn an inch and a half away from its
normal condition, and the front muscles of
my thigh got so bad that I could not step
forward, nor would my legs bear my weight,
which was but nine stone.
“I consulted many physicians, and was
ultimately told that all the doctors in
Australia could not cure me; but I was
recommended by one to try Electricity, as I
might hope for some relief from its use.
“In looking through the newspapers, I
came across an article on Dr. McLaughlin’s
Electric Belts. I wrote for their book, and
I tell you what, I hadn’t read far in it until
I was all a quiver with excitement and hope-
ful anticipations. It described similar cases
to my own, and gave careful details as to
their cures I sent a careful description of
my case to them, and they wrote me a com-
prehensive diagnosis explaining each symp-
tom, and how they proposed to cure me.
Their letter was so straightforward and can-
did that my confidence was gained at once,
and I will ever bless the time.
“ Anyhow, I got a Belt, and wore it
according to instructions. In three weeks I
began to improve. In three months I was
cured. I wore the Belt for four months, to
make certain of no relapse, although the
Specialists had placed six months as the
period of my treatment. Happily for me,
my cure took just half that time.
“ This all took place three years ago, and
I have had no return of the trouble since.
Wonderful! do you say ? A miracle, and
nothing else, and don’t you make any mistake
about it. Dr.. McLaughlin is a wonderful
scientist, and his Belt is a marvel of mechani-
cal ingenuity and professional skill.
“Just imagine yourself walking along,
suddenly losing the elastic power of your leg
muscles, and then falling in a heap, unable
to rise without assistance. That was my
condition before I sent; for the Dr.
McLaughlin’s Coy.’s book* and began their
treatment.
“I induced a man who suffered from in-
digestion to write to the Company for their
book. He got a Belt, and it cured him.
The book was my inspiration. Every sufferer
should read it.
“ Well, I hope you may never need an
Electric Belt, but if you do—Don’t hesitate.
Nor need you ever fear to recommend it. It
will not betray your trust. Good day, and
may Dr. McLaughlin’s Electric Belt go into
the home of every sufferer.”
